THE RED PAGE.
The Butchered Brontes.
Balzac’s remarkable story of the Succubus may
serve as one type of sex-sacrifice. Reverse the
pose: and the story of the Cretan Minotaur and
the maidens will serve as the other.
Most people know that sex has no place in the
lowest organisms through which, in their own or
kindred forms, we trace humanity’s ancestry.
Sex was life’s happy later-thought; born, as we
imagine, of the vital principle’s constant struggle
for intensity. Hence division in order to increase
the shock of union, on the principle that atoms
repelled to an intolerable point attract. The
greater the polarity the stronger the magnet.
Doubtless sex has advantaged life on its way to
the climax of intensity. Judged by any abstract
standard, it has doubtfully advantaged the average
individual form of life. So many pleasures :so
many pangs : and numerator cancels denomina-
tor into the sum as before. The life-result is
intrinsically the same— x always—only it is x
raised to humanity’s power.
But whether sex be ill or good for the average
human individual, for the exceptional individual
it is almost always ill. Se is pitilessly sacrificed
to the race, sis hopes of individual perpetuity
to the perpetuation of the common life. Mar-
riage is literally and physiologically a prior death
—the individual’s Calvary for the communi-
ty’s, the race’s salvation. The Succubus still
destroys the most brilliant of our youths : to the
Minotaur is still delivered every year a tribute of
the choicest of our maidens. Natural; and
therefore well and right from the race’s point of
view : to mend, as men have tried, is generally to
mar : yet how many bright aspirations, hopes of
progress, mental triumphs, glorious poems, are
trampled under the little pink feet of the army of
babies 1
These thoughts recur with Charlotte Bronte’s
life-story, once more partially told in Clement
Shorter’s “Charlotte Bronte and her Circle.”
The book itself adds little to our knowledge of
the family. It appears to have been undertaken
partly with the object of whitewashing Char-
lotte’s father and husband, and it succeeds in leav-
ing them mfioh as before. Another object was
seemingly to decry the well-established theory that
Charlotte was a woman like others—as in the
matter of M. Heger—and to give her the decent,
respectable character suited to the daughter of
one parson and the wife of another. The gist is
a numerous collection of unpublished letters, of
which Mrs. Gaskell skimmed the cream. The
remainder one is glad to see, though they have no
very great intrinsic interest. And Shorter adds
a biographical commentary on the journalistic
level, always with a brief for the defence of the
parsons aforesaid.
The book is issued by the religious publishing
house of Hodder and Stoughton, which is the
recognised print-vehicle of parsons from “lan
Maclaren ” downwards, and which issues a
monthly magazine, The Bookman, edited by Dr.
Nicoll, a Scotch ex-parson, to the main end of
booming the publications of the firm and its par-
son-allies and evangelical acquaintances. The
Bookman began well, with a show of independ-
ence, but latterly'the twang of devotion to piety
and the publishers—or the publishers and piety—
has sent it into the regions of literary discredit
where Gladstone roareth and the Nonconformist
conscience mourneth for its first-born.
It was written the other day that to get a genius
you want from one parent a good-quality blood
with plenty of red corpuscles, from the other a
food-quality brain and a touch of nervous disease.
'he Bronte family, perhaps the most characteris-
tic genius-family of which there is record, fills
these conditions admirably. The father was a
lusty Irishman, who wrote verses himself. The
taint of degeneracy was in his blood : he showed
insane tendencies, drank, was morbidly cruel to
his wife. The story of the sufferings of that poor
creature is one of the most affecting in literature.
She was not strong—rather delicate, indeed, and
of consumptive constitution; yet she seems to
have been normally healthy, and in the pleasant
Cornish sunshine of her birthplace might have
led a long and happy life. The Rev.
Patrick Bronte, as curate -in • charge of
Haworth, took her to a cheerless home on
the borders of a bleak Yorkshire moor,
with a graveyard on two sides of the house, and
not a soul near with whom she could have friendly
intercourse. There he murdered her by sexual
abuse; and, after producing six children in seven
years, the poor tortured wretch died horribly of
cancer supervening on physical exhaustion. In
the intervals of her child-bearing the Rev. Patrick
Bronte threw the babies’ colored shoes on the fire
in a fit of temper, or cut her silk dress into shreds,
or, when he was in a worse rage than usual, took
to firing off the pistol which night and day he
kept constantly by him. “Coldness, neglect,
tyranny, cruelty ” are Wemyss Reid’s words for
the conduct of the master; “habitual dread”
his phrase for the attitude of the slave.
And the clerical brute actually upbraided her
for having so many children ! If the poor
creature had turned and shot the Rev. Patrick
Bronte with his own pistol, she would have done
rather less than her duty to herself and her chil-
dren and humanity. But she was only a woman,
alas ! —patient and uncomplaining ; and, as she
fades lingeringly out of existence, in the agony of
her cancer, we hear her asking gently now and
again to be lifted up in bed that she might watch
the nurse cleaning the grate—“ because you clean
it as we used to clean it in my old home in Corn-
wall.” One feels keenly the want of faith in God
in circumstances like these. It would be such
a consolation to believe in a Deity who would
toast the Rev. Patrick Bronte.
Shorter’s whitewash doesn’t even begin to make
a white mark on him. It amounts to an assertion
that Mrs. Gaskell took an untrustworthy ser-
vant’s gossip; that another servant loved the Rev.
Pat; and that Charlotte’s husband, the Rev.
Arthur Nicholls, speaks highly of him. In a
word, Shorter answers precise, detailed charges
with side-issue generalities—and the charges re-
main. The Rev. Arthur also, Shorter says, has
been much maligned. He has been called cold
and unsympathetic, whereas Charlotte herself is
in superlatives about his love and kindness.
II«re again the answer is inconclusive, for loving
feminine self-deception is so common that we see
daily any wife of any husband blind to
qualities the very reverse of loveable. There
is no doubt that Charlotte, like her
mother, was a martyr to her sex. But her mar-
riage was more merciful : it did not torture her—
it killed her plump. She died nine months after-
wards, of an illness connected with childbirth.
Weak by constitution, in fact, and with such a
history of headwork, she was unable to bear the
strain of maternity. This is the penalty which
women pay for extraordinary intellectual power.
In proportion as is diverted to nourish the brain
the blood which maternity demands elsewhere,
the result is sterility, as in the case of George
Eliot, or death, as in the case of Charlotte Bronte.
When Charlotte married at 38 she was in fair
health, and had before her perhaps half her
career in literature. She threw away life and
fame because she could not resist the importuni-
ties of a man whom she states expressly that she
did not love ; and so one of the most gifted
geniuses of the century was sacrificed in endeavor-
ing to perform an act of reproduction achieved a
dozen times over by the commonest female clod
among her husband’s parishioners !
The Rev. Arthur Nicholls was a substantial
Scotchman selfish, of cold manners, rather
stubborn. His character commanded respect, but
his disposition did not invite affection. He was
not at all the man of Charlotte’s dreams, as she
candidly avows. Why, then, did she marry him ?
For a complexity of reasons. In the first place,
she was 38, and lonely, and tired, and heartsick
of the sombre, drinking, pistol-shooting father.
In the second place, the Rev. Arthur looked
like a • terribly sincere lover: he wept, grew
visibly thinner, and would take no refusal.
Charlotte couldn’t help being impressed: surely
with such a man, she thought, if with any, will
I find happiness. Perhaps the father’s bitter
opposition to the match helped to bias Charlotte
in favor of it. And finally, she looked forward
into the loveless, lonely future, and—in great
ignorance of what marriage meant, yet with many
intellectual misgivings—decided to take the leap.
There is no reason to think that the Rev.
Arthur treated his wife otherwise than kindly.
Like many others of his type, he was a very decent
fellow as long as he got his own way, and Char-
lotte gave him his own way. Her head could
never have approved of him ; but her heart began
to regard him with affection which deepened into
something like love. The sex-awakening of a
woman of 38 is apt to produce a passion which,
if it be fairly treated, gilds the marital hori-
zon for some time. As the years pass, the
Sleeping Beauties grow more and more inclined
to overlook small personal objections to the
Princes who burst through the hedge of
briars. Charlotte’s married life had not time to
become unhappy. When illness took her, her
husband made a most kind and devoted nurse—
which was quite in character. And when she
died he returned to keep company with her old
father until the old father died in his turn. Those
six years of voluntary martyrdom ought to wipe
out the memory of the deficiencies of the Rev.
Arthur Nicholls —for which, in any case, he can-
not positively be blamed.
These things, among others, are proofs that
literature is made on physiological principles ; and
that a family doctor —who can point to one page,
and say—“ Scrofula !” to another —“ Eupepsia ! ”
and to a third—“ Phthisis ! ” —should be easily
the best of literary critics.
Items.
Fiction-criticism in Lond. Times is of the
most incompetent published. Frankfort Moore—
who has not seen Australia —never tires of re-
iterating how the knowledge of Australian life
shown in his “From the Bush to the Breakers”
was praised by the Times as much more accurate
than that of “Rolf Boldrewood.”
Melb. Argus is “ Rolf Boldrewood’s ”
faithful flatterer, and when even Melb. Argus
condemns it there seems no need to wade through
R.B’s “The Sealskin Cloak’’—the last produc-
tion of one of the industrious local publishers’-
machines. Besides, the S.Q. looks dull, and
stodgy, and guide-booky, and all Melb. Argus
says it is. Words have a blessed physiognomy of
their own which tells the expert observer at once
whether there is anything behind it. “ Observer ”
—not “reader”—for the printed page yields its
secret to the eye before the mind has grasped an
image or a thought. Sometimes a stupid-looking
person is really intelligent, but a page is always
as stupid as it looks. Only, as every page has a
different face, you mustn’t judge the book by a
glimpse of one.
“Teufelswelt,” an anonymous epic pub-
lished by Sebastian Bach, Melb., is as tedious as
ten cab-horses yawning in unison. People should
preach in prose : it saves time and temper and
much wear and tear of the Muse.
Coventry Patmore, who died the other
day at a long age, was a poet of some tender
thoughts and gracious phrases. But he lacked
scope and strength—ranging for a distance on the
heights of this characteristic couplet—
Mildred's of Earth, yet happier far
Than most men’s thoughts of Heaven are—
and climbing no higher.
Dec. number of The Austral Light, a
Melb. Catholic magazine, is specially sent for re-
view. This magazine always jumps painfully in the
hobbles of its creed, and this number is not so
good as some which have preceded it. George
Dunderdale’s article on “The Land and the Way
we Lost It ”is pithy, informed, and readable. P.
Moore writes interestingly on Art, and somer-
saults to an uninteresting religious peroration.
The remaining contents are dull or worthless.
Saturday Review : “ With all hia bril-
liance and accomplishment, Stevenson never pos-
sessed a style, certainly not a poetical style, which
could rank with that of the finest masters. His
incurable habit was to use language dispropor-
tionate to his matter, words raised to just one
power too high, to borrow a phrase from mathe-
matics. It was so adroitly done that no one could
call his style inflated ; but there was always mis-
sing the sense of reserve, of power waived.” Or,
as The Bulletin has more pithijy put it, the Man
was never quite big enough for the Phrase.
The Ballad of the Barber.
Here is the tale of Carrousel,
The barber of Meridian-street.
He cut, and coiffed, and shaved so well,
That all the world was at his feet.
The King, the Queen, and all the Court,
To no one else would trust their hair,
And reigning belles of every sort
Owed their successes to his care.
With carriage and with cabriolet
Daily Meridian-street was blocked,
Like bees about a bright bouquet
The beaux about his doorway flocked.
Such was his art he could with ease
Curl wit into the dullest face ;
Or to a goddess of old Greece
Add a new wonder and a grace.
All powders, paints, and subtle dyes,
And costliest scents that men distil,
And rare pomades, forgot their price
And marvelled at his splendid skill.
The curling irons in his hand
Almost grew quick enough to speak,
The razor was a magic wand
That understood the softest cheek.
Yet with no pride his heart was moved ;
He was so modest in his ways !
His daily task was all he loved,
And now and then a little praise.
An equal care he would bestow
On problems simple or complex;
And nobody had seen him show
A preference for either sex.
How came it then one summer day,
Coiffing the daughter of the King,
He lengthened out the least delay
And loitered in his hairdressing ?
The Princess was a pretty child,
Thirteen years old, or thereabout.
Sh ■ was as joyous and as wild
As spring flowers when the sun is out.
Her gold hair fell down to her feet
And hung about her pretty eyes;
She was as lyrical and sweet
As one of Schubert’s melodies.
Three times the barber curled a lock,
And thrice he straightened it again ;
And twice the irons scorched her frock,
And twioe he stumbled in her train.
His fingers lost their cunning quite,
His ivory combs obeyed no more;
Something or other dimmed his sight,
And moved mysteriously the floor.
He leant upon the toilet table,
His fingers fumbled in his breast;
He felt as foolish as a fable,
And feeble as a pointless jest.
He snatched a bottle of Cologne,
And broke the neck between his hands 5
He felt as if he was alone,
And mighty as a king’s commands.
The Princess gave a little scream,
Carrousel’s cut was sharp and deep ;
He left her softly as a dream
That leaves a sleeper to his sleep.
He left the room on pointed feet;
Smiling that things had gone so well.
They hanged him in Meridian Street.
You pray in vain for Carrousel.
AUBREY BEARDSLEY
Ernest Favenc’s boys’ story, ‘‘Marooned
on Australia ” (Blaekie), makes excellent timber
for local Xmas-boxes. There is a happy surfeit of
adventures among the 17th-century “ Indians ” of
Westralia, with a fine array of fresh corpses when
they front the Mongol invader. And every
decent boy smelleth the battle afar off, and saith
among the trumpets Ha, ha ! The author dedi-
cates “ to the memory of Edmond Marin de la
Meslee, by whose accidental death Australia lost
both an ardent geographer and an enthusiastic
student of the history of the Austral Continent.”

the XMAS CAROL?
What was OLD SCROOGE like before he saw
MARLEY’S GHOST ? A miserable wretch,
begrudging a starving cur a lick out of the gutter,
the children their Xmas feast, and all mankind its
Xmas enjoyment.
What would you be like if you didn’t see
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
XMAS SHOW.
Not so bad as OLD SCROOGE, surely, for he
was a bad case indeed. But you would not, nay,
could not, be happy. Your heart would yearn
for something, and you wouldn’t know what. You
would be low-spirited, snappish, and bilious. There
would be no brightness in your eye, no elasticity
in your step, your weary feet would, be heavy as
lead, your flesh would fall away, and instead of
the sleek, WELL-GROOMED CITIZEN, a lean,
woebegone, CADAVEROUS PANTALOON
would OCCUPY YOUR PANTS and wear your
skin. Friend, don’t risk it, make a point of seeing

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
CIRCEAN TYPES.
1. —The Strange Woman.
Two types of Temptress you may chance to meet,
As forth you fare, on pleasure bent, my son.
An open trafficker in shame is one,
Who hawks her dubious wares from street to street.
Oft warm and true, a service not unmeet
At times she renders, though depraved, undone,
And lost, men deem her. Scorn her not ; yet
shun,
0 shun her and her kisses rotten-sweet!
Poor waif ! Not blameless, yet not all to blame.
Think : for a paltry coin she gives herself—
Her body and soul; the simulated breath
Of love ; her woman’s crown, her honest fame.
Pity the slave of Passion or of Pelf,
But seek her not. Her ways go down to death.
2.—And the Familiar.
No soiled dove, self-sacrificed is she,
The second Temptress with the Judas-kiss ;
But of the baleful serpent tribe, whose hiss
Is heard not till the victim, no more free,
Is held by strong, hypnotic witchery.
A flirt —a harlot of the mind —is this
Who deals in base, dishonored bills of bliss,
Yet robs her dupes of more than harlot’s fee.
0 doubly, trebly shun her ! Lewd and vile
At heart is she, though free from outward fault;
A Circe, without Circe’s saving salt
Of healthy lust. The lover whom her smile
Enslaves, einbrutes and hardens, never more
Tastes peace, or joy, or love’s delicious store.
M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
LIFE.
Life is the wild and restless sea,
And Death the moveless shore
That hemmeth Life eternally,
And past its border none may see
Until for evermore.
Life is the caged bird’s joyless plight
By bars encircling bound ;
Death opes tbecage : with sweet delight
The captive soars in upward flight
And spurns the leaden ground.
Life is the sombre night and cold
Illumined by the moon ;
Death is the rising sun. Behold !
The shades are back for ever rolled,
Grief ever bringeth boon.
R.S.L.M.
STILL WATERS.
My dainty little Puritan,
How would you take it if a man
Dared to offend you ?
Your face—l may not call it sly—
But there’s a hint—l’m hanged if I
Can comprehend you !
O should I trembling trespass make
And kiss you for your own sweet sake,
I wonder whether
You would reprove that stolen kiss
Or tempt me into more than this,
Or—both together?
Yic.
WYVIS.
TO KITTY.
Sweet Kitty, with the sable hair,
And eyes as azure as the heavens—
Why do I carry everywhere
A mind at sixes and at sevens ?
Saying, “ Inimitable Kitty,
Can she be loving as she’s pretty ? ”
Often the wind-glades on the sea
Waver in momentary directions,
Unsettled as you seem to be
In your adorable affections ;
And as they blow and faint alternate,
Their meaning—no one can discern it.
Let the great tempest rise—and lo !
The ocean trembles to its oozes,
Stirred, all its elements overflow
In torrent that repression loses ;
So might it be, delicious Kitty,
Were you more loving and less witty !
Love shall breathe on the sleeping bud
That you have never found within you,
The leaves shall drown you in a flood
Of limpid witchery, and win you
Into a sweet surrender, Kitty,
That’s neither gratitude nor pity.
M.L.
H.C.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with A us-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage. England £l, with
6s. 6d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of l§d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
SATURDAY, DECEMBER 19, 1896.
The Democratic Victory.
The Maoriland general election has passed
over, and the Seddon Ministry remains in
power and is prepared to meet its third con-
secutive Parliament with what appears to be
still a solid working majority. It is a
seriously diminished one, it is true; but the
marvel, according to all the precedents of
party Government, is that there should be
any majority at all. The regular course of
proceedings is that each new Parliament
brings with it a new Cabinet. The Govern-
ment which gets such a solid majority to
begin with as Ballance and Seddon started
with in 1891 is rare ; the one which keeps its
advantage through the whole term of Parlia-
ment is still more rare ; and the one which
goes to the country and returns with a still
larger majority to last through a second Par-
liament is so unusual as to be a political
phenomenon. As to the Government which,
after serving through two complete Parlia-
ments, comes back successful to start a third
term of office, such a thing is almost beyond
precedent. In the regular course of events
the Ministry is decisively thrown out at the
first opportunity, and replaced by the other
party, which is also thrown out at the first
opportunity, the customary attitude of the
public being one of disgust at both parties,
tempered with a feeling that possibly the one
it hasn’t lately seen so much of may be a
shade less disgusting than the other one.
Therefore, the situation in Maoriland either
means that the Seddon Ministry is, in public
opinion, about the best Ministry on earth,
or else that the Opposition is such an un-
speakably loathsome Opposition that its
iniquity puts it almost outside the pale of
humanity.
* * * *
The signs are, however, that this is the last
Parliament which will be bossed by Seddon,
Mackenzie and Co.-—at least, for the present.
The solid Vxovernment majority has gone
down from about 25 to something like a
dozen, and, though the independent members
may raise the figures to 15 or 16, still the
Ministerial phalanx is evidently not quite
what it was. Unless something great and
unexpected arises to revive the jaded enthu-
siasm of the community, the 12 ought, in the
ordinary course of things, to disappear alto-
gether at next election, and the Tory party
should come back to power with a staggery
majority of one or two, and lead a precarious
existence for a while among the flesh-pots
and the portfolios. The Seddon Ministry,
to all appearance, is beginning to die slowly
of sheer weariness—the disease that every
Ministry dies of when it survives all other
casualties. A Ministry of archangels would
peg out from exactly the same cause under
the same conditions. If a Government is so
much above the average in honesty, brains,
enterprise, and democratic instincts that it
gives no tangible cause why the public should
want to get rid of it, it is still bound to die
at last because the community grows tired of
its mere continuance. The names of its
members, their portraits in the papers, their
voices on the platform, all become a cause of
offence ; the large “ S ” in front of the name
of Seddon and the big “ M ” before the cog-
nomen of Mackenzie grow to be a public
wrong, because people have seen these initials
so often ; and their owners have to go because
the human soul abhors hash and manna, and
everything else which is too often repeated.
Therefore The Bulletin opines that the
Seddon Cabinet is at last on the down-grade.
* * * *
Still, despite these probabilities, the pros-
pects of the Tory party in Maoriland are
miserably poor. It has come back numeri-
cally stronger than it was ; but, then, the
Government-party consists of those members
who are agreed to stand by the Ministry, and
the Opposition, while it includes all those who
are willing to oppose the Ministry, isn’t
necessarily agreed to stand by anything.
Also, it is still a long way from anything
even dimly resembling a majority. The head
and front of the Opposition is the old Tory
party which denounces all that Ballance
and Seddon have done, yet which got back
alive from the elections partly, at least, by
promising not to undo the alleged evils these
leaders accomplished. A Tory party which
exists partly on the votes it gets for being
wholly anti-democratic in theory, and partly
on those it gets by promising to be somewhat
democratic in practice, is good enough while
it is in Opposition and has nothing to do, but
when it comes into power and has to
adopt some distinct line of action, it
must alienate one or the other half
of its ragged regiment of supporters.
Another scrag-end of the Conservative
party consists apparently of the more aggra-
vated and incurable Prohibitionists. Their
vote was for sale to whichever party would
promise to grant the most desperate variety
of Prohibition, and as the party which is in
power and has to redeem its pledges imme-
diately can never promise so much, as the
party which is out of power and therefore
can’t be put to the test, the Tory faction
naturally bid the highest. The Ministry
had to pay cash ; the Opposition had merely
to give its promissory-note payable in three
or six or more years, and therein lay all the
difference. But when the promissory note
falls due the Cold Tea following must
get Prohibition or its vote will be lost, and
Prohibition means great loss of revenue, and
loss of revenue either means more taxation
on the poor which will alienate the popular
vote, or more taxation on the rich which will
make the Tories themselves desert the Tory
party, or immense borrowing which will lead
to more trouble later on. Therefore the
Cold Tea vote is another item which may
help later on to put the Seddon Ministry out
of power, and yet may make its successors
presently wish they had never been born.
Apart from these leading elements the mot-
ley party includes the casual individuals
whom Minister Seddon has quarrelled with
or Minister Mackenzie has aimed pickle-
bottles at in the course of their lengthy
official career, and all these together make a
brave show on the Opposition benches, but
as material to make a new Government out
of they are somewhat wanting in cohesion.
* * * *
Other matters also tend to strengthen the
Opposition in the present and do very little
good for it in the future. Maoriland is not
a solid community like N.S. Wales or Vic-
toria. It began as a number of separate
provinces, each with its Superintendent, and
its little farthing Legislature, and its capital,
and its finance, and its debt, and its Works
Policy, and all the rest. The provincial
feeling survived the abolition of the pro-
vincial system ; it survives to this day, in
fact, and is not likely to wholly wear out for
another quarter-century. All the larger pro-
vinces rage considerably against each other
over matters of finance, and the impossi-
bility of pleasing all alike accumulates a
steadily-increasing grievance on the head of
every Government. What is demanded is,
in effect, that each province shall get its
exact share, or rather more, of expenditure
according to the amount it says it contributes
in direct taxation and land, postal, customs,
excise, and railway revenue ; also that the
loan moneys shall be distributed with the
same ghastly accuracy. Therefore, the old,
rich provinces complain of undue expendi-
ture to open up the poor and new ones, and
the new ones want to know why the pro-
vinces which got all the money in the past
should also get it all in the future ; and
balance-sheets are produced showing that
every separate province is the most ill-used
one in the whole group. Finally, the repre-
sentatives of the more desperately aggra-
vated provinces begin to go into Opposition,
it being always understood in Maoriland that
the Opposition, when it comes into power,
will do absolute justice all round and fully
satisfy everybody. The longer a Ministry
is in office the larger grows the provincial
difficulty, and the louder the demand that the
Otago Central railway, and the Canterbury
Midland railway, and the North Island Trunk
line shall each be finished first, and that
every province will get all it wants before
any of the others get anything. And the
Seddon Ministry, having been in office longer
than usual, is beginning to get the provincial
difficulty on its hands in a very bad shape.
* * * •
Also, there are other political troubles
peculiar to Maoriland which every Govern-
ment collides with sooner or later. It has
the only war-debt in Australasia, and, as a
war-debt is represented by no revenue-pro-
ducing assets, thusadds seriously to the burden
of taxation. It was never blessed with the
splendid land-revenues enjoyed by some of
the other provinces, for a considerable por-
tion of its lands remained in the hands of the
native race, whereas the other provinces
annexed their soil bodily and proceeded to
turn it all into money. This reduced the
sources of revenue and further increased
taxation. The province has the same desire
for railways and other public works as its
neighbors. It doesn’t want further borrow-
ing, because it still remembers how Grey
brought it within half a hair’s-breadth of
default and insolvency. It doesn’t want
more taxation, being already, for the reasons
set forth above, the most heavily-taxed pro-
vince of the group. As a consequence, there
is probably, in Maoriland, a more intense
demand that the Government shall make
bricks without straw—good bricks and plenty
of them—than there is in any other of the
provinces. And the Maoriland Opposition
promises to make the bricks—it is in favor,
it says, of less taxation and the abolition of
borrowing, and a vigorous Public Works
policy, and a steady reduction of the public
debt—therefore, the dull, illogical section of
the community will probably drift in time to
the Tory standard. This is good enough in
the present, but as the Opposition proposes
to reduce taxation on its own special class—
the rich—and hardly dare levy more taxation
on the poor, it will almost certainly have a
huge deficit to struggle with whenever its
turn of office comes round, and its beautiful
programme is hopeless for the-simple reason
that a pint bottle won’t hold two quarts
under any circumstances.
* * * *
It is still possible, of course, that the
Ministry may survive this Parliament, and
that, the election of 1899 may give it another
majority like that of 1893. There are always
chances for a Ministry of such energy and
ability as that of Richard Seddon ; and its
present position is remarkably good. The
Bank of N.Z. is gradually righting itself
under State management. The State Land
Bank is doing well and seems likely to do
better. The Government’s policy of acquir-
ing and cutting up big estates is achieving
wonders. The B.N.Z. Estates Co. difficulty
is approaching a settlement; the Colonial
Bank scandal has died of inanition ; the
worst of the Midland Railway trouble seems
over; and ex-Treasurer Ward has gone
under, in a ministerial sense, with less
disaster than was expected. If all the State
enterprises keep on prospering, as they seem
practically certain to do, and the Old Age Pen-
sion and the State Fire Insurance schemes
can be carried through and prove as success-
ful as the rest of the Government’s under-
takings, it will be difficult to avoid recognising
the fact that the Seddon Ministry, despite
its faults, which are many, has more enter-
prise and genius and democratic instinct and
mana and good luck than any other Ministry
that ever existed in Australasia. Again, the
chapter of accidents has gone well with the
Government hitherto, and may continue to
do so. Its great stumbling-block, the Cold
Tea party, may prove, after all, to be only
a temporary accident. The Opposition
can hardly go on for ever without announc-
ing some sort of a policy ; it can’t live con-
stantly on the hazy statement that it
advocates the “restoration of confidence”;
and it can hardly present any policy which
won’t disgust some part of its motley follow-
ing. In fact, all the chances are in favor of
the Democratic Government remaining on
top for another six or twelve years—save
one, its own old age. That is a disability for
which there is absolutely no remedy.
The Efficacy of Prayer.
At the C. of E. Diocesan Sj'nod, held at Auckland on
Oct. 25, Archdeacon Willis moved : “ That the Bishop
be respectfully invited to ask the clergy of the diocese to
order a form of prayer to be used in all churches for
God’s good guidance to the people of the colony on the
occasion of the approaching general election for the
House of Representatives.” The President heartily
approved the motion, which was agreed to.
It is well that such things should be noted and
pondered, for they press home a fact of which we
are too apt to lose sight: that the forces of ignor-
ance and superstition are still immense, and that
even now, with all our civilisation, given but a
little concentration of those forces under the in-
fluence of enthusiasm or panic, but a little weaken-
ing of the defences of intellect, and the scanty
domain of science and enlightenment, so hardly
won, might once again be swallowed by the
wilderness of barbarism, as civilisations as grand
and but little less extended have been swallowed
up already. It is little more than 100 years since
the last heretic was burnt alive in Spain ; it is not
yet 200 years since Mrs. Hicks and her nine-year-
old daughter were officially hanged in England
for witchcraft; and assaults upon supposed witches
and wizards, occasionally rising to murder, as in
the recent Irish case, are not uncommon even
now. And though our children are taught to
ridicule the Chinese fashion of preventing the ill-
effects of eclipses by banging gongs to frighten
away the devil who is eating the sun, when
Halley’s comet appeared in 1456 it was regarded
as the dispenser of war, pestilence and famine.
By order of the Pope the church-bells of Europe
were rung to scare the monster away, and an
additional daily prayer was added to the supplica-
tions of the faithful. Whereupon (says Draper) the
comet slunk away into the abysses of space, terror-
stricken by the maledictions of Callixtus II L>
and did not venture back for 75 years. The faith-
ful were duly congratulated and comforted by the
assurance that, as in previous instances relating to
eclipses, droughts and rains, so also as regards
this “ nefarious” comet, victory had been vouch-
safed to the only True Church.
* * * *
The only true church and all the other true
churches have nowadays tacitly resigned jurisdic-
tion over comets and eclipses, though they
still claim considerable authority in the matter
of droughts and rains. The eternal and omni-
potent God is permitted even to send the earth
revolving round the sun, which He was prac-
tically forbidden to do by the bull of Pope
Paul Y. denouncing the Copernican doctrine.
As regards the Universe, there is not much doubt
that the Almighty is really almighty. But
when it comes to a question of sending a shower
of rain to Wagga Wagga, Omniscience is still re-
peatedly shown that it does not know its own
business, and Omnipotence works subject to the
advice and instructions of the Rev. Jeremiah
Blazes and his colleagues in the ministry.
» * * *
When you come to consider it, “ the efficacy of
prayer” is indeed a remarkable thing. Even
Christian children are nowadays taught that the
earth is one of the most insignificant of planets
chipped off a comparatively insignificant sun. But
they are rarely taught to try and realise the dimen-
sions of the solar system, or to understand that
other systems so far transcend ours as to reduce
it to microscopic absurdity in the great scheme of
things. Our sun is about 92 millions of miles
away from the earth—so far that, seen from
the sun, the earth is a mere speck, a dust-
mote so tiny that the dot upon this i, held
a couple of feet away from the reader, appears
several hundred times larger. Yet the very
nearest of the suns which we call stars, orbs
which crowd the heavens, orbs to which our sun
is hardly more than a gas-light, is 230,000 times
farther from us than the sun —more than 20,000
million miles away. And far beyond that stretches
an infinity of suns and stars until eyes and tele-
scopes can pierce no farther. Yet when an infini-
tesimal animalcule, on this infinitesimal earth,
wants a shower of rain, he has only to flop down
on his knees, and the Lord of all the Shining
Universes will dart like a meteor through infinite
space to do the bidding of the Rev. Jeremiah
Blazes!
* * ♦ *
More, He will turn back this universe to do
it, and snap the chain of cause and effect which
stretches from now to infinity. Eor the universe
is governed by universal law. In all human ex-
perience that law has never once been broken.
The moisture which the sun evaporates from the
sea is determined to the last drop by the heat of
the sun and the extent of the sea and the sur-
rounding conditions, and these to the last iota
depend upon precedent conditions, and these
again upon precedent conditions, and so back-
wards through eternity. The height at which
clouds are formed, the direction of the winds
which float them over the surface of the land,
the spot at which the clouds will condense into
rain, these are all a part of the inevitable result of
causes themselves the result of preceding causes.
While the law of cause and effect holds—and no
other physical theory is rationally conceivable —it
is as impossible for God Himself to create, to make
out of nothing, a single drop of rain as it would be
for the meanest of the emmets who pray to Him.
* * * *
Similarly with the election of representatives to
M.L. Parliament. A voter is what he is, partly
because his father and mother were what they
were, and because their fathers and mothers were
what they were, and so on backwards to infinity.
And he is partly what he is because he has been
educated in a certain way, and fed on certain
foods, and has been subjected to this and that in-
fluence as he has gone through life. In a word,
he is the product of heredity and environment, and
his heredity and environment having been so-and-
so, his conduct in any circumstances must be such-
and-such. There is no room for the interposition
of Deity, unless Deity breaks the first of its own
laws—the law that effect invariably follows cause,
and corresponds in nature and extent with the
nature and extent of the sum-total of causes.
Constituted as a man is, and moved by the motives
which do actuate him, he cannot help voting for
the candidate for whom he does vote. It is logical
and intelligent to bring fresh influences to bear
upon him, to give him fresh motives, with the
object of turning his vote in any particular direc-
tion, but appeal to God to effect a sudden and total
change in the character of a “ bad ” man, so that
he will vote for a “good” candidate, would be
supremely ridiculous-even if it were proved that
there is a God after the pattern of the churches,
and that He had taken a specific interest in human
affairs.
* * * *
The fact is that the reign of law is acknow-
ledged even by the superstitious in proportion to
their knowledge. It is only when causes are
obscure, or not comprehended, that Divine inter-
position is sought with regard to effects. It is
comprehended even by pious people nowadays
that a comet is merely a star moving in a regular
and calculable course, and probably no civilised
person, religious or not, dreams of praying to God
to alter its course as religious people prayed in
1456. But little is generally known of physical
science, and pious shepherds, as ignorant as their
flocks, have no scruple in praying for an alteration
in the quantity of moisture evaporated by the sun
from the sea, or an alteration in the rotation of
the earth upon its axis which would cause a wind
to bring clouds over Wagga Wagga, or Warwick,
or Mount Gambier. And it is well-understood by
shepherds, at least, that discredit must not be cast
upon religion by praying for things which will
furnish a definite test of whether prayer is effica-
cious or not. If you pray for rain all over Aus-
tralia, a shower is bound to fall somewhere ; but if
Archdeacon Willis, who moved the resolution at
the Dunedin synod, fell down and broke his leg,
he would certainly not pray to God to come and
set it. That would furnish a definite test of the
value of prayer; but it is recognised that there is
no use in asking God to set broken legs. The
Archdeacon would send for a doctor right away.
Yet it would probably be a good deal easier for
God to miraculously set a broken leg than to
miraculously alter the constitutions, and disposi-
tions, and brains of a large number of M.L. elec-
tors.
* * * *
In 1881, when President Garfield lay dying,
every Christian in the United States was ordered
to pray for his recovery. Such a unanimous blast
of prayer went up as very seldom went up on


this earth before. And Garfield died. The
other day all the Christian Endeavorers in the
U.S., more than 100,000 strong, prayed for a week
together for the conversion of infidel Ingersoll.
Precisely at the same hour every day they all
went down on their marrowbones and wrestled ler-
vently with Satan on behalf of Ingersoll.
Ingersoll watched the farce humorously, re-
gretted the waste of time, and announced at the
close that he was rather more of an infidel, if any-
thing. In all cases where the results of prayer
can be appreciated, they are uniformly nil—as is
to be expected. As there are at least two chances
in any given contingency, it is obvious that prayer
must sometimes seem to win. There are two or
three faith-hospitals in England, and admission
was once sought to one of them for a patient with
the broken leg which Archdeacon Willis would
take to his doctor. “No;’’was the. reply, “we
only take chronic diseases.” That is, the faith-
cure is only applicable to cases in which the
patient may get better anyhow, without opera-
tion.
* * « *
Savages and children (who are savages), and
the very ignorant religious (who are also savages)
put the faith of the calculating praying-man to
shame. Savages pray for definite objects, and
whack their God over the head when He does not
grant them. Little Johnny prays for a kite, or a
ball, and is grievously troubled when the Omnipo-
tent makes no response. And very ignorant
religious people, who really believe in their Bible,
and do not pick and choose its doctrines to suit
their convenience, don’t take their sick to the
doctors, but call for the elders of the church to
pray over them, as enjoined in James v., 14. The
patients frequently die (there was a case in S.A.
recently), and then the professing Christians of
the land denounce the real Christians, and fine, or
imprison them, for holding a faith in prayer which
they have given up themselves because they know
it’s no good. And then these same professing
Christians go and pray for rain, or for the conver-
sion of stupid or wicked electors, and prove that
their own ignorance is just as dense, only a little
less Scriptural. Prayer as an aspiration, as an
ecstasy, as an unburdening of the soul to the Un-
known, is not irrational, and is apparently bene-
ficial to certain qualities of the complex thing that
we call human nature. But prayer as a petition,
as a begging, prayer for rain as a lazy man’s sub-
stitute for irrigation, prayer for a new heart for
M.L. electors as a substitute for education and
social and sanatory influences, is utterly useless
and ridiculous.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Naval Squadron Swindle.
In 1887 a wave of lunacy passed over Austra-
lasian legislatures. Several leading politicians
had been bought and sold at the Imperial Con-
ference, which decided it was time Australians
began to share the burdens of the British Em-
pire; and when they came home they did their
best to secure acquiescence in the shameful bar-
gain of the Naval Defence Bill. Only in one
province, Queensland, was there substantial
opposition. Backed up by the dutiful Courier,
MTlwraitii used the Bill as an election cry,
and denounced an agreement which “ will
not only bind us down for a term of
years to pay tribute to Great Britain,
but will also implicate us in the risks
of war which she runs with other
nations.” Then “Betsy and I made up,” the
Griffilwraith arose in all its ignominy, and
Queensland passed the Bill four years late.
This measure, to which Australasia’s Imperial-
istic legislators were bribed and cajoled to
assent, is probably the most foolish compact
ever made in the history of these provinces.
All our war risks come through Britain ;
Britain’s sole concern is to protect the hundreds
of millions of British capital here invested ;
yet we must actually invite attack from
Britain’s European enemies by paying annual
tribute to support a British squadron ! The
seven leaky-boilererl vessels, of which four
attend the Melbourne Cup and three rust
continually in harbor, cost £700,000 to con-
struct, and in these days of 2| per cent.
Consols this represents to J. Bull an outlay of
£19,250 per annum. He charges Australasia 5
per cent, on the capital cost, or £35,000 per
annum, so that £15,750 goes towards a sinking
fund to help pay of! the original cost. This
charge is quite independent of up-keep, for
which Australasia pays £91,000 per annum
additional. We pay for the maintenance of the
vessels —repairs to hull, machinery, masts,
yards and rigging; we pay for ammunition
and torpedoes ; we pay for victuals,
stores, and medicines—-the sailors’ pants,
and the gold lace on the officers’ caps.
And in return what have we ? The
right to say not one little word in control of
either men or ships. The Admiral of “our
fleet ” gets his instructions, not from us, but
from the British Admiralty ! This is not
taxation without representation ; for it is
worse—it is taxation for somebody else’s re-
presentation. In six years, we have al-
ready paid away £756,000 more than the
whole squadron cost—and nothing to show
for it ! There is a promise that the fleet is to
remain in Australian waters—a very safe pro-
mise, seeing that they who made it can break it
when they please ! How much exactly would
that promise be worth in time of war ? One
cheerful Admiral has told us that Australia would
probably be best defended in the China Seas. An-
other suggested that “our squadron” might be
w'anted to reinforce the British Channel fleet !
And now it is reported by cable that a pro-
minent British statesman has said that, in case
of war, the British Admiralty will use “ our
fleet ” where it pleases. We may have it in
time of peace when it isn’t needed for Aus-
tralian defence; in time of war, when it is
needed to save our cities from attack, we have
it on the best authority that the fleet we pay
for will probably be away helping to save
Hingland from invasion, and we will take our
chance. Luckily, this wondrous agreement lasts
only four years longer. But it is cunningly
drafted : it is to persist unless specific notice is
given by the Australasian Parliaments two years
before the date of termination. The fact that
notice ought to be given grows more and more
conspicuous every day.
A Large Legal Muddle.
Anyone who carefully studies the N.S.
Wales Landaninkum Tax Act must come
to the conclusion that the lawyer is the worst
lawmaker going. The proverbial coaeh-and-
four enactment is a fool to this lawyer-made
statute. There are gaps in it which would take
a furniture-van and a mob of brumbies without
stretching. For instance, section 42 provides
that the Commissioners, before proceeding to
give notice to taxpayers, must complete an
assessment-book, which, under section 31, must
contain names of owners and descriptions of
land to be taxed, with full particulars. Having
completed this book, the Commissioners may
then proceed to give notices, &c. How, at pre-
sent, not half the information required for this
book has been obtained, so that it cannot
possibly be completed. As a matter of fact, it
is alleged that no such book has ever been com-
menced, so that all notices sent out so far are
probably illegal. This appears to be a gap
which admits of a large amount of traffic. The
point as to the Court of Review seems to indi-
cate an equally wide thoroughfare. Section 44
gives taxpayers who have received notices of
assessment to which they object 30 days within
which they can appeal to the Court of Review.
Now, a large batch of notices was issued on
October 23rd, yet no Court of Review was
gazetted until November 18th, leaving only
four days instead of thirty in which taxpayers
could appeal. Landowners “ out back ” could
not even get the announcement of the appoint-
ment of the Court until after the time in which
appeals could legally be lodged had expired.
This must break the Act all up if any attempt
to enforce payment is made in such cases. Even
an Australian Court of Equity would issue an
injunction against any such unjust action.
The promise of the Commissioners to regard
objections as notices of appeal is a breach of
the Act. When Parliament says appeals must
be made to a Court of Review, no little tin god
in the Public Service can set that aside and say
appeals can be lodged somewhere else. The
question of notices opens up still another rent
in this charming Act. Under section 47 the
tax is payable on a date to be gazetted, and the
Commissioners may sue for the amount if not
paid 60 days later, or, under the amending Act,
00 days later. The Commissioners gazetted
October 23rd ns the day on which the tax must
be paid. They can therefore sue on January
21st. Now, not half the notices are yet
issued, nor can the balances be completed
for some time, probably not until January.
But in any case all taxpayers who do not get
their notices next week at latest cannot have
their 30 days clear within which to appeal.
This must upset the legality of all late
notices. The idiotic flaw in the Act which
makes “the present ” Customs Chief Powell
first Commissioner, with no provision for
resignation or further appointments, makes
a fitting climax to the muddle. No won-
der lawyer Makinson is going to fight
all 115 cases entrusted to him, that Rhada'
manthus Heydon has already been briefed to
fight a test case, and that the whole Loranorder
party is going about arranging coaching teams
to gallop through the Act. Altogether, as the
early and late notices under the land-tax are
obviously illegal and the middle ones very
doubtful, the situation is about as muddled as
it can be. And this is how lawyers make law.
4 1_
Wanted—A Crown Defender.
A certain unconsidered aspect of the Glen-
brook (N.S.W.) tragedy is worth considering.
Suppose one of the victims of Butler or
Harwood or whoever it was—there is no legal
proof yet against anyone in particular, but
it certainly was someone, and for the purpose
of this argument he may as well be called
Butler as Cholmondeley or Mephistopheles
—suppose the aforesaid victim—a man with-
out money or influence—had come on top
in the struggle and killed that man of many
aliases in self-defence. The absolutely correct
and right-minded thing under such circum-
stances would be for him to give himself up
and tell his improbable tale to the police. He
would be locked up. The police and detectives
would hunt up his antecedents and probably
prove ugly circumstances in his past history.
Being locked up, he couldn’t search for
evidence to disprove them, however false
they might be. Also, he couldn’t, in his
cell, dig up the dead man’s past career,
on which the whole case would hang. The
detective force engaged for the prosecution
might hunt up the smellful record of the corpse,
but as its business would be to find evidence
against the prisoner it would more likely leave
the matter alone. There is no detective force
provided on the side of the defence, so the
matter would continue to be left alone, unless
the prisoner was rich enough to hire a special
detective service of his own. The rest of the
story unrolls like a panorama. Trial—the
charge (supported by the police, detectives,
Crown Law department, State Prosecutor,
police-witnesses, and the evening newspaper
horror) being that the ruffian in the dock had
treacherously murdered the benefactor who
provided all the money for the prospecting trip,
and otherwise befriended him. Evidence to
prove that the prisoner, or someone else of the
same name, had previously been in trouble for
assault and other things. Unless the prisoner
is called Phlxynotrms it is almost always
possible to mix him up with the misdeeds
of some other person of the same name, and if he
is named Phlxynotrms it can generally be
shown that his real name is Smith, and the
previous convictions of Smith are as numerous
as the sands on the sea-shore. Found guilty—
there being no evidence on his side except his
own entirely uncorroborated statement, which
was too preposterous for belief. Sentenced to
death amid general execration. Hanged amid
ditto. That is how things would almost cer-
tainly have gone in New South Wales ; there-
fore, the man who kills another in self-defence
where there are no witnesses would do well to
bury him deep and silently beneath the wattle,
and then light out at his best pace for parts
unknown. The chance of the other man, if he
had killed Butler in a disagreement down
a gully, would have been almost as bad as
Butler’s if it is proved that he killed the
Other man. And if the other man hadn’t
killed Butler, but merely crippled him
with a shovel in self-defence, he might have
been tried in the same way, and Butler’s
evidence against him might have made his con-
viction all the more certain, and he might
have been hanged for attempted murder just
the same. Until there is a Crown De-
fender as well as a Crown Frosecutor, and a
detective force to work up the defence as well
as the prosecution, the inadvisability of the
other man leaving Butler —or whoever it was—
alive when once he got on top is sufficiently
evident, and the advisability of finishing him
off with the shovel, and making him as unrecog-
nisable as possible with the crowbar, and then
planting him very deep down under a grassy
mound, sticks out conspicuously. The man
who takes any chances with Australian criminal
law, unless he has much money behind him, is
a madman or some new kind of suicide.
The “Meejum.”
In a recent issue The Bulletin explained,
in re the U.S. Presidential election, that it
didn’t believe in the silver craze of the Bime-
tallic League, and since then a storm of litera-
ture about the “ meejum of exchange” has
raged around this office. Most of the “ meejum ”
cranks write to say that they are sorry for The
Bulletin’s ignorance, and state that it is going
back on democratic principles because it doesn’t
see why any community should pledge itself to
buy the product of the bloated silver-mine-
owning capitalist at more than its value. The
following is an extract from about the most
coherent letter of the heap :
I am sorry to see The Bulletin joining in the chorus of
“Yah” against. Bryan and his followers. If ever there
was a “ Fat Man ” party in existence it is the party which
has partly bounced and partly cajoled the electors of the
United States to vote the M’Kinlky ticket. I am certain
that your attitude is the result of misapprehension, espe-
cially on the currency question. Few writers go deeply
enough into the currency question to get to the root of
the matter. The world’s currency should have expanded
with the world’s expanding trade. Had it been left to
itself, that is to say, had both gold and silver continued
to be coined into money of full debt-paying power, the
currency would have thus expanded. The demonetisation
of silver in 1873, however, contracted the world’s currency
by nearly one-half when it most needed expansion.
Hence the fall in prices whioh makes industry in gold
standard countries unprofitable and causes capital to be
unemployed.
The Bulletin is deeply grateful to this and
several dozen other writers who are sorry for
its ignorance, and offers a few suggestions in
reply. (1) Silver was not demonetised in 1873,
as the silver cranks persistently allege. One or
two countries abandoned, or partially aban-
doned, the silver standard then, or about then ;
others did it previously; some, like Austria
(1892), France (1870), and India, did it later ;
some, like Mexico and China, stick to silver to
this day, and the United States actually ex-
tended the silver currency after ’73. The
“crime of 1873” is only a catchword, and a
pretty thin one at that. (2) “ The world’s cur-
rency should have expanded with the world’s ex-
panding trade.” Well, it did. Commerce more
than doubled between 1860 and 1890. The world’s
supply of money also more than doubled.
“ The demonetisation of silver in 1873” (which
didn’t happen) didn’t contract the currency ;
the currency went on expanding. (3) Also, the
great recent fall in prices began in ’65, not in
’73; the fall from ’65 to ’7O was far larger,
taking the world all over, than that from ’7O
to ’B3. Further, this paper doesn’t see why
the masses should barrack for the silver Fat
Man any more than for the gold Fat Man. The
silver Fat Man says he represents the cause of
the poor, but every Fat Person who wants to
use the poor for his own ends says the same
thing, and it wants to be supported by better
evidence.
The discovery of the bodies of Preston and
Weller, the carefully-buried victims of the
Glenbrook assassin, has moved quite a lot of
people to moralise over the old, threadbare
maxim that “murder will out.” It is a com-
forting maxim in a sense, and tends to keep up
a belief in law’norder and establishes a sort of
idea that Providence personally lends a hand
to hunt up the assassin, but it is a rather stupid
maxim all the same, and rests on no visible
evidence whatever. All the public really knows
is that the murders it knows of do come out ;
the murders it doesn’t know of naturally don’t,
because if they did come out it would know of
them. The two bodies which have been dis-
covered in the neighborhood of Glenbrook are
evidence, of a kind, that murder will out; the
innumerable bodies which haven’t been found
at any number of other places are equally good
evidence that it won’t. The fact that no one
knows whether there are any bodies at these
other places, wherever they may be, is con-
fronted by the equally solid fact that no one can
prove there are not, and, unless someone can
prove there are not, then the comforting maxim
goes bung. All the evidence is that murder
simply takes its chance of coming out, like any
other crime. It comes to light very often
because the gun makes an awful noise in going
off, and the bloodstain is large and red and
obvious, and the remains are cumbersome and
heavy and take a long time to decay. And it
very often doesn’t come to light, inasmuch as
in 1894 there were 25 known murders in New
South Wales against only six convictions, and
how many murders there may have been which
were not known is mere guesswork.
Victorian Assembly’s attempts at insolvency-
law amendment are getting the usual kind of
opposition from “ respectable ” Fat Man in the
Council. The Lower House was for ruling
that no uncertificated insolvent should be en-
titled to obtain credit to the amount of <£2o
without notifying his insolvency to the firm he
was dealing with. Cornelius Job Ham “ con-
sidered this most un-English legislation, even
though it was in the English act,” and Fat Man
collectively would have none of it. He refused
to draw a line at the higher amount of
,£5O. Nothing less than full rejection of
this un-Hinglish British clause would satisfy
him, so the clause was struck out. An uncer-
tificated insolvent, under the present law, is
allowed to trade upon the ignorance of any mer-
chant who may happen to be unacquainted with
the gentleman’s commercial past. When the
gentleman has obtajned all the credit he can
get in his seeming character of a “ good mark,”
he is at liberty to stop payment again
without filing his schedule. An uncertificated
insolvent cannot be made any more bankrupt
than he was before. He stands on velvet as
regards his new creditors. They trusted him
at their peril. The law that operates against
an ordinary trader doesn’t operate against him .
He’s all right. And the Vic. Leg. Council,
being mainly composed of sanctimonious shuf-
flers and persons of weak, money-making intel-
lect, desires to preserve the uncertificated
insolvent from any sort of obligation. The
Council, in short, was asked to pass a clause
requiring a wholesale purchaser of goods to
state privately an all-important commercial
truth about himself when there should be
urgent reason for its private announcement,
and Fat Man, otherwise the Council aforesaid,
cried “ Truth be damned ! ” What the Hupper
’Ouse requires is a Bill to make truth a penal
offence.
The collapse of the Labor-party at Dunedin
(M.L.) election is another proof, on top of
thousands which went before it, that no cause
is much good which hasn’t its own press organ
to keep it before the public. Pinkerton and
Earnshaw, the two defeated members, were,
with the exception of the Victorian, Tren-
WITH, about the straightest and ablest
Labor politicians in Australasia. Earnshaw,
some years ago, beat the heavy and antiquated
Larnach out of sight. It was as big a surprise
as when the late Goliath was homicided by a
certain youthful party who is still at largo. In
those days Labor had an organ of its own in
Dunedin, but that little journal pegged out
from want and neglect about ’93, and ever since
the local press has been wholly Tory. For
three years the Dunedin papers have carefully
“educated” the people in the ways of the
National Hass, with the result that Dunedin
has gone back to Scobik Mackenzie and that
other battered campaigner, Henry Smith
Fish. Considering how weary the country was
years ago of Mackenzie and Fish, the resur-
rection of that pair of tired failures is a
remarkable episode.
Sad are the political uses of agricultural
shows. At Whittlesea (Vic.) the other week a
few local Tories gave their voice of the country
its annual shout over the show luncheon. One
Walsh, addressing himself particularly to
half-a-dozen of the fattest graziers in the dis-
trict, said : “No one present would think of
claiming for the present Parliament that it was
a reflex either of the wealth, the industry, or
the intelligence of the country.” It had made
awful strides towards Socialism (murmurs of
“ Down with dynermite ! ” from several choice
specimens of wealth and hintelligence), and
would have burst up the country but for the
wisdom of the Hupper House, which “ re-
flected in the truest sense the feelings and
interests of the best portion of the com-
munity.” This excited consumer of pro-
vender having sat down, Robert Harper
rose (and Staugiiton, M.L.A., after him)
to acknowledge that Walsh’s remarks,
although severe, were quite justified. Parlia-
ment, according to Harper, does not represent
the people— i.e., “ the best portion of the com-
munity,” and he is quite sure that many of their
proposals are not in accordance with the people’s
wishes, &c., &c. The Whittlesea game is
worked at every place where horses, cattle, pigs,
and poultry assemble and meet together once a
year. It is the game which J. B. Patterson
worked during his brief burst of champagned
Government, and it has become so notorious
that an agricultural show nowadays is thought
of as a function whereat dumb animals are in-
troduced casually in the interests of other
animals who want a chance to get their land-
owning sentiments unduly reported.

The drollest opponent of early-closing on Satur-
day seems to be a Vic. Leg. Councillor named
Melville, who asks : “Is the Government
going to deprive the people of the enjoyment
of Saturday night in the way they have grown
up to it ? ” The “ people ” referred to by this
lamentable creature are Melville, M.L.G., and
all others who, under previous conditions, have
been able to walk abroad on Saturday evenings.
For their enjoyment the shops ought to remain
open as of old. They have grown up to what
Melville would call the cheerful blaze of gas
that glorifies late shopping, even as the Saxons
of 800 years ago grew up to the habit of ex-
tinguishing all lights when Curfew sounded.
It would astonish Melville, M.L.C., to learn
that the history of civilisation is one eternal
story of some people being deprived of the chance
to do something which other people had long-
time objected to. Sunday was the great day for
public sports in Merrie England, and the toil-
ing population grew up to enjoy themselves
accordingly, but the Government ordered the
blessed Sabbath to be kept holy, since when
small shop assistants have been expected to
work and pray without any weekly burst of
recreation. Yet, if The Bulletin were to ask
Melville, the Scawtchman, whether it was
right of the powers that used to be to suddenly
deprive the masses of their old-established en-
joyments, Melville aforesaid would answer
“ Yes.” In his opinion the Lord disapproves
of Sunday sports. Well, in this journal’s
opinion, the same Lord is in favor of a uni-
versal Saturday half-holiday, notwithstanding
the fact that Melville, M.L.C., hasn’t grown
up to it.
Dear Bulletin, —St. James’s Gazette calls
attention to the humbugging system of London
Postal Department in delivering on telegram
forms the city “ telegraph ” messages which are
transmitted from office to office by pneumatic
tubes. These pneumagrams, it is contended,
should be charged for at certainly not more
than 50 per cent, of the genuine wire rates ;
also, the original paper lodged by the sender
should go right along to the addressee, instead
of being re-copied at the receiving office and
delivered under false pretences by a telegraph-
messenger. In Paris it has long been the prac-
tice to deal honestly with tube correspondence,
and the complaint of the cockney newspaper
begins to be echoed in Melbourne, where the
fraudulent farce of issuing a “ telegram ” copy
of a document that was partly blown anil
partly sucked to its official destination is in
harmony with other post-office scandals lately
brought to light.—F.

Zola’s “Therese,” the last great effort of
the Potter-Bellew co. at Sydney Lyceum, was a
really great affair. As an artistic but rather
verbose combination of devil, nightmare and de-
composed green spook it lays over most dramas
badly. It is the story of a young couple who
marry in Act 111. (when the bride proceeds to un-
dress on the stage), but between her and her hus-
band there constantly arises the vision of her late
husband—a weedy, babbling creature whom they
drowned jointly a year before, as an impediment
to their love. His spectre has haunted them ever
since, and it haunts them worse as time goes on—
a spectre with two inflamed eyes above a thin red
nose, and a thin catarrhal nose above a loose,
foolish, slouchy mouth and a green necktie. The
spectre grows specially active on the wedding
night; the happy pair talk about him with shud-
ders as they wander round each other half-dressed
in the bridal chamber, and about how they shoved
him under, and how unpleasant he looked when he
was found a week later, and how his ghost takes
its dripping walks at night to see them. The
deceased’s mother, stepping in casually, hears the
story, and is struck all of a heap with paralysis.
* * * •
Thereafter they make a happy family of
four; the newly-married couple, the ghost, and
the speechless paralytic who can only glare. The
husband and wife degenerate rapidlyin this society.
The husband becomes drunken and unshaven —a
shaking ma,ss of nerves and starts and jumps ; he
grows uncivil, and doesn’t change his collar re-
gularly. The beautiful Therese gets to be slovenly,
cross, and unkempt and knotty about the hair.
When she lays the table for the evening meal of
the drunk and the ghost and the general paralytic,
she dashes the plates anyhow on the untidy cloth
and throws the spoons promiscuously at anything,
and drops the thin soup around as one in an angry
dream. They curse each other over the meal, and
talk in shuddery whispers about the bloated state
of the late husband when he was fished out of the
water. Through all their married life they have
really talked of nothing but the bloated state of
the late husband. When evening falls and the
ghost begins to actively pervade the premises, and
the only light is the baleful glare in the eye of the
paralytic, they threaten to murder each other
and have their regular evening family con-
vulsions; and tie themselves in knots on the
carpet in the presence of the soup and the
ghost and the frozen mother. The gentleman
decides to give himself up and be comfortably
executed, and the lady helps him so enthusiasti-
cally to rush otit of the room on his way to the
police-station that they get jammed in the passage.
Then they each decide to kill the other, but it
doesn’t come off. So the shadows deepen, and
they call each other more dreadful names, and
gibber worse than ever about how they pushed
the late husband’s foolish head under water —and
at this moment the paralytic mother recovers, and
uses such _ awful language that they hurriedly
commit suicide. The one falls dead with his head
to the north and the other falls dead with her
head pointing to the south, and the ghost of the
decomposed one is laid, and the horror is at rest.
* * » *
Whatever Mrs. Potter cannot do, she cer-
tainly can do the dreadful Therese admirably.
The unutterable gruesomeness of the character
seems to suit her, and she rises to heights, or
depths, or something, that she has never reached
before in this country. The gruesomeness of the
other character seems to suit Kyrle Bellew equally
well; they degenerate beautifully together, and
have mutual family fits on the carpet in a manner
that is both powerful and impressive. The re-
mainder of the very small cast —said remainder
being Wilson Forbes, Oily Peering, Berkeley,
Mrs. Hill and Miss Polly Emery—is generally
satisfactory. “Therese ”is hardly likely ever to
be a popular play, but there are more creeps and
shudders to the square inch in it than in almost
any other place outside Bedlain, and that is at
least something.
** * *
Good old “ Called Back,” at Sydney Royal,
is doing rather better business than might have
been expected considering that the drama is now a
long way past its first youth. It depends merely
on Paolo Macari, and Rignold’s Macari is, in some
respects, a gratifying conspirator. He is large and
heavy and slow ; some of his speeches are
delivered with wearisome deliberation, and at
times it becomes a matter of wonderment why
any intelligent Czar should employ so painfully
gradual an agent. G. Rignold putting his feet
down with a solid clump in the paths of vice is
indeed an impressive spectacle. But even with
these drawbacks Paolo Macari is a finished ruffian.
There is high art in his leer, and his scowl,
and his soft insinuating voice, and his
large, wormy smile, and his impressive
crawlsomeness, and though he wouldn’t
Bhine much as a real spy because he has gallows
and grease written large on his countenance, he is
all in the way of a stage spy that could reasonably
be asked for. The fact that he is the villain,
however, is much too conspicuous ; his big black
eye-brows and moustache, and his mass of black
hair, and his murky hat, and his big, sombre
frock-coat, and the absolute blackness of his whole
outfit make him look like a huge blob of ink upon
the landscape. No one could look so desperately
black without being a sinner of the worst descrip-
tion. Plimmer’s hero is very good indeed all
through. The new heroine, Miss Emily Hughes,
is a moderate success ; she is pretty, and as
the deranged girl in the limelight she makes
a graceful picture, and there are patches of
strength in her rendering of the character, and
also patches of the other fellow. The Bulle-
tin hangs up its judgment until it has further
considered Miss Hughes. Miss Eanny Reynolds,
as a widow decorated with gin and peppermint,
and the clever Miss Watts-Phillips, are two dis-
tinct successes. Also good old Stirling Whyte,
the personage with the wail in his voice, is about
the most conscientious old man who ever tottered
with a long white beard along the road to Siberia.
The biggest scene in the piece is that in which
Stirling Whyte in his casual rag and his long
white hair and with a dishcloth on each foot
throws himself up in the Siberian atmosphere like
a great quantity of snakes, and curses Macari
with that robust curse which finally takes him in
the back in the last act. And the second best
scene, in The Bulletin’s opinion, is that in which
Macari in a garret unbends his great mind by
thinking aloud about cats.
* * * *
The “ Milk White Flag,” at Her Majesty’s,
originally put up for one week only, struck so vast
a patch of luck that it was extended to three.
To-night (Thursday) is now advertised as the.very
last, but as the boom still continues it is impos-
sible to say for certain that some more new
arrangements won’t be made at the eleventh hour
and the “Flag’ be further prolonged. The
reason of this great boom is not wholly apparent.
There is a vast amount of gold lace and military
hat in the play, also a lot of detached wit, a good
deal of interesting satire, some respectable music,
and various other things. Still it is a great deal
of something about nothing, and a very limited
quantity of nothing at that; it hasn’t even a
semblance of cohesion; and there is a serious
want of feminine interest in it. The corpse on
ice has something to do with its success, and so
has the Napoleonic Conor, and the melodious
whistle of Frank Lawton holds it up materially in
a rather weak place, and the irrelevant high-kick
of Bessie Clayton fills another great void where
otherwise the cold wind of criticism would blow
through. Further, the co. rushes its absurdity
along with a whoop and a bang which leave no
time for serious-minded contemplation, and among
all these reasons the “Milk White Flag ” is a big
success for no cause in particular. Having
got which lucid remarks off its chest, The Bulle-
tin devoutly trusts that its readers know what it
means a good deal better than it does itself. After
vigorous consideration of the “Flag,” no paper can
be expected to understand its own meaning to any
extent. The most distinct feeling which
Hoyt s comedy-sparkle leaves behind is a vague
idea that one has been humorously kicked on the
head by a horse in an opium-vision.

Carl Hertz has returned to Sydney Tivon,
which is now crowded up with talent. Coleman
and Alice Leamar are on hand at one and the
same time. Those breakneck artists, the Vaidis
Sisters, who are whirled round up by the roof
hanging by one tooth to a flying trapeze, and who
walk head-down wards like the domestic fly on the
ceiling, are also there, and are still at the pinnacle
of their glory. It is a pretty high pinnacle, too,
and if one of the sisters falls off it and comes
down head first

James MacMahon’s cinematographe in
Pitt-street, Sydney, is as healthy as ever. The
other cinematographe has removed from the Cri-
terion to new quarters at 478 George-street, and,
so far, the demand for cinematographe seems to
keep both shows going. All the little casual shows
which come from no one knows where, and rage
for a brief season at Xmas-time, and then vanish
again, are also beginning to be heard of, but these
necessary evils are hereby let off with the usual
caution.

A swell Hebraic amateur co., mostly
named Levy and Cohen, and partly described as
Abrahams, and also alluded to in places as Marks
Baumann, gave three representations of
Mikado ” last week at Sydney Criterion, in aid
of that deserving charity, the Moses Montefiore
Jewish Home. The general result was better than
that of most amateur co s and quite as good as
the work of some alleged professionals. The
chorus was above par, and though no one stood
out like a tower among the leading characters, the
co. maintained a general level of decided respect-
ability. In fact, it might play “Mikado ” a few
more times without the sudden death and burial
of anybody becoming urgently necessary.
