The new Sydney Palace of Varieties
(it hasn’t yet opened) seems already to be the scene
of considerable not and disorder, and the manage-
ment is reported to have been throwing itself out
of the unfinished building with a good deal of
enthusiasm. Sometime last week a very large
stout gentleman advertised that he was lessee of
the premises, and that the opening would take
place on the 26th, and a smaller stout gentleman'
with a soft hat advertised that he was the lessee
and that the opening, irrespective of what the
other stout gentleman might say, was fixed for the
19 th. Two different managers were also announced.
Each of the conflicting parties was understood to
say that he was the only authentic stout gentleman,
and that the other one was entirely bogus, and
that his signature wouldn’t be recognised
Finally, however, the trouble seemed to
simmer down at least, only one advt. appears
now—and the opening is fixed for the 19th ;
and what it was all about is still practically a
mystery As there are no teeth-marks on the ear
of any of the parties concerned, a certain amount
of decorum was evidently maintained, even when
the trouble was at its worst.
** * *
Brough’s season at Melb. Bijou is conclud-
ing with a trial gallop of “ Nancy and Co.,”a farci-
cal comedy in one medium-sized first act. followed
by three brief outbreaks of madness. Augustin
iJaly, the Murkan manager, whilst evolving
Nancy and Co.’" from his inner consciousness of
a dozen other such comedies, introduced a some-
what original leading-lady part for the famous
Miss Ada Rehan. This is the character (here
played by Mrs. Brough) of a soulful young
matron who—under a masculine alias, and
unknown to her husband—collaborates by
Post with a young man in writing a play.
When she gets news that their joint effort is about
to be staged, the authoress comes along in a state
of excitement to reveal her identity to the
astonished other fellow, and a secret interview
between them leads to a whirl of compromising cir-
cumstances. The literary lady in question is not
too improbable to be humanly interesting, the
other characters are lively fictions, and the rush of
events travels on old familiar lines at express
speed. Sometimes the fooling is almost exquisite.
Here and there, on the other hand, a conjic situa-
tion seems puerile rather than droll, but, taken
for all-and-all, this is a promising entertainment,
likely to improve rapidly in effect when the B.
company has it well under control. Seven Mel-
bourne performances and further rehearsals should
give “ Nancy and Co.” a fair chance to become a
big success in Sydney. It was already a consider-
able success last Saturday.
Mrs. Brough offers a sufficiently natural
suggestion of Nancy Brasher, the clever warm-
blooded woman in love with a commonplace,
jealous husband. Also Harry Roberts, making
his debut with the company, drops easily into his
place as Nancy’s literary partner. His style
might be a little lighter and broader, but it
couldn’t be more pleasant and unforced than at
present. In fact, Roberts appears certain to go
on and prosper exceedingly in the new engage-
ment. Titheradge devotes himself chiefly to
hunting for imaginary proofs of his wife’s mis-
behaviour, and kicking at the doors of eccentric
chambers where somebody is hiding from his
wrath. Brough is a baffled amateur detective and
disseminator of false suspicions in a long brown
coat. Certain other principals flit around pro-
miscuously, or sit concealed under rugs ; and
scenery for the four acts is supplied by a couplß of
•inexpensive interiors.
** * *
“One of the Best’' has progressed, favor-
ably at Melbourne Royal these last few weeks.
Bland Holt’s gaudy drama came in a time of hot
weather when the average spectator gave his
patronage under protest, and the effort of follow-
ing the plot was rather too much for him, but
since the temperature began to abate the reputa-
tion of the braw Heelan’ leg-show has risen
steadily. On Saturday the drum and fife band
played with great intensity to a crowded house,
therefore “ One of the Best ” may be expected to
feed the managerial exchequer until Xmas. It is
full of items which improve on acquaintance. Bor
instance, Mrs. Bland Holt, bringing desi>atches for
the hero in the ta-ra-ra pocket of her “ rationals,”
appears to get nicer every time one sees her. The
military manoeuvres on foot work more smoothly
and the supers, as a rule, betray a growing interest
m the fate of Walter Baker, who, for his part,
seems less painfully conscious of his undressed
limbs and overwhelming headgear. Moreover, the
fact that there are no battles, murders, or gallant
rescues attached to the present Royal panorama is
so utterly astounding that it can’t be fully grasped
within the limits of a first visit, and a person
needs to go twice before feeling certain on the
point.

“ Stars of All Nations ” retired sadly from
the Princess’s last week, leaving the stage free
for a fortnight’s rehearsal of the Firm’s Xmas
annual. A terrible freak of variety-show was
perpetrated towards the close of the “ Star’s ”
season. A stoutish little lady in a green-satin cos-
tume sang a ditty concerning a little yaller coon,
and, when the refrain had been sundry times re-
peated, an irrelevant boy, popping up by private
arrangement in a private box, sang it once again
without accompaniment from the orchestra.
The boy, who was a poor little vocalist at his best,
cracked on the top note, but, under any musical
circs., the spectacle of the green satin lady getting
assistance from this unexpected quarter (at the
Princess s, of all theatres) would have given public
sentiment a shock. Luckily, there wasn’t much
audience on hand to witness the act of sacrilege.
* * * -*
“ Matsa, the Fire Queen ; or &c., &c.,” is
the name finally bestowed upon the Firm’s panto-
mimic extravaganza, and, judging from the length
of the title, a fortnight’s rehearsal will be found
rather too short than otherwise. Further parti-
culars next week.

At Melb. Opera House the boys, also the
adults, have taken very kindly to Chirgwin, and
that old-established study in black-and-white is a
large success. Time has robbed the tall Kaffir of
his original leanness, but all his other characteris-
tics remain as before. He sings fetcbingly through
his nose, and is an artist on manv instruments ;
and in several particulars. Chirgwin, as a lot of his
present admirers will remember, has given the
same sort of entertainment for about 20 years
without exhausting his popularity, so it is prac-
tical ly useless to question his cleverness. Only
the fittest survive to any great .extent on the
public stage.

Les Frferes Eclair, the excellent “ crocodile and demon ’

melodrama at Melb. Royal is Barratta, who went forth and

The sudden disappearance of Arthur Garner’s name
fromadvts. of the new variety-show at Melb. Princess’s
was very generally remarked. At the outset this out-of-
place enterprise was all Garner; then it became less
Garner, and finally the ex-Triuinvir faded right away.
Brough, when he engaged Arthur Roberts, wanted
sister Laura to play a boy-part, but the young lady
couldn’t persuade herself to wear trousers seriously, not
even in farcical comedy. As Little Billee, in the “ Trilby’’
burlesque, she didn’t profess to be a real member of the
other sex.
King Hedley is running open-air concerts at Perth
Cremorne Gardens, with Regina “ Ben Bolt ” Nagel as his
pole-star.
A new show is an X-ray threepence-in-the-sioc machine.
“ Take your bones while you wait. Come and see your
innards all a-growing. Special terms to sweethearts and
newly-married couples ’’—will be common street-calls in a
week or two.
Clifford Ferley, Melbourne’s prize theatrical benefit
organiser, who has more enthusiasm to the cubic inch
than others to the cubic ton, has just received from the
police department a certificate for heroism displayed in
assisting four policemen to capture a gang of roughs and
midnight prowlers in Russell-street. Cliff, violently
arrested the ringleader “ on his own,” and held him in an
iron grip till the police came up and handcuffed him.
Cliff ought now to organise a testimonial benefit to him-
self, under vice-regal patronage, as a mark of public
esteem of his bravery.
The weird drama recently staged at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) isn’t by a long way the first snake drama Aus-
tralia has seen. The lamented Johnny Hall took to this
branch of art once, and staged a production in which Alt.
Boothman had a desperate hand-to-hand encounter with
18 feet of infuriated python. It was Boothman’s great
scene, and the first night he waded in so desperately that
he cut the snake to flinders and emerged covered with
glory, applause, blood and mangled boa-constrictor. The
management remonstrated privately about the excessive
damage done to the animal, and Boothman promised to
be more careful of the next one. Second evening Alf.
and the new serpent charged each other, and the former,
again carried away by the situation, reduced his an-
tagonist- to unrecognisable sausage-meat inside 16 rounds.
Then the management decided that it couldn’t afford
to keep Boothman supplied with a new serpent every
night, and changed the bill.
South Australians curse the present drought for more
reasons than one. All over the blighted land amateur
dramatic eo’s are hurling “The Curse of Drink” and
“ Ten Nights in a Bar-room” at unoffending citizens, who
are prevented from killing the performers because the
proceeds are “ for the distressed farmers.” Why Heaven
has seen fit to kill the farmers’ cattle and wither the crops,
and yet spares the amateur actor, is an inscrutable
mystery.
There is one Sydney man who curses long and loudly
the cineinatographe. In his amiable, husbandly way he
took his wife to see the cin. (new abbreviation), and in the
Sunday afternoon disembarkation scene at Manly, his
better half saw what she believed to be her husband com-
ing ashore with another lady. To be the more convinced,
she saw the tableau fully half-a-dozen times with the aid
of opera-glasses. The accused male indignantly denies
everything, but, as he cannot prove a complete alibi for
that particular Sunday afternoon, and his wife won’t
entertain the idea of an “ extraordinary likeness,” there
is a big storm in the once happy ’ome. The possibilities
of the cin. as a worker of mischief to supposedly upright
people are. great.

only had an alleged one previously.

The Wild-Vat Column.
The private depositors of the Queensland
National Bank have a really solid grievance against the
Government for the “ assistance ” it rendered to that
dreadful institution. Roughly speaking, the Govern-
ment has £4,000.000 in the institution ; the public about
±'4,500,000. But the Government has a prior claim, there-
fore all the loss falls on private depositors, unless the whole
of the private deposits are insufficient to cover it; at all
events, private deposits have to be exhausted first. The
Q.N. directors have fooled away, so far as is known, about
one-third of the deposits. Presumably they fooled away the
Government and private money with the same beautiful
facility; therefore the situation is as follows : If there
had been no State support the deposits would have
been about 41 millions, and when the directors
‘blewed” one-third of it there would be three
millions left, or 13s. 4d. in the £. State support gave
these fatuous directors B*4 millions to play with instead
of 4*4- The loss of one-third therefore makes nearly three
millions gone. The State’s prior claim saddles the whole
of this on to private depositors, therefore State support
reduces their assets to about 6s. Bd. in the £. If there
are more revelations to come, which is quite possible
the whole of the private deposits may be swallowed
up in the general shortage. If there had been only pri-
vate deposits to deal with even the most awful directorate
on earth couldn’t have stolen or fooled away quite all of
them ; it must have left enough to pay a dividend some-
where. Supposing the general deficit runs to 11s. in the
£, however, the whole of the private depositors’ money
will go in making good their own and the Government’s
deficit.
The Government got the same rate of interest as
private depositors, and the private depositors were com-
pelled to guarantee repayment of its inonpy to the extent
of every cent they had in the bank without receiving any
payment for this stupendous risk. Government “sup-
port ”in the past just about doubles their loss. Which is
one solid reason why any Government should start a State
Bank, and keep its money there at its own risk. No State
has any right to go round swindling private depositors in
this fashion.
The Bulletin hereby retracts everything it has
ever said about the advisability of keeping the Q.N.B.
afloat. A complete return shows that there are 7702
private holders of interest-bearing deposits to the amount
of £4,180,000. Of these 4430 depositors and £2,719,000 of
the money belong to England. Therefore, the proposal
to hand the institution over to the depositors only means
creating another English bank in Australia, and
increasing the permanent mortgage of J. Bull on this un-
happy country. There are already five Australasian
banks bossed in London : the Union, Australasia, E.S.
and A., London, and National of Maorilaiul. The Q.N., if
the proposed arrangement is carried out, though its
nominal head office will probably be in Brisbane, will
make six. And considering that there is already a great
deal too much British financial institution in this country,
and too much British mortgage, and too much tendency
to have everything done for us by our dear brother Bull,
the Q.N. Bank had better shut up and end its utterly dis-
creditable career for good. Other Australian banks have
lost their capital and reserves, and yet go on ; but so far
every one which acknowledged its inability to pay its de-
positors has shut up and the directors, as a rule, have been
tried —and acquitted. Even in smellful Melbourne the
law went through the form of trying Davies and Loader
and their colleagues. Therefore, precedent, so dear to
the soul of every Upper House, demands that the Q.N.B.
should close up’ and that directors Hart, M.L.C., A. H.
Palmer, K.C.M.G., M.L.C., Morehead, M.L.C., and Web-
ster should be proceeded against forthwith. If they
object, all Tub Bulletin can say is that the spec-
tacle of three M’s.L.C. refusing to follow precedent and
demanding “ rash and experimental legislation ” in their
own interests, is a lamentable affair.
* * * *
Brisbane Worker publishes what purport to be
extracts from two speeches by Minister Tozer as reported
in Hansard. On 4th August, ’96, Tozer is represented as
saying: “ I have had sufficient confidence in the Queens-
land National Bank to leave my money in it. The best
proof I can give of the sincerity of my belief that the bank
is solvent is that I risk my all in it.” The second extract
represents the same Tozer on Ist December, 1896, saying :
“ I have not the slightest pecuniary interest in connection
with that bank.” If both these extracts are solid fact,
then there is something touching in the picture of Tozer
solemnly attesting the solvency of the wretched bank,
and stating that he was backing his opinion with a good
part of his fortune ; and then the same Tozer, a very short
time afterwards, getting clear of the concern that he had
such beautiful confidence in. If Tozer really assured the
public that his faith in the Q.N. Bank was so vast that he
trusted it with his hard-earned pile, and other people
trusted it on the strength of that assurance, and then
Tozer got out silently and left them still trusting, what
kind of a moral claim have these people upon Tozer sup-
posing anything goes wrong?
One thrilling proposal made by Finney,
M.L.A., in Queensland Parliament was to the effect that
the State should accept from the Q.N. Bank a composi-
tion of 13s. 4d. in the £ and call things square. This
would mean dropping over a million, and Treasurer Nel-
son, with the fear of his next financial statement before
his eyes, sat on the proposition instantly. Also Dickson
objected to it on the ground that such an arrangement
“ would create an impression that the bank couldn’t pay
20s. in the £.” Which was a gorgeous piece of bluff.
The report of the Govt.’s own committee of inquiry
says distinctly that the Q.N.B. can’t pay 20s. in the £ or
anything like it.
* * * *
Thus the Capricornian (Rockhampton) on the
Q.N.8.: “ But the man in whose hands lay the manage-
ment of the bank till the beginning of the present year,
and who therefore was primarily responsible for all
errors, has passed beyond human reach." Again, dear
friends, the man who is dead !
* * * •
The case of the Dominion Banking Corporation,
of Melbourne, was unearthed again the other day in the
law-courts. The Dominion was a very strange and awful
bank which died an unnatural death. It had a nominal
capital of £3,000,000 in 600,000 shares, but only 70,000
seem to have been taken up. The co., in fact, seems to
have been mainly C. H. James, the long-haired “ Father
of the Land Boom,” who held 68,179 shares out of the
70,000. When it burst, a meeting, which was naturally
almost wholly 0. H. James, appointed a liquidator and
gave him absolute power, among other things, to “com-
promise all calls and liability to calls.” Thus the liquida-
tor (largely appointed by 0. H. James) received (partly
from C. H. James) power to accept from 0. H. James, if
he felt so inclined, a halfpenny in the £ of his
immense liability. The liquidator called on 0. H. James
to pay up £171,399, and James, it is alleged, hasn’t done
so up to date. Until James pays the shareholders will
probably get just about nothing, as the other shareholders
amount to practically nil and are largely insolvent.
Therefore, an English creditor, who had over £20,000 in
the hopeless institution, asked Judge Hood : (1) That the
co. should be wound up under supervision of the court,
instead of by the liquidator so largely appointed by C. H.
James ; (2) that if C. 11. James doesn’t pay up his calls he
be made insolvent like any other individual, and asked to
explain where his property has gone to. The court, how-
ever, didn’t seem to like laying a hand roughly on the
“ Father of the Boom,” but it yielded so far that if
the liquidator proposes to compromise with James
the aforesaid English creditor shall have due notice, and
may be represented at his own cost in any argument that
ensues. Which is very comforting.
The full balance-sheet of the London Bank of
Australia proves to be quite as bad as the cabled sum-
mary represented. The result of the half-year’s opera-
tions was a net loss of £232, and, as the bank also paid a
dividend of 554 per cent. (£4728) on preference capital, its
funds decreased £4960 as the result of six months’ work.
Why any dividend at all was paid, seeing: there were no
current earnings to pay it out of, is not very clear. This
leaves only £10,936 to show as the accumulation of three
years, during which the original capital has received
no dividends. The contingency account (which is really
the reserve-fund of the old bank set aside to meet losses)
has been gradually fading for some time, and now
the remaining £331,000 is deducted from the assets in one
fell swoop. This, according to the report, is done “to
meet the requirements of probable legislation in Victoria.”
As the only probable legislation in Victoria which has anj r
apparent bearing on the case is the Companies Bill, pro-
viding for more stringent audit, and the Credit Foncier
Bill, this is rather unaccountable. The London Bank is
now negotiating for better terms from its reconstructed
depositors, and, all things considered, it seems to want
them.

Vic. Commissioners of Audit report that for
the year ended 30th June, 1806, the Victorian public
debt decreased by £372,000, and the annual interest bill
was reduced by £11,544. This sort of thing is so wildly
unusual in Australian finance that it ought to be framed.
* * * *
According to the Commissioners of Audit, the
Vic. revenue received during last financial year was
£6,458,000; ordinary expenditure during same period
(not counting £250,000 in reduction of public debt),
£6,006,000; surplus, £452,000. That is how things would
have panned out if the accounts had been kept on N.S.W.
Premier Reid’s “ cash basis,” and if Turner had been on
the same dodge he would have had much the larger sur-
plus of the two. G. Reid’s finance is mostly small dodgery
with a view to making things look sound when they
aren't.
• • * *
For the first five months of the present financial
year the N.S.W. revenue has been £220,000 less than for
the corresponding period of last year. Also, so far ex-
penditure is £168,000 ahead of revenue, which looks bad
for the promised surplus.
Of the N.S.W. 3 per cent, local stock .£207,000
was taken up in October. Then for November the sales
only amounted to £54,000. Treasurer Reid, however,
still reckons on getting £1,000,000 a year. When J. Bull
was content in ’95 with £3 4s. per cent., £3 per cent, cer-
tainly ought to be enough for any common, degraded
cornstalk.
* * * *
The Maoriland local .£500,000 loan at was a
success, though with no great margin. The amount
offered was £508,000, and the loan went off at a small
premium. The direct saving by local flotation is about
£7OOO in expenses, the cost of remitting the half-yearly
interest, and the Bank of England “ management ex-
panses ; ” also, there is a further indirect saving through
the retention of £17,500 a year interest in the country.
Further, the Government retains the right to pay off the
loan at any time between 1907 and 1922, which gives a
margin of 15 years for conversion. Considering that the
last M. L. loan floated in London had to be fixed for 50
years, and taking all the other circumstances into account,
Maoriland seems to have done quite as well in the local
market as it did in London.
• ♦ • *
That heart-breaking institution, the Sydney-
Permanent Freehold Land and Building Co., is recon-
structing some more. It first reconstructed in 1891,
getting its deposits extended for a lengthy period at 5 per
cent. Then it reconstructed again in 1895. It agreed to
pay the half-year’s interest, falling due 24th March, 1895,
in six half-yearly instalments of Bs. 4d. per cent. Future
interest was to be reduced to 2f, per cent., and, for two
years, payment at even this reduced rate was to be sus-
pended, the amount due being added to the principal.
Payment of the principal was postponed indefinitely.
Now, by way of a third reconstruction, the institution ap-
parently admits that it can’t even pay the small instal-
ments of that old half-year’s interest, which has been due
ever since March, ’95, and it proposes that its recon-
structed deposits shall henceforth bear no interest at all,
that interest now due shall be added to principal, and that
the principal shall be repaid at the rate of Is. in the £ at
any time between now and the end of the world, when
there is enough money on hand to provide the Is. afore-
said. The Sydney P. F. L. and B. Co. owes £134,000 of
deposits, and its £250,000 of alleged assets apparently
yield about 1 per cent, all round. As the chairman says
that liquidation wouldn’t pan out for more than 17s. in
the £, it would seem that even he reckons that £llO,OOO
of the assets are only paper-value. Taking it all round, a
reconstruction which stops payment of all interest and
fixes the principal indefinitely is about the largest order
on record. Even Jd. in the £is at least a great deal more
definite.
* * * *
The balance-sheet of Melb. Grand Coffee Palace
shows a profit for the half-year of £534, which is rather
over 1)4 per cent, per annum on capital. This is so much
better than some recent returns that the half-yearly
meeting ought to have been a cheerful affair, but a gloom
was cast over things by a shareholder who denounced the
balance-sheet, and alleged that the co. had really
made a loss. He also demanded an immediate winding
up, but was badly defeated. The rest of the meeting was
largely row and personalities, and indignant explanations
by a person named Murphy. As the malcontents were
left quite unsatisfied at the end of the meeting, it is ex-
pected that at next half-yearly gathering proceedings
will be opened with an axe.
* * * *
The National Trustees, Executors, and Agency
Co. (Melbourne), one of the very few Vic. trustee com-
panies which has always kept its head above water, pre-
sents a very respectable balance-sheet. The profits for
the year, after providing £l6 for depreciation of the
tables and chairs, and the" big iron safe, &c., in the office,
are £2986. Then in the insane desire to keep up its
record the board pays the usual 10 per cent, dividend
(£3000), and consequently a prosperous year’s work leaves
it £l4 to the bad. Last year the net profit was £3OOl,
and the same dividend left it just £1 to the good. The
year before the net profit was £2813, and the same 10 per
cent, dividend left it again £lB7 to the bad. Conse-
quently, the National Trustees have gone backwards
£2OO in three good years solely through the 10 per cent,
mania. The co. has £30,000 capital and a solid reserve of
£23,400, but £43,000 of these funds are invested in mort-
gages, which are always a more or less uncertain item.
Also, there are considerable risks about the trustee busi-
ness, which require a large margin to cover them, and
apart from everything else the payment of dividends
which are not fully covered by current earnings is a fool
of a business at ali times.
* * * *
N.S.W. pastoralists are beginning to feel bad in
these lean times over the exorbitant charges of the Sydney
woolbrokers. The situation is a complicated one. Sup-
pose a clip of 200 bales, each 4001 b., or 80,0001 b. in all,
to be worth absolutely 6,' t d. per lb., or £2083 6s. Bd. In
the first place, the seller is charged one-eighth of a penny
per lb., or £4l 13s. 4d. Then the buyer is charged one-
eighth, or another £4l 13s. 4d. The next trouble is that
the buyer at auction must go up by bids of a farthing per
lb., therefore, if he is prepared to give only 6Jd. net he
must necessarily bid only 6d., and the odd eighth of a
penny thus sacrificed means the loss of another £41135.
4d. Then on the £2OOO actually realised by the wool there
is a deduction of 1) per cent, commission, or £3O. Con-
sequently, the account sales pan out as follows Value
of wool, £2083 6s. Bd. Less £41135. 4d. charged to seller ;
£41135. 4d. charged to buyer and deducted by him from
his bid; £4l 13s. 4d. further deducted by buyer on ac-
count of rule limiting him to farthing bids ; £3O commis-
sion ; net result, £1928 6s. Bd. In other words, charges
amount to 5)4 per cent., and another 2 per cent, is casu-
ally lost by the wayside, as it were, making 7)4 P er cent,
in all. On very low-priced wool the selling charges alone
run as high as 10 per cent. No wonder the pastoralist is
beginning to kick against the wool-broking iniquity.
A. J.S. Bank shares {£7 10s. paid up), buyers,
205.; sellers, 265. Last year they climbed as high as 48s.
The “ restoration of confidence” again.
Evidence in the case of the late City Treasurer
of Sydney, Arthur J. Speer, charged with the embezzle-
ment of £725, disclosed the extraordinary fact that an
account (called the City Treasurer’s account) had not been
audited by the official auditors or anyone else since 1890.
Speer alone had absolute charge of it and gave no state-
ment of it to anyone, the auditors not even knowing of
its existence. This, it was pleaded, was the usual custom.
The account was one into which contractors’ bonds and
over-paid rates were paid. The total deficiency was said
in evidence to amount to about £2500.
* * * *
The record of Block 14 for the year indicates
plenty of energy. For 11 months the mine sent away
46,000 tons of mineral products, of which 10,601 was car-
bonate ore, 13,733 sulphide concentrates for smelting,
8274 sulphide slimes for smelting, and 11,538 tons concen-
trates for shipment.
* * * *
The Junction continues to take out far more
than a fair proportion of the richer ore —absolutely more
shipping ore, in fact, than milling ore.
* * * *
Block 10 made a net profit last half year of
£5740. The co’s financial position is fairly strong; its
surplus of liquid assets (mostly “ sundry debtors ”) is
£26,583. The cash in hand at the end of the half-year
was within £5OO of enough to pay off liabilities. The
zinc tailings, which it is proposed to sell, are not taken
credit for—and very rightly, though they do average
12oz. silver and 5 per cent, lead per ton. It is the 34 per
cent, zinc that damns them.
« * * *
This year’s Broken Hill dividend-total will
amount to £540,000, independent of Tarrawingee,
Stephen’s Creek (B.H. Water Supply, Ltd.), and divs. in
respect of sales of properties outright. Of this total, the
B.H. Prop, is, of course, by far the largest contributor,
with £432,000 against its name ; then B.H. Junction with
£55,000, ai.d Block 10 and Block 14 with £20.000 apiece.
The just-started Junction North has scattered £12,000.
Official figures for Broken Hill are, so far, avail-
able for only nine months of this year : to the end of Oct.
the output actually exported was valued at £1,286,877.
Each quarter showed an advance on its predecessor : the
third quarter’s figures were just over £450,000. The
British has got to work in the fourth quarter, and the
Block 10 and Central new mills have been set going in
Nov., so that this last quarter’s outturn should leave the
half-million well behind. In which case, the year’s
exports will reach £1,800,000. Up to the end of Nov.,
this proportion had been more than maintained.
* * * *
An attempt is likely to be made to get some of
the public’s money into the old Angas mine, in the
Thackaringa district, near Silverton. Judging from past
experiences the Angas is a good mine to recommend to an
enemy.
* * • •
The Victory mine, Purnamoota (near Broken
Hill), will probably resume work shortly. Of, say, 100
mines and “ holes ” once busy around Purnamoota, only
one is now being worked, viz., the Black Prince. All the
others have been given over to the rabbits, which tenant
the surface-workings in thousands.
* * • *
New Crown of the Peak mine (N.S.W.) has
been placed under offer for £25,000 cash. The share-
holders had no difficulty in arriving at that decision. The
trouble will be to find the money.
* • * »
Orange (N.S.W.): “ The Prince of Wales mine
at Burnt Yards has been sold to the Cooper Australasian
Exploration Co. The Bletchington mine is also under
offer to the same co.” The first is an old mine, rich very
many years ago, and which the mining enterprise of
Orange has left religiously alone for years. The other is a
comparatively new mine.
• • * *
From Pambula, N.S.W. : “The Wolumla pros-
pectors are putting through another rich crushing.” As
to the value of the gold from the last, “mum” is the
word. Whispered that it was less than £1 an oz.
* * * *
Private enterprise is evincing faith in the future
of Wyalong. Though long delayed, Sully’s chlorination
works are nearing completion. Neild and fils, the dis-
coverers of the camp, are now running chlorination
works and doing good work, principally on their own ore.
Deeble, the well-known Bendigo pyrites-worker, is put-
ting up a plant which, complete, will cost some £14,000.
His own patented process, but the use of cyanide is the
basis of it. Stated, too, that the South German mine,
Maldon, Vic., is adopting the process.
* * *
What has become of the famous Hill End mine
bought by little Dr. Simon last year ? It had some won-
derful crushings. The rumor is that the rich veins have
been exhausted, and that is why the little battery has
remained so long idle.
* * * *
Highland Mary, Swamp Oak, N.S.W., owned
and worked successfully by the Brown Bros., is likely to
be sold next month. An unusually substantial deposit
has been put up. Price not yet disclosed, but it would
seem a fair thing at £20,000. Since the Browns took
possession they have crushed 1147 tons for 35570 z., and
are now running through their final crushing, from which
it is said about IOOOoz. are expected.
• • * *
Cobar, N.S.W. : “Barrass and Conley have got
a suspension of labor for the lease in dispute. The last
clean-up was satisfactory and fully up to expectations.”
Hang the expectations; how much gold did they get ?
What, too, has become of the great under-writing syndi-
cate ? Did John Howell report on the mine, and, if so,
what ? Has it floated or has it sunk ?
* * * »
The New Bushman’s mine, Parkes (N.S.W.),
can barely keep itself out of the mud. From the last
report it would seem that even 50 tons of poor ore brought
down the average of a six or eight weeks’ crushing. It
must be hard up for an excuse. The vein in the winzes is
looking well, but as these are below the bottom level, the
shaft must be deepened before it can be worked. It will
also, sooner or later, want a new shaft entirely. It would
be interesting to know how Messrs. Baxter and Saddler
came out of this magnificent amalgamation.
4c * ♦ 4c
Golconda, Parkes, N.S.W.: “ Driving and beat-
ing out backs to get stone out quickly. Hope to have a
crushing shortly of say 20 tons 1 ” An average of N.S.W.
gold-mining.
* * * *
The Hillgrove gold-producers are, with the ex-
ception of the Baker’s Creek, doing much better than
usual. Eleanora, 520 tons, 4310 z. ; Sunlight, 584 tons,
3610 z. ; West Sunlight, 342 tons, 3530 z. ; Baker’s Creek,
518 tons, 3470 z. This is about the lowest average the
mine has shown for many years. No profit in this, as
indicated by the price of the shares. When is that
English concern, the Garibaldi, going to crush ? What,
too, has become of the Baker’s Creek Extended ?
» * » *
The nominal capital of Westralian mines on the
London market is £66,261,000, and the dividends thereon
for last two years were £292,000, equal to about five shil-
lings per cent, per annum. And it was all contributed by
about half-a-dozen mines, four of which are now dead or
thereabouts.
« * * •
The Forrest Govt, rose to the occasion in dealing
with the jumping of the Hainault co’s lease. It was de
dared forfeited by Govt. Gazette notice for non-payment
of rent. The shares of this London Co. fell at once from
£2 to 10s. The London papers teemed with references to
the insecurity of mining titles in W.A. A panic was
threatened. No delay was allowed. A Cabinet meeting
was held, followed by an Executive, and the forfeiture
was annulled. The trouble, it was alleged, arose by mis-
take. But what about the jumper? Under the law he
came into legal possession. He was not a jumper in the
ordinary sense. The moment a lease is thus declared void
it is Crown land, open to anyone, and having gone into
possession he has a good cause for compensation against
the Govt.
* * * •
Coolgardie : “ Ten tons of stone from the Fre-
mantle Consolidated returned 1870 z., and before the
crushing 30lb. of specimens were removed, literally hang-
ing together with gold. The mine is to be placed on the
London market." Of course ! Mines like that want
capital. Much better to get gold out of London than out
of the mine !
* • * *
The Lady Loch mine, Coolgardie, has lots of
newspaper defenders in London. “ There is not one atom
of truth in the rumor that the reef has cut out," says the
Mining World. But is it not a cold-blooded truth that
the rich chute which produced nearly 3000 oz. from 800
tons is exhausted ? If not, why have the shares fallen
from ±'3 15s. to 265. ?
* « • *
Cue (W.A.) “ Victory United —145 tons, 3680 z.”
Splendid if it can be kept up. The haphazard crushings
of nine-tenths of W.A. mines have helped to knock the
bottom out of the boom. Calculations were based on the
regularity and uniformity of toe Band returns. Now it
has dawned on Bull that the continued presence of a bat-
tery is more than the average W.A. mine can stand.
* • • *
Modest Maryanski, mining expert, got himself
into a state of excitement over the finding of telluride
of gold in the Boulder Main Reef mine. He says : “It
Betties for ever the doubt that existed, all over the world,
as to the continuity in depth of the rich ore bodies en-
countered at Hannan’s. The rich gold of the 80-called
lode formations has come from the decomposition of the
telluride of gold. The discovery will improve, immediate-
ly, the state of the market, will restore confidence in the
future of gold mining in the colony, and will result in a
new mining boom, but on a sound and solid basis.” It is
always the new boom that’s going to be solid. The old
boom is always tacitly admitted to have been rotten. But
what has become of the bodies of rich telluride in Block
46 and other mines found many months ago ? Modest M.
seemed to have been unaware of their existence. M.M.’s
declaration has caused another telluride shriek by news-
papers.
* * * *
Ironclad M. Co., Pilbarra, W.A. : “The chair-
man said there was nothing to do but to wind up. Nothing
had been left undone to make it a profitable venture, but
the property had proved valueless." Another great news-
paper mine.
* * * *
At last the Fortune of War mine, W. A., has
had a crushing— Bo tons for 40oz.—like the historic baby,
a very little one.
• • • •
The Heidelberg G.M. Co. is out with a pro-
spectus. Location, Black Flag, W.A. The mine is re-
ported on by Chas. Chewings, Geologist, I’h. D.F.R., &c.,
and G. B. Fearby. But what the N.S.W. public want to
know at present is, what has become of the other mines
examined and recommended by these great experts, par-
ticularly those around the General Gordon, the scrip of
all of which are at an alarming discount.

Croesus No. 1 North: “At the 100 ft. level the
lode is 25ft. wide and averages from 5 to 7oz. to the ton.”
Then why in Heaven’s name don’t they crush ? News of
this sort is never believed now till supported by a crush-
ing, and it is a waste of money to send it, and of space to
print it.

Lake View, one of the few great mines found in
Westralia : 720 tons, 19190 z., also producing 10 tons of
concentrates, worth 350 z. to the ton. The sulphurets
found in opening up the 200 ft.-level are good enough to
ship, and above there is a big body of ore which, for a
great length, will mill an average of 3oz. It should become
a very big producer in a few months.

A cheerful report of the Kangaroo mine on the
Murchison : “ A premature and primitive battery, erected
nearly two years ago, only crushed a few weeks. The
mine has had much exemption, and was almost jumped
recently. All the gold was, of course, in the tailings,
and the whirlwinds have distributed these well over the
country.”

The half-yearly statement of accounts (to 31st
October, 1896) of the Curtin Davis Proprietary Co., an
elegantly-printed publication in an eau de Nil tinted
wrapper, sets forth £22,000 of cash and bank balances as
liquid assets, and proclaims the manager’s comforting
belief that the property will be a “highly payable mine”
when the railway is open for traffic (in March next) and
the rock drills are at work.

Charters Towers crushed for Nov. 19,183 tons
for 20,0240 z. over the oz. per ton. Divs. went
£18,233, making an eleven months’ total of £219,000.
The share market closed weaker than at Nov. 1, but with
some promise of recovery for Xmas. The biggest yield
was Brilliant’s—3o39oz. from 2634 tons, paying a 4d. div.
of £4333. Day Dawn Block and Wyndham, 2535 tons,
24960 z., div. £12,500 ; Brilliant and St. George, 1728 tons,
21750 z., div. £5400; Papuan Brilliant and Vic., 590 tons,
12580 z., div. £2400. The Brilliant Deep Levels is now down
2553 ft., and they are going to test her with the drill foi
another 500 ft. The pyrites works paid a 6d. div., though
not yet in full swing.
* * * *
Croydon Consols’ figures to date are 31,0870 z. of
gold won from 9955 tons stone, valued at £73,957 ; divi-
dends, £52,909. Where’s Westralia ? The present com-
pany has taken £43,875 gold out this year, averaging
about £lO per ton in value. The mine has paid a total of
£30,000 to the English holders who bought it for £10,500.
Last div., £20,000.
Edward Dyson’s book of verses is now
published. It includes all his best Bulletin
work, and is handsomely bound in cloth. The
title is “Rhymes from the Mines and Other
Lines,” and a big sale is confidently anticipated.
More than any other, Dyson is the miners’ poet,
and his verse swings with the pick, has the clash
of hammer on drill. Post-free from Bulletin
office, ss. 6d. *

COOLALTA
CAMEO
An odour as of violets, which renders
drowsy the atmosphere of Collins-street, Melb.,
at Saturday “ Block ” time, has been a source of
much puzzle lately. The secret is a new lady’s
cordial, which is simply gin flavored with orris
root. This revelation knocks the basis out of
much amatory poesy.
* * * »

day. The daily papers report that the marble
pedestal is 22ft. high, and the statue of the by-
gone mediocrity is 15ft. high and weighs four tons,
while two bronze allegorical figures attached weigh

• * * *
Just before the Maoriland election, one
candidate was openly accused of cruelty to his
wife, and a hurried meeting of his supporters was
held to consider how to refute the calumny. Then
a committee of about 120 lady electors sat in
secret, and cross-examined the candidate’s wife as
to his habits, customs, morals, and general pro-
ceedings. Also, it is alleged, on bad authority,
that they inspected the lady carefully all over in
search of footmarks and welts. Finally, they
reported the charge to be wholly groundless, and
all was well once more.
* • * *
Melb. Social Purity Society lately raised its
periodical wail about that tender, misguided bud,
the local flower-girl. This time, however, it re-
ceived a bad shock. One interesting child in short
frocks and with flying hair proved to be 23 years
of age, married, and the lawful mother of two
bounding infants. And the society was just ask-
ing for legislation to compel her parents to keep
her off the cruel streets, and to send her to bed
every night at eight.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —What think you of an
undertaker’s hearse being seized on the way to the
cemetery for debt ? This happened in a far West
town not so long ago.—D.S.

paper promulgates
the idea that “ main-

Darlinghurst, on the
debtors’ side of the
gaol. That is not so.
The “ wife-starver ”
is shaved,shorn, and
dressed like the worst
criminal in gaol. The
warders know noth-
ing of a m a n’s
offence, the uniform
making all equal.
The only advantage
a wife-starver has is
that he cannot be re-
moved from Darling-
hurst to any other
gaol, because he may
at any moment be

The grievances
of Melbourne tram-
employes smoulder
continuously. Long
hours and short pay
excite universal dis-
content. The trams
have been working
long enough now to
supply the older
hands with samples
of the quaint diseases
that lack the nerves
and joints of the
man who spends half
his time on a jolting
car. Varicose veins
afflict a large percen-
tage of the conduc-
tors. “ Look here,”
said a conductor to
the writer, the other
day, producing a bag
of flour from under
the seat, ‘‘ I have to
carry this about with
me now. Many of
our fellows have to
do the same. When
the veins get very
bad, we dab on a
handful. ” And flour’s
going up, too.

* * * *
Just about the time when Staughton,
M.L.A., drew a scornful distinction between the
Vic. Liberal parties and “ respectable people,”
Mrs. Staughton entertained the 400 leaders of
“ respectability ” in honor of Miss Jessie Staugh-
ton’s formal introduction to their mystic circle.
An instructive gathering. The crowd didn’t in-
clude a single believer in one-man-one-vote nor a
solitary supporter of land-taxation, but they all
agreed in thinking that the voice of the (best)
people is the voice of God. It realised the
Staugh tonic conception of what a Liberal party
ought to be, and The Bulletin herewith chal-
lenges the Victorian Labor members to organise
anything like it.
Whatever precautions may be taken to
prevent diseased animals from conversion into
human food, the public has small show of getting
its breakfast sausage free from germs so long as
butchers pursue their present practice of buying
their wrapping-paper from all and sundry. The
writer recently saw a beef-retailer hand sixpence
to a small girl in return for a considerable bundle
of old newspapers, some of which had the muddy
marks of hob-nailed boots upon them. The man
of meat made no inquiry, and took the dirty foot-
prints as a matter of course. It suggests a sub-
ject for a poem : “Footprints on the Meat.”
* * * *
Again the hapless Boulia (Q.) climate.
The winter just passed was exceptionally bitter,
and now —seven days in succession the ther-
mometer rises to about 110' in the shade. Also,
some of the local stations have had no rain for two
years, and the chief Chinaman hung himself lately
in despair.
* * * *
Man caught coming out of a Sydney pub.,
on a Sunday, with a bottle of whisky
sticking out of his pocket, and accusing
constable swore it wasn’t there when he
went in: “Yer Worship, the bottle was
left over at the stables, across the road,
the night before, for me, and I forgot to
take it home. I came for it on the Sun-
day morning, and then went over to the
pub. to see a sick horse.”!!! Beak
Isaacs said it was the most extraordinary
thing he had ever heard that “a man
like Brown should forget a bottle of
whisky on Saturday night, remember it
on Sunday morning, get it first, and go
straight over to the pub. with it —in his
pocket.” But the lawyer was “great,”
and the witnesses were many, and
“down went M‘Ginty ” in the person of
the constable. Verdict for the sick horse.
The Adelaide hospital trouble attained its
second birthday recently and is still going strong.
The latest contribution to the tale of woe is a
pamphlet by Professor Watson, in which he de-
nounces the alleged ignorance and clumsiness of
the new doctors. Said pamphlet is brightened
and lit up and illuminated, so to speak, with
raw-meat pictures of operations said to have
been performed by the new staff. To some out-
siders these sketches are merely slabs of rather
awkward-looking flesh mostly upside-down, but
the writer seems to imply that they are serious
evidence against the present management, and he
whoops accordingly. Meanwhile, the public, among
whom the dreadful thing has been extensively cir-
culated, is inclined to kick. When a medico, in pur-
suance of his own private grudge, spreads this sort of

document all over the city, and any man is liable to
come home at night and find the fire out and the
baby black in the face and his wife in hysterics
because she has been scared by the sudden arrival
of a tract containing sketches of “ innards,” it
seems to be carrying the thing a little too far.
* * * *
The Adelaide hospital row has burst up
the Adelaide medical school, and turned it into a
mere nursery appendage to Melbourne Univer-
sity. For the future, the Adelaide school will
only teach the first two years’ work of the Mel-
bourne course, and then export its budding Galens
to the Victorian school.

If a teacher of cycling can give evidence in
a Melb. court without having to divulge his pre-
cious name, how much privilege would the Bench
allow to, say, a professor of the banjo or concer-
tina ? He, probably, would be permitted to
whisper his narrative from behind a screen. Or
impart it downwards through a funnel from a hole
in the roof.

Another strange instance of predatory
somnambulism is recorded ; this time from Queens-
land. A highly-respected lady-resident, long
suspected of kleptomania, it is discovered, has
been asleep while operating on the premises of her
acquaintances. Whether her efforts were the
outcome of hypnotic suggestion or not isn’t yet
stated.

* * * *
The Glenbrook (N.S.W.) murderer, who-
ever he might be, seems to have had ideas on the
matter of economy. At least, the theory is that
he made his victims dig their own graves, the
deceased-that-was-to-be pegging away under the
impression that it was a shaft, not. a tomb, and
that he was looking for gold. Then, when the
hole was deep enough, there was a bang and a
corpse and the thing was done. There was no
weary grave-digging afterwards with the un-
pleasant deceased fixing the digger with his glassy
eye all the while; no heat and worry and perspira-
tion ; no hunting for new spots when the grave
bottomed on hopeless rock before it got a foot

DREAMS.
When to you come pleasant dreams,
And the dream-scape rosy seems,
Be you prince or be you peasant,
Thank the Lord that they are pleasant.
But, when dreams are far from pleasant,
Be you prince or be you peasant,
And the dream-scape sable seems,
Thank the Lord they are but dreams.

« * * *
Since the discovery of the two bodies at
Glenbrook, it would seem, from the statement of
the oldest inhabitant (as set forth in the evening
papers), that Butler has lately been seen in almost
every part of Australia, and that, in each case, he
was along with a mate who has since gone a-miss-
ing. Every man who hasn’t been seen lately is
now understood to have been buried by Butler,
and there is a general desire to dig up all N.S. W.
in search of the victim. In most cases the oldest
inhabitant recognised Butler by his portrait in the
Telesraph, which made him look just a shade
less villainous than the delegates to the Bathurst
Convention. Meanwhile, if anybody at all
resembling him is arrested and brought to Sydney,
he will have a bad time, for the 1827 people who
were nearly going out prospecting with Butler, or
any other stranger, are excited enough to swear to
anybody, and everybody that anybody has nearly
gone prospecting with anywhere in Australia at
any time in the last three years or more, is Butler
just now, to all intents and purposes.
* * * *
These graves in the Blue Mountains empha-
sise the fact that the great stumbling-block toinart-
istic homicide is the body. What is urgently needed
by the murdering fraternity is a handy chemical
compound which will reduce the human frame to
a pound or two of dust and reject the three-fourths
of water which helps to compose the bodily avoir-
dupois. When this is accomplished, it will only
be necessary to pour the latter down the sink, and
the dust can bo jammed into a tin, and shied over
the fence as one goes to the picnic.
» * * *
Where the tragedy is there the wild-eyed,
long-haired, long-nailed man with the rapt ex-
pression and the book under his arm is gathered
together. The Glenbrook mystery was hardly
two days old before prophetic-looking individuals
began to haunt the detective office and shako an
impressive claw in the air, and offer to go into
a trance and reveal everything. There was a
reward of .£lOO awaiting the person who located
Lee Weller’s remains, and the seer witli the far-
away expression in his eye had only to dis-
cover .him and earn the money, but this
simple expedient never occurred to the
tribe. They preferred to argue the matter
with unsympathetic policemen, and try to
get hot, cross, aggravated sergeants to listen while
they told about the trance they intended going
into, and the things they meant to see when they
got into it, and the body they proposed to find in
the gully that they were going to locate, provided
someone would only listen to them. There are
twenty or thirty infallible advertising prophets in
Sydney alone, and not one of them seems to have
done anything towards earning that .£lOO re-
ward and the big advt. that would have gone
along with it. Trie prophets are a dreamy, un-
business-like crowd.

Tasmania has but
one village - settlement
experiment, started a
couple of years since by
a committee of ladies
with Parliamentary in-
fluence. By various de-
vices—one being a “bird”
ball, for which birds were
slaughtered and stuffed
to decorate the costumes
of the promoters —the
ladies have raised £4OO
from the general public.
Parliament has planked
down in all =£Boo. Legis-
lative Councillors profess
to deprecate the “spoon-
feeding ” of the five or
six families comprising
the’Settlement, but agreed
to the latest grant of
.£2OO, because the bene-
volent ladies aforesaid
assured them that their
bantling was “ such a
little one.” The settle-
ment is stuck on the
bleakest, barrenest, most
God-forsaken spot in the
country, and is generally
admitted, in and out of
Parliament, as being
utterly incapable of pro-
ducing a crop of anything
barring bricks or suicides.
Failure being therefore
assured, Fatman displays
commendable readiness
in the matter of Parlia-
mentary assistance.
Meanwhile, thousands of
acres of magnificent agri-
cultural land are kept
locked up pending the
arrival of the incre-
ment.
A few years ago a party of miners, some
miles from Maryborough (Vic.), made a hair-
raising discovery. They were sinking in what
they thought was virgin ground when they broke
into an old drive —or rather the bottom of their
shaft dropped out, and they fell into it with a
sudden bump and curse. When they gathered
themselves up and looked round they found they
had company there—seven or eight dead men,
mostly bones. One theory is that their shaft fell
in on them an age or two back, and they weren’t
missed. Another is that an adjacent old-time
shanty-keeper hocussed them to death, robbed
them, buried them and then filled up the shaft,
and they weren’t missed. Either way they were
not missed, for there was no record of their disap-
pearance. After all, when 3i million people
straggle thinly over two thousand million acres of

• * • *
Varley’s Searchlight is reported to be
dead or dying. It was a desperately pious little
paper, which saw incurable wickedness in every-
body except Varley and his little crowd of saints,
and its whole story was that everybody was Lost.
When the public ascertained, on reading the first
number, that everybody was Lost, interest waned.
Nobody could be more Lost than he was in the
first issue, and the subject was exhausted, and
Varley’s mission was used up. When a paper
damns everybody in one number, it leaves nothing
to say in the next one, except that everybody is
still keeping that way.
A certain Australian Judge, who is rapidly
getting himself disliked, recently opened the
criminal-court at a country town where he was
informed there were no prisoners to be tried,
“ This,” remarked his Honor, “ either proves that
the district possesses an unusually high moral stan-
dard, or else there is nothing in it worth stealing,
or else the police are not active enough to catch
the criminals.” Then the local inspector turned
dark purple with indignation and rose to remon-
strate, but while he was doing a preliminary choke
or two his Honor had departed.

* * * *
Palmists, clairvoyants, and several other
kinds of humbug now ply their callings from door
to door in Sydney and suburbs. By way of in-
troduction they offer hair-dyes, corn eradicators,
etc., to the guileless female who answers the bell.
A versatile pair of battlers (male and female) now
doing the suburbs profess themselves able to turn
their hands to phrenology, thought - reading,
palmistry, clairvoyancy, the manufacture of tooth-
ache cure and love-potions, the discovery of lost
property, and several other methods of gathering
in their daily crust. Yet they are a singularly
seedy-looking couple.
* * * *
A Prahran (Vic.) widow of 50, who an-
nexed one of Tattersall’s smaller sweeps and did
not keep the fact to herself, received three offers
of marriage within the fortnight following the
Cup, two being by letter from utter strangers, who
described themselves as “ possessing a little means,
of loving disposition, and generally considered
handsome.” The wily widow wasn’t taking any.
The handsome snake with a loving disposition
generally elopes with the servant-girl.
* * * •
Illegal burials are a valuable source of
revenue in some Sydney suburban municipalities.
Certain of them have graveyards which have been
officially closed because they are already over-
crowded with dead, and because the germ-infested
cemetery, in thickly-peopled localities, is a, serious
danger to the living. The fine for burial in a
closed cemetery is £ 5, which money goes to the
municipality, and sometimes the revejrue from
this source runs as high as =£2oo a-year. No local
official attempts to prevent burials in a closed
cemetery; the funeral may be conducted as
publicly as you like, and as often. Then, when it
is all over, the authorities hold out a significant
hand for the £5, and hope that you will come along
and plant some more relatives there shortly.

“PAIR GIRLS AND GRAY HORSES.”
(A TOAST.)
To all fair girls ! For the sake of one
Whose bright blue eyes wore awhile my star,
Whoso hair had the rich red gold of the sun
When his kisses fall where the leaf lips are.
To all fair girls ! How the red wine gleams
To the glass’s rim as it gleamed that night
In the jewelled hand of my Dame of Dreams—
Oh ! jewelled fingers so soft and white !
To all fair girls ! Turn your glasses down,
Here’s “Blissful bridals and long to live ! ”
And if I am slighting your eyes of brown,
Oh, gipsies born of the night, forgive !
To all gray horses! Fill up again
For the sake of a gray horse dear to me,
For a foam-fed bit and a snatching rein
And a reaching galloper fast and free.
To all gray horses ! For one steed’s sake
Who has carried me many a journey tall
In the dawn mists dim when the magpies wake,
In the starry haze when the night-dews fall.
To all gray horses ! Now drink you deep,
For red wine ruins no rider’s nerves :
“ Light work and a long, long after-sleep! ”
As each gray horse in the world deserves.
Fair girls and gray horses ! To each his way,
But golden and gray are the loves to hold;
And if gold tresses must turn to gray
Gray horses need never be turned into gold !

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Dec. 15, 1896.
Mr Dear Myee—
The Austral Wheel Race Meeting on the Melb.
Cricket Ground attracted more women this year
than ever, and as a dress-show it is rivalling
Flemington. Most of the frocks, however, wasted
themselves on the desert air, or words to that
effect, for the wearers sat perched and jammed in
the grand-stand all the afternoon, and didn’t have
a chance to promenade. On the final day the
lady-enthusiasts filled the members’ reserve and
the “cow-shed,” and spread themselves all over
the surrounding landscape to such an extent that
they had to he politely “ moved on ” from the
smoking-stand. Every man, woman, and child in
the little village down South is cycling mad ; the
race-winners are heroes, and the woman who
boasts that she knows C. Kellow to nod to is
gazed at with respectful awe. Among the well-
dressed thousands on the stand on the “ Austral ”
day was a little actress in a dress like a wax
matchbox —all stripes and plaids—the betrothed of
a well-known professional cyclist.
It is ordained in the Book of Fate that bicycle
sports are always held in a raging wind, worrying
all the women’s fringes out of curl, giving their
hats a drunken list to leeward, and generally
taking the starch out of their appearance. Except
for that drawback, Sydney Bicycle Club’s races on
Saturday were bright and festive. The new stand
entered unobtrusively into existence without any
formal opening, although there was a general im-
pression that somebody ought to have risen to the
occasion with speeches and champagne. Lady
members should be grateful for the new structure ;
it is an artistic two-storeyed pavilion of red brick,
with stone staircase, leading to a green lawn in
front. Members, on Saturday, seemed to be too
bashful to be the first to tread the grass, and it
was deserted. There are large afternoon-tea-rooms,
with grass frontages, placed at the back of the
stand on both floors, so that one can have a cup of
tea without being huddled into a cellar, as in the
old-style refreshment-room. Staircases to the top-
floor are breezy; on Saturday, those ascending
and descending performed an involuntary serpen-
tine dance with their skirts, and felt glad if they
had coppers or reconstructed deposit-receipts in
their pockets to act as sinkers.
Saturday at Sydney Lyceum saw the first night
of Zola’s Therese ” ; the management evidently
counted on the naughty author as a draw, for his
name was printed on the programmes in large
capitals. It was a ghastly, creepy, crawly per-
formance, the sort of play that makes you feel as
if there were a mouse running about the floor, an
extra-sized tarantula taking his hairy way down
the wall, and a beetle clawing the back of your
neck. The plot is flavored with Hamlet. Kyrle
B. and Mrs. Potter plan to murder Mrs. P.’s
husband, and subsequently marry like Gertrude
and Claudius. Mrs. Hill, as mother-in-law,
doubles the parts of Hamlet and the Ghost; as a
galvanised corpse in a bath-chair who has been
paralysed on suddenly learning her daughter-in-
law’s guilt, she has a rdle horrible enough to be
stuck in an Amurkan burlesque and labelled
funny.” The situations are “real”; so is Kyrle’s
remorse, which is obviously heightened by a tear-
ful regret that he didn’t “do for” the whole cast
while he was about the job.
Mrs. Potter was weirdly Potteresque about the
hair, which was twisted into twin nobs on the nape
of her neck. There was no Worth in the bill.
Ther&se wore only one pretty frock—blue muslin
with an iip-to-date fichu and long sash-ends of

Two conspicuous weddings occurred in Sydney
on the 9th. Miss Gaden’s marriage with Mr. j.
Thompson Brown enveloped St. John’s, Darling-
hurst,%m a high-scented atmosphere of flowers and
perfumery, and a rustling whirl of satin, lace, and
feathers. The knot was tied in a bower of foliage
and lilies, in which the clergyman smiled blandly
like a jack-in-the-green. The bridegroom is im-
ported from Scotland, and is taking his bride fora
tour around Australia in the congenial mosquito
season before settling down.
Wedding No. 2 was more uncommon, being a
Jewish ceremony, which implies standing under a
gorgeous velvet canopy like a four-poster, held aloft
by four supporters, smashing a wine-glass, and
being united to a man with his hat on. Miss Rosa
Gotthelf was married to Mr. E. Hallenstein at the
Grand Synagogue, all Potts Point being assembled
as lookers-on.
A suburban church essayed to raise some neces-
sary coin last week by a concert which worked out
on highly original lines. Tableaux—strictly proper
groups, of course—-of a semi-religious nature were
arranged, and suitable music was to be played
while the pictures were showing. But they turned
on a worldly young man at the piano, and the
reverend showman looked pained when for appro-
priate tunes to the first “ tablow,” “ Faith, Hope,,
and Charity,” the performer hammered out, “I
believe it, for my mother told me so,” and “ Could
X only back a winner,” and ‘‘Please give me a
P™ny. ” Then the limelight fell on “ The Horrors,
of War After the Battle,” and the secular person
out “ More work for the undertaker.” All
the “tablow” were accompanied in the same
flippant strain, but the promoter of the entertain-
ment felt his hair rise at two girls, sisters of a
legal luminary, who did a turn —and a very clever
turn, too—imitating street-singing boys clad in
ragged shirts and pants, and with bare feet realis-
tically dirt-smudged. Next time that Bethel
makes a raid it will probably fall back on the
orthodox Berlin woolwork scramble or “cake
fair. ”
Colored dinners are now the Nicest form of
hospitality. A “ pink ” bean-feast means candle-
shades, flowers, and silk fixings all of rosy hue, with
lots of cochineal in the cookery. The vermilion
insect is innocuous, so anything in that tint is all
right. “Yellow” repasts are shaded in saffron,
also harmless, though physicky to taste. But a
“ green ” banquet must make the guests think
hard and run over in their minds all the dyes cal-
culated to give a verdant hue, from angelica to
arsenic. Green soup may owe its tone to spinach,
but green chops and steaks only suggest decom-
position. And fancy the effect of green lamp-
shades on the complexion ! As tertiary tints and
minor tones are now fashionable, wan, seasick-
looking revellers may be next invited to feast on a
color scheme of verdigris, sulphur-yellow, or hell-
blue.
Badge-wearing is spreading to the S. Pacific.
The lady-principal of a girls’ school in Apia,
Samoa, opened a few years ago by M. E. Jersey,
is visiting Australia and giving facts about the
spread of the good work among the dusky belles.
The poor girls are now deprived of their cigarettes
and asked to join an “ anti-smoking league,”
signifying the same by wearing a white ribbon
badge. The school-marm doesn’t explain how
the badge is fixed on ; perhaps they stick it to
their chests with glue. A Samoan girl looks so
pretty reclining on her mat and smoking her little
bit of twisted tobacco leaf. But the principal,
bred in the soul-stifling atmosphere of middle-
class provincial England, decrees smoking a deadly
sin, and forbids it along with the dances and all
the other island frivolities.
Veils are now being “downed” by some doc-
tors who claim to have discovered death-dealing
germs in the few inches of net that look so fragile
and are rather warmer on the face than flannel.
If a man wants to find out what discomfort a
woman will suffer for the sake of keeping her hair
tidy, let him tie a bit of gossamer round his face
and wear it down town on a hot day. Veils are
one of the small tortures of life. They want so
much fixing. I know a girl who always gives her-
self an hour to put her veil right, and then some-
times the spots get wrong and a large black dab
on the eye makes her look as if she had been in a
street fight, or a patch in front of her smile blackens
a front tooth.
The newly-announced Govt. House engagement
of Captain Ferguson to the Hon Margaret Brand
makes his Ex. father-in-law elect to -£ 14,000 per
an. Moral for a Governor with marriageable
daughters : Always have opulent aides.
From Kalgoorlie I hear of an energetic woman
who successfully can vassed for the recent Mayoral
election in a highly novel way. H. G. Parsons,
the winning candidate, employed a hugely fat
black-gin dressed in bloomers of sacking canvas,
bright green blouse, yellow sash, and vast hat with
scarlet feathers, to travel through the town on a
bicycle distributing handbills.
A recent Westralian bridal was blighted owing
to the temporary disappearance of the groom. He
had a “ touch of the sun,” and in his delirium
wandered far afield, so that it took him some days
to find his way back home. His bride had taken
his defection pliilosophically, and economically sold
the wedding shew-bread to glorify another bridal
feast. When the lost one turned up there was no
time to purchase another frosted mound ; and,
besides, it was felt that the groom might have an-
other vanishing attack, and it was hardly worth
while to stake much expensive cake on so unreli-
able a performer. So they celebrated the wedding
with a shilling “Canterbury.”
There is nothing so hideous as a mode too old-
fashioned to be smart and too new to be antique,
so, although the action of “Called Back,” played
by the Rignold co., at the Royal, is dated in the
sixties, the ladies of the cast wisely drape them-
selves to date. Emily Hughes, as Pauline, has
two fashionable frocks—the first, a practicable-
looking white silk crepon, with yellow stripes,
prettily fixed up with flimsy chiffon, and white
hat (a garden-party effect); the other is a pink
satin evening-gown. In her mad moments, she is
■clad in regulation-white flowing draperies; a
stage-heroine never disgraces her “store-clothes”
by lunacy, but always keeps a special gown for
going mad in. Mary Vaughan (Miss Watts-
Phillips) has many changes of raiment; her
prettiest is a pale-blue brocade, with a guinea-a-
yard effect. Rignold, as the oily Macari, has a
picturesque brigandish cloak and a musical Italian
■cadence in his voice, and the Siberian exiles shrink
from the knout and grovel naturally—in their
■dirty old coats they look nearly as ragged as
Domain-loafers, and much cooler. On Monday
night, a budding Lawton in the gallery contri-
buted a gratis whistling-accompaniment to the
■orchestra, which was much appreciated by the
■other gods.
Three newly-wedded couples, whose new-found
bliss doesn’t date back further than last week, all
ran up against each other, much to their mutual
■embarrassment, at the same honeymoon resort a
■day or two ago. All had elaborately announced
per society pars, their departure on a large and
dignified tour, and when they collided about a
half-crown train-ride from the city the situation
was really painful.
The prophesied rise in the matrimonial market
is still booming. This week’s flutter was the
wedding of Miss Pearl Lamb and Mr. Wm. Allen
at St. Mark’s. Points about this ceremony differ-
entiating it from others were no bridesmaids and
many gifts from Govt. House, including some
vice-regal upholstery, millinery and jewelleiy.
Shops are full of Xmas novelties. Many are
the same old novelties, but some are really new.
People give queer presents. I think it’s rather a
broad hint to present a friend with nail-cleaning
apparatus. Yet “ manicure ” sets are popular as
gifts, and more and more elaborate each festive
season ; one is continually meeting elegant little
silver trays containing polisher of “shammy,”
silver box of powder, curly nail-scissors, and
other implements the use of which is
“shrouded in mystery” to plain folk, ac-
customed to do the business with a threepenny
nail-brush and a cake of brown Windsor.
A hint to fan-purchasers: fans are dwindling
to the tiny size affected by our grandmothers ; at
’Ome fashionables now flutter diminutive makes,
much bespangled. A snake-skin leather belt is
now considered a toney gift. If any young man
wishes to delight his best girl with an offering of
gloves, they should be of white kid with black
stitchings.
A ghoulish yarn from Bananaland. Townsman
died and undertaker was called in. Sorrowing
family ordered an expensive oaken, silver-plated
coffin. Subsequent to funeral bereaved widow
called on undertaker and explained that deceased
hadn’t left enough to pay for his interment. So
the coffin-seller arose by night and went to the
cemetery with two of his men, and exhumed the
body, casting it back into the grave minus the
shell, which he took home with him. This is not
an encore verse for “ More Work for the Under-
taker,” although it reads like one.
On a large passenger-boat, a few days out, a
confused man wandered at dusk into the wrong
cabin, and was bewildered to hear a faint voice
from the upper berth say “ I’m so glad you’ve
come. You’re later than usual this evening,
aren’t you ? ” He stammered something, and a
sea-sick old fright popped up her head, gave a
shrill scream, and fainted with horror at seeing a
man in her cabin. Next day the trespasser apolo-
gised for everything he hadn’t done, and the old
maid explained that she was so nervous that a
steward came in every evening to screw down the
dead-light for her. “ But why scream at me more
than at him?” the man asked after thinking hard
for a few moments. “Oh, it’s quite different,”
replied the old lady, tossing her head snortfully,
“ don’t you see, he’s only the steward.”
When Nice Australians die they go to South
Kensington. They may not be actually corpsed
when they go there, but, as J. C. Neild bitterly
remarks, they might as well be dead for all the
notice they attract. The nooent waterspout of
oblivion overwhelms them. So in order to feel
less blotted out they make up parties of their
fellow-cliquers on the mail-boat, and put on
their best clothes to go to the Imperial In-
stitute, where they sit in the gardens with a
far-off gaze, not having yet got the ocean out of
their eyes. They look exactly like the people
you’d avoid on the deck of a liner; and they say
“Oh, how pretty ! ” when the colored lights are
switched on, and stroll into the galleries of the
unuseful pile of buildings, and note with royal
thrills murky shadows alleged to be photos.. of
royalties on china labelled “Specimens of photo-
ceramics, taken by H.R.H. the Princess of
Wales,” and observe dusty trophies of “ big game
shot in South Africa by Lord Toodles ” ; and the
lady-reporter from the Antipodes prowls around
and puts them all into the British Australasian
as “ prominent Australians,” along with ex-
colonial bishops, governors, and other magnates.
And that pleases them mightily. But the view
of things antipodean exchanged by bishopesses,
governoresses, and so on when they foregather in
a South Kensington boudoir, no one knoweth.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“ Coolgardie brides, who have only experienced
winter-time over there, have hitherto written
home to Victoria that the climate was ‘heavenly.’
Now they are in the first swelter of summer, and
their letters are less pleasant reading, what with
the costliness of laundry-work, the plague of field-
mice, blue-bottle flies, and, worst of all, the willy-
willies.
“ A girl who arrived there to be married saw
her first sand-storm during the marriage cere-
mony. The building filled with red dust; the
bridegroom and best man rushed to close the doors
and windows, and the parson changed the strain
of his prayers to a frantic petition for the safety of
the roof of his new church. The bride was
inclined to talk of ill omens until they cheered her
up with the news that it was quite ordinary W. A.
weather. Her forebodings returned in full force
when she came out of church, and saw that, of her
neat home, which had been finished all but the
windows, only the stumps remained.
“ The latest fad of Vic. Govt. House ladies is
swimming, and they go in for this amusement
with vigour in the early morn at Kenny’s Baths,
where a couple of bathing-boxes are set apart for
them. Lady B. arrays herself in a vivid scarlet
serge bathing-dress elaborated with white braid,
but a breathless public learns that Mrs. Freeman-
Thomas depends on the bathing-house proprietors
for her suit, and encases herself in the unlovely
blue serge garment in which countless others have
probably disported themselves. His Ex., as sas-
siety is interested to hear, gravely mops up his
ample vice-regal person with the towels served out
by the establishment and struggles into his vest-
ments entirely unaided.
“ The snappiest cycle girl in Melb. is a fair un-
known who wears an exquisite white cloth habit
with silver buttons and white cap, and who
carries, even against fearful odds, a white moire
parasol. The way she manages to steer her course
through the city traffic with one hand, bearing
aloft her shade with the other, her fair face be-
wreathed in smiles, baffles mere prose description
and turns all other cycle girls jaundiced with
jealousy.

A Tasmanian correspondent writes :
“Launceston went in for a dizzy cycle fete
lately. It passed off very well. The scene
was the little square, somewhere about the
average size of a boomster’s garden at Toorak—
the little square with its lawns and fountain, and
with the tiger and bear scraping themselves
against the iron bars of their shabby domains,
where hundreds of white rats and black rats
scramble in and out of their weatherboard villa.
In this time-honored, unimproved dot of a public
garden some 2000 people contrived to congregate
(at great danger to life and limb owing to numerous
reckless cyclists and cyclistes) and had a real good
time as good times go in Tasmania. The only
accidents comprised a Chinaman knocked down
by a bike and a lady’s eye blackened by the
cork of an exploding hop-beer bottle. We are not
a murderous crowd in Tasmania.
Yours affectionately,
CLEO.
The Bulletin’s Xmas number this year
was the largest and best yet issued, and it also
put up the largest circulation record to date.
For several years past the supply has given out on
the morning of publication. This time, by very
largely increasing the supply, a special effort has
been made to satisfy all demands. A very few
copies are still available.
The coming Tasmanian elections will, if
the local pious party can manage it, turn largely
on the question of Tattersall’s sweeps. The good
men of Vandemonia hold that Tattersall’s inno-
cent little gamble is brother to Sodom and Gom-
morrah, and they will ask the electors to cast out
Adams before he brings the divine wrath down on
their little insular fly-speck.
Arthur Streeton’s exhibition of paintings
in Melbourne threatens to excite a rush of Sydney
artists with goods for sale. The local gallery
trustees have secured “The Purple Moon’s Trans-
parent Light ” for =£lso, little Dr. Springthorpe
invested .£5O in “On the River” (what wouldn’t
he give for a soulful treatment of “On the Bike”!)
and other Streeton pictures have found purchasers.
The collection, which numbers about forty charac-
teristic specimens of the visitor’s charming work,
is exhibited at 88 Elizabeth-street.
A considerable circle of friends lament the
loss of Benjamin Dennison, actuary and secretary
to the Mutual Life of New York in Australia.
Mr. Dennison was a man of rare ability, without
a trace of assumption, and one of the best of good
fellows to those who knew and appreciated his
worth. He was a pupil of Ralph Price Hardy,
the distinguished actuary, and came to Australia
some fifteen years ago in the service of the Colonial
Mutual. Soon after Major Rennie established
the Australian department of his company Mr.
Dennison joined his staff, where he remained
until his death. He had long suffered from lung
disease, and passed away peacefully early Sunday
morning.
How is the Bulli Colliery Fund getting on ?
When last heard of, there were some .£30,000 to
credit, and probable claims worth, perhaps,
.£5OOO, leaving a prospective balance of possibly
=625,000, and a tidy billet for somebody to mind
it. What’s the matter with erecting the Bulli
colliery balance, after probable claims have been
actuarially provided for, into a National Mining
Relief Fund, and helping the day-to-day sufferers,
like those at Stockton? Your average trustee
seems so anxious to do business on the best mathe-
matical principles, and to keep his office, and his
secretary, and his office - expenses afloat as
long as possible, that those for whom the
public subscribed the money, those who should be
the recipients of charity on the instant, when they
want it, are killed off, or learn to provide for
themselves, before the Board has done enquiring
into their cases. Public money for charitable re-
lief is subscribed to be spent, not to be kept on
hand and put out at interest, and the sooner the
average trustee gets this into his head the better.
Time deals gently with the woman who
uses “Babeskin” soap. It softly removes the
tell-tale lines of the years, and renews the delicate
bloom of youth. One shilling packages of two
cakes. All chemists.*

Louis Becke’s latest photo, from London
reweals that once gaunt and piratical-looking
genius as a prosperous-looking personage with a
tendency towards a double chin.
Professor William Miller, 50 years old on
Dec. 13, is one of the very few athletes who meet
their jubilee trim of figure, virile and vigorous.
Most of them by that time are pot-shaped, and
beer-bloated, and bung generally.
J.P. : “ Vhat vhas you plead? Troonk
aber not troonk ! Dot vhas do say, kildy aber
notkildy?” Accused: “Guilty.” J.P. : “ Vined
abound, aber der rising ov der goord.” For of
