N.S.W. Labor Bureau boss Joseph Creer
merely smiles at the numerous press attacks on
his capacity. But he is driven to the verge of in-
sanity, when inspecting relief-works, by his con-
stituents’habit of whistling “Pretty Joey” till
the locality seems a cockatoo training-school.
Lawyer Geake, the absconding embezzler
who was landed back at Smellbourne last week, is
said to have been guilty of most exemplary conduct
on his return journey. Geake was a whale for divine
service, and, under police supervision, he led pas-
sengers and crew in prayer and thanksgiving.
Poor Lee Weller, on his last birthday,
wrote his autograph in a friend’s birthday-book in
front of the verse : “ We walk by faith, not by
sight.” Then he seems to have walked by faith
after his mate into the gully at Glenbrook, and by
not keeping that energetic prospector in sight he
was dropped with a gun from behind.
Melb.: “A life-size statue, badly muti-
lated or dilapidated, apparently of the public-
spirited and benevolent Ormond, adorns the yard
of a firm of gravestone choppers, and seems to
have been taken down froin somewhere. May I
suggest that it could profitably be traded off on
some English provincial town as the Prince of
Wales?”
Victoria’s prize fat woman —she weighed
24 stone —died peacefully a little while ago at
Dunolly, aged GG. The local paper proudly men-
tions that the upper part of her arm was a yard in
circumference. The illness of this substantial
lady commenced with a sudden fainting-fit, and
eleven friends with difficulty got her to bed, which
she never again quitted.
Deakin has a marvellous memory for
figures. His three hours and 20 minutes’ speech
on the Water Commission’s report —in all, accord-
ing to the Hansakd calculation, he spoke 28,200
words —was delivered from only a few scrappy
notes, roughly prepared. It was full of figures,
but he seemed to carry them nearly all in his head.
He smashed up the Water Commission, and did it
easily, though, as that body consisted of the most
mildewed and muddle-headed set of duffers that
could have been picked from the Assembly, the
feat was comparatively easy for Deakin.

more pwofitable to allow the tails to gwow?”

Matthew “Mercantile” Davies is said to
have been the hidden wire-puller who conceived
and engineered the translation of Vic. barristers
and Vic. attorneys into amalgams. Attorneys
were barred from the Supreme Court bench, the
only gate thereto being the Bar. Matthew’s
ambition was a judgeship, therefore the amalga-
mation. Had not the boom burst when it did,
Matthew would now likely have been sitting in
the seat of Johnnie Madden, and he may get there
yet, thanks to gall and pertinacity.

the hapless Q.N.B. isthe appointment of Norman to
the Agent-Generalship. That popular old warrior
possesses some 63 shares (uncalled capital, .£126)
in the Q.N.8., and it is openly averred in the
Bananaland journals that the spectacle of the
ex-Govemor with his 63 shares tied round his
neck, lecturing Bull on the glories of Bananaland,
will inspire a confidence, etc., etc., etc.
The curse of Butler ! Butler No. 1, the
notorious alleged murderer from Maoriland, was
recently awarded a long sentence in Melbourne.
Butler No. 2 was the Sydney divorce-case male-
factor. Butler No. 3 is badly wanted over the
Glenbrook (N.S.W.) murders. Yet the Sydney
and Mel*>. Directories only show one column of
Butlers against three solid pages of Smith, and it
is only once in a decade that any great criminal is
named Smith.

King O’Malley, M.P. (S.A.), the much-
advertised ’Murkan lightning streak insurance
drummer, recently got a verdict' against the wart-
cure man, who threatened to burst him up and
aim him out of Parliament. The S. A. public will
now be very glad to have a prolonged rest from
both parties. A Society for Being Tired of
O’Malley would be a huge success in Adelaide just
now.
A jamful. address by the officers of the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society to Leslie
J. Park, on his recent appointment as general
manager, fetched a historic reply from the recipi-
ent. Mr. Park’s burst of thanks closed with a
quotation from Richelieu —“ What was my art —
Genius some say, soma fortune—Witchcraft some
—Not so—my art was justice.” The Bulletin is
glad to think that the genial city gent, in the
modern bell-topper disclaimed all pretensions to
Witchcraft.
VIEW OF FIVE DOCK BY NIGHT.
“Five Dock streets arc still in darkness, except on those nights when a
friendly moon hangs her lamp above. Some time ago, it will be remembered,
the Gas Company, in consequence of heavy arrears of rates, sent some of their
men to the borough and cut off the supply of gas to the public lights. The
aldermen met and considered the position, but could do nothing. The Gas
Company desired payment, and the council had not the necessary funds to
meet the demand. A statement of account was asked for by the council, and
furnished by the Gas Company. Then the Mayor (Alderman J. Croker) and
Aldermen Rodd and Ghest waited upon the company, and a lengthy discus-
sion ensued, but in the absence of payment of the amount the company declined
to restore the supply of gas.”
In the earliest days of Melb., Sam Hordern,
the founder of the Hordern family, ran a cooperage
on the site of the present General Post-Office.
The notorious
Gipsy Smith
(bushranger, &c.)
was arrested on
his arrival in Aus-
tralia in the old
days by that clever
ex-convict, Detec-
tive . The
charge was a seri-
ous one, both men
were armed, and
things looked like
a fight, when Gip-
sy, looking hard
at his captor, said:
“Good Lord,
Jack, is this how
we meet after
twenty years ? It
is a d— — hard
world this.” And
so it was. Both
men had worked
together in Eng-
land and had
“ been sent out ”
for the one job,
and after twenty
years thus they
met. Gipsy got a
long sentence, but
he was true to his
word and never
told what he knew
about his old
friend the detec-
tive.
N. : “ Bis-
marck’s career has
been an unbroken
line of frauds, end-
ing with one last
month. Yet he is the clergy’s idol, because he
says religion inspired him all through.”
Baronet Read, ex-Governor of Darling-
hurst prison, successfully resists all pressure to
return to his ancestral halls, and whiles away his
pensioned leisure within a stone’s throw of his
late official residence. Sir John is a County Clare
baronet, with an early-developed liking for police
duty. He retired from the English police force
with a pension, and secured another pension, of
nearly =6400 a year, from N.S. Wales when he
handed over his charge of the gaol. The baronet’s
son, another N.S.W. pensioner, is also reluctant
to quit Australia, but when the title of tenth
baronet falls to him he is likely to re-gild his crest
and return to his devoted barony.
Poor Clark Irving used to describe ex-
Judge Windeyer’s address to the Sydney I ni-
versity electors at his first contest. The Judge
tramped up and down the platform, stormed,
roared, wept, and generally aroused the feelings
of the electoral undergraduates. “ I heard, said
barrister Irving, “all the noises in nature, from
the barn-yard to the desert.”
The Dean Curse was in full blast the other
day at Cooma. Meagher, who lost his practice
and his seat in Parliament through it, was putting
up at the local hotel, having come to inflict his
dreary lecture on the Cooma population. And
John Asbury, Mrs. Seymour’s long-lost husband,
was putting up the same day at the lock-up oppo-
site on a charge of helpless old age and beggary.
Melb. Undertaker Sleight, who was the
other day undertaken himself, was jovial to the
end. Only a week before he died he met a well-
known solicitor, also passing through the Valley,
and offered to bet a funeral against the drawing
up of a will, that he would last the longest. The
man of law won, and now it dawns on him that
the dead undertaker can’t bury him, so the bet is
irrecoverable.
Poor Jimmy Mirams’ latest gleam of pros-
perity was quickly extinguished. The ex-Premiei
Permanent secretary was hardly started in hi*
Collins-street stationery shop when the Argus
people came down on him with a thud for unpaid
law costs in his abortive libel-suit. Then a line in
the daily papers announced that the little man’s
estate was compulsorily sequestrated, and Mirams
and his umbrella go down once more in the deep
commercial waters.
Hall-Jones, the new M.L. Minister for
Works, was a serious incubus on his party at the
recent election. He is a stodgy, lifeless, conscien-
tious orator, whose speeches are a waste of figure*
droned out in the voice of a wind sighing in a
valley of dry bones. Hall-Jones has a good, hard-
working brain, but when he is put up to charm
the electors, and proceeds to blow dry facts across
the plains of Canterbury, he is a heavy-weight for
any Ministry to carry.
Ex-Mayoress Yates, of Onehunga (M.L.),
has been defeated for the Mayoralty by 133 votes.
Mrs. Yates’ regime was chiefly noted for the long
and determined strike of the poundkeepet and the
inferior quality of the illumination in Onehunga’s
solitary lamp. Moreover, all the louts of Auck-
land and Onehunga used to roll up at council-
meetings to boo-hoo and throw road-metal on the
roof, and this kind of notoriety made Onehunga
grow tired of lady Mayors.
Dr. Balls-Headley, Melbourne s doss
women’s doctor, has been selected to write the
chapter on the causation of the special diseases of
women for the great “English System of Medi-
cine,” now issuing. The doctor, who is chief
surgeon of Melb. Woman’s Hospital, lately pub-
lished a treatise on the evolution of the diseases
of women, which has shaken up the world of
medicine pretty considerably, and badly punctured
many old medical traditions.
Dr. C. J. Martin, who has just resigned
the demonstratorship of physiology at Sydney
University to become lecturer thereon at Melb.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Under the head of “ State- Aided Insol-
vencies ” somebody raises a reasonable question in
the Melb. press. Ought not a civil-servant who,
in the face of a fixed salary, files a schedule which
deserves no sympathy, to be turned out of his
billet to make way for another fellow who won t
systematically bilk the shop-
keepers? Every salaried ser-
vant who pays his way is taxed
to help supply the deficiencies
of those who don’t. That’s
an unavoidable law of retail
trade which the Government
could soften somewhat by
checking insolvency among its
employes.
A HARD CASE.
(Sunday morning in Pitt-street, as Christian
Endeavorer passes on.)
BILLY (log.): “Gumbo, what did him parson
pfeller say?”
CUMBO: “Him say ‘ Cumbo, my brudder, you born
in sin, ’but I say him no fear; Cumbo born
in Mullengudgery. ”
An exchange announces that R. D. Meagher
will oppose Crick in West Macquarie at next
general election.
On the Manuka (M.L.) electoral roll one
woman-voter has registered herself : “ Occupa-
tion : domestic slave.”
A Melb. Tory
The Socialists’ creed is formulated in the shibboleth,
“ each man according to his needs,” whereas in'the main
the present rule is “ each according to his deeds."
In 71 years (1825-1895) there have been
only three when there were no hangings in New
South Wales. The spreading gallows tree is a
busy institution in Botany Bay.
The “ Freetrade League of Victoria ” states
that its total receipts for the last two years have
been .£lO4 9s. lOd. The great Freetrade revival
doesn’t pan out much in solid cash, anyhow.
A candidate for a West Coast (M.L.)
County Council announced that he was in favor
of “ the abolition of the duty on smoked fish, pro-
hibition of blow-flies, free coal to settlers on the
Nine-mile Beach, and a supply of apple dumplings
for stray dogs.”
London Morning Post wonders how
alleged Freetrade Premier Reid can attitudinise
as an anti-Chinese legislator—who, by that atti-
tude, proclaims himself a Protectionist. The Post
doesn’t know what curious two-legged comedians
there are in N.S.W.
They let the Gentleman One down very
easily in Holy Adelaide. The other day “ the
case of , a practitioner,” was called ; ——, a
Cractitioner, having adhered to money that didn’t
elong to him. Ordered to be struck off the rolls
if he didn’t pay up in a given time ; if he does re-
fund the money, will remain a practitioner as
usual.
N.S.W. Premier Reid went on his mission
to Queensland, and, for over a week, the papers
reported how he was steadily eating and drinking
his way over the province in the cause of Federa-
tion. Now an abrupt telegram reports that
Queensland is going to leave things just as they
are. Considering all the good victuals that have
been put into the business, this is by no means
the sort of thing-Australia looked for.
It was alleged the other day, in Sydney
Insolvency Court, that a bankrupt had for six
years occupied a house in Woolloomooloo, rented
at 50s. a week. In 1890, six months after he began
to occupy the property, it was resumed by Govern-
ment for some purpose or other, and as no one
ever demanded the rent thereafter, insolvent natu-
rally didn’t pay any. Apparently, the N.S.W.
Government is a very poor rent-collector.
Ash, law-partner of Premier Kingston, is
the most stubborn antagonist of the S.A. Govt, in
all attempts to ameliorate the condition of the
workers. Recently Kingston spoke of “Ash’s
ingenious objections to everything designed to
benefit humanity,” described him as “ the incarna-
tion of selfishness,” and accused him of “ putting
Private Greed before public good.” Yet the pair
join amicably together to take the fees of clients.
Ash the other day had one poor unfortunate wit-
ness under examination for 10 hours.
According to T. Davis, M.P., there are
240 alleged “ clubs ” in and around Sydney, where
the “ flowing bowl ” flows unrestrained
every Sunday. This writer knows of at least
three clubs on the books of which his name
appears as a “ registered member.” He has
never been in two of them more than once
—in fact, he couldn’t go straightaway to
one of those two—and has been only three
times in the third, which he “joined’’six
months or so ago. He paid no entrance
or other fee, but signed his “registered
membership ” in a book kept for that pur-
pose, after having been duly vouched for as
“straight” by his introducer. The only
drawback to these “clubs,” from a Sunday
“professional ” point of view, is that every
now and again the alleged proprietor gets
harassed by the police, and the “club”
changes its address, so that, unless a de-
sultory “member” keeps in touch with its
befallings, he may one Sunday walk a couple
of miles to where it was, only to discover
that the locality knows it no more. But
why bother about “clubs” when Sunday
drinks can be got in every other hotel in
Sydney ?
Queensland Minister Tozer reports
that up in the gay and drinky North a
Govt, official has been suspended for six
months, for being in a tangled condition
and galloping through the town with a wo-
man’s bonnet on. They are growing par-
ticular about smadl trifles in the North.
There was a time in that riotous land of
snakes when a Govt, official could gallop
through the town with hardly anything on
save his unblemished character, and no one
dreamt of suspending him. The present
writer remembers an occasion when three
perfectly nude Govt, officers—a P.M., a
sub-inspector of police, and a C.P.S. —were
jumping for drinks in the back yard of a
North Queensland pub., at 2 a.m., but that is too
long a story to tell just here.
Now that Duncan Gil-
lies is coming back to Vic-
toria and talks of re-entering
politics, it is worth while re-
minding a short-memoried
feneration what sort of man
)unc.an is. In 1890, when
the boom was just about
bursting, dear old Gillies
brought in a Bill for build-
ing =£14,500,000 worth of
new railways about 800
miles in all—in the already
over-railwayed province.
Then the House, seeing that
railways were going round,
rose unanimously and every
man demanded lines all over
his electorate, and presently
it found itself seriously con-
sidering the construction of
4400 miles of railway with
>£77,000,000 of borrowed
money. And Duncan, to
all appearance, had no
serious objection to going
right on with the whole
stupendous job; but a panic
fell on the Assembly, and it
fled in terror from its own
monstrosity, and the great
scheme died of inflation.
The proposal to reduce
N.S.W. Civil Service pen-
sions 15 per cent., even if it
were square and honest, is a
miserable shuffling of the
trouble on to the morrow.
The Superannuation Fund
amounts to about .£489,000 ;
it ought to be about
£3,000,000. If all the pro-
spective pensions were can-
celled, and the contributions
came in just the same, and
no payments were refunded,
as provided by the late Act,
to officials who have with-
drawn from the fund, and
the existing pensions were
cut down 40 per cent., the
Fund would crowd through.
Or it would crowd through
if present and prospective
pensions were cut down
about 70 per cent. The 15
per cent, reduction is only
a device to make things last
Reid’s time, so that his suc-
cessor will have to find the
£2, 500,000 or do the actual
repudiation.
The Maoriland Chief Justice has decided
that the “tied” public-house system is illegal
according to a very old statute, which prohibits
“ unreasonable restraints on trade.” If the tied
house system can be abolished the great beer
industry will totter on its foundations, and the
Great Australian Thirst will get an amazing
shock.
The legal trades-union of Queensland has
just, so to speak, put another row of spikes on
the gate and extra broken bottles on the wall.
The new rules for admission make it practically
impossible for another toiler, like Pat Real, to
ornament the Bench.
Two of N.S.W. Co-
lonial Secretary Brun-
ker’s sons are in receipt
of Govt, salaries. E.
Brunker is in charge of
the Customs office at
Morpeth, and draws
=£3Bs per year. G. W.
N. Brunker is local man-
ager of the Govt. Savings
Bank, and also draws
£5O per year as secretary
to Maitland Council.
The voice of Fink,
in Vic. Assembly :
If the banks, with their
careful and economical ad-
ministration and skilful man-
agement, could not make a
profit of 1 per cent, the
business could only be car-
ried on by Government at a
great loss.
That strange, wild cachin-
nation in the distance is
supposed to be the voices
of Drury and Longmuir,
the Men that are Dead,
howling at Fink from
another and a better
world. And the hollow
echo from further back
is the cackle of poor old
ex-banker, Jimmy Mun-
ro, in his little wooden
house-agency office,
where he sits and thinks
of his defunct institutions
and wonders where ’e are.
John Haynes pro-
poses in next session of
N. S. W. Parliament to
move for a Royal Com-
mission to inquire into
the current prison sys-
tem. If the Commis-
sion be appointed, it is to
be hoped that it will re-
commend the abolition
wholly of penal servitude,
and the substitution of
industrial servitude. The
dull, brute mind which
condemns a man to purely
afflictive labor while work in any Govt, depart-
ment needs to be done is the material out of which
the irreclaimable criminal is easily manufactured.
The Leg. Council Committee, which is
vainly trying to unravel Adelaide Hospital tangle,
terminates its existence with the prorogation on
Dec. 18, and as, at time of writing, it had disposed
of two witnesses after three weeks’ work, it has a
hopeless task on hand. In all probability it would
take six months of daily sittings to examine all
the parties concerned, and then no one would be
much the wiser. The Hospital squabble has now
got into such a tangle that no one really knows
anything about it.
THERE WERE OTHERS.
“ Well, you needn't get so high and mighty about it — you’re not the
only girl who won’t marry me!”
Poor old John MTntyre, leader of Yic.
Opposition, has only one answer for every new
proposition—that the matter should be postponed
so as to give time for sufficient consideration. If
old John came across one of those glaciers which
flow three inches in a year, his first idea would be
to suggest that it should avoid undue precipita-
tion.
This is the sort of stuff that the dreadful,

Hansard (vide report of proceedings on Wednes-

has discharged the loose expectoration of his
intellectual bowels on my democratic head ” .

The Australian Star on Maoriland elec-
tion : “The country had had enough of Mr.
Sneddon and socialistic experiment, and was
resolved to get back to those principles whereon
the colony had been established.” Anybody who
has heard of the recent downfall of anybody
named Sneddon will please communicate with
this office.
Almost the only point on which the M.L.
Tory party committed itself definitely was that
the urgent need of the country was “ the restora-
tion of confidence and faith in the future as a pre-
liminary to the restoration of general prosperity.”
In short, the Tory party stated boldly and con-
fidently that it was m favor of a beautiful greenish
haze trimmed with giraffe sneeze cut bias.
A Hobart lodge chemist the other day
received a prescription signed by the lodge doctor
—“Port wine, ter die.” When referred to
the nearest pub. to have it filled, the lodge mem-
ber (a Rechabite) actually got indignant, and,
when asked how he expected a chemist to supply
him with a bottle of port and medicines also at
the munificent lodge rate of l£d. per week, fell to
language.
The Seating in Shops Bill was under dis-
cussion in the S.A. Assembly, and the clause
which directs that “suitable” seats shall be pro-
vided for women employees was being talked
about. “ What is a ‘ suitable ’ seat ? ” asked
M‘Donald, M.P., whose past career has not been
very consistent. “Well,” answered Kingston,
“the Treasury bench is a suitable seat for
Ministers, but some members appear to be quite
content with a rail.”
Hot Weather Dyspepsia.
Hot weather dyspepsia is easily recognised by
heaviness and pain in the stomach after meals,
loss of appetite, languidness, bad taste in the
mouth —especially on rising—flatulency in stomach
or bowels, headaches, and constipation. These
symptoms of indigestion indicate that the liver is
torpid ; and in fact in a hot climate one’s liver is
the barometer of health. This is why Warner’s
Safe Cure is popular in Australia. The following
letter from Mr. W. B. Carah, wholesale and retail
tobacconist, 232 Swan st., Richmond, dated Sept.
16, 1896, is one of thousands the vendors of this
medicine can produce; “About twelve months
ago I had a very bad attack of influenza, which
left me with severe pains in my back, terrible
headaches, and not only loss of appetite but also
sleep. I consulted a medical man, who told me
that my liver was very badly affected and my

I had only got through my third bottle when I
was completely restored to perfect health, and
have had no return of the unpleasant symptoms
since. I have also recommended it to a great
many friends with successful results. ”*

Judging by the orders received, this year’s
Xmas Bulletin will circulate over a larger extent
of the earth than any previous issue and will
cover more ground than any other paper ever
published in this hemisphere. It is a monumen-
tal specimen of journalism, and the misguided
person who hasn’t read it has missed something
the like of which he will never see again—until
Xmas, 1897.
The Yic. Government is endeavoring to
push a Usury Bill through Parliament. It pro-
vides that 8 per cent, shall be the maximum inte-
rest on landed security, 10 per cent, on all other
security, and, where no interest is specified in
writing, 6 per cent, shall be the highest rate
allowed by law. Every loan is to be made in full,
the practice of deducting a bonus being absolutely
prohibited. A thick voice can already be heard
in the distance denouncing this as another beastly
interference with the liberty of the subject.
Dear Bulletin, — Be poisoning on W.
Q’sland stations. The food provided for shearers
on some stations consists largely of fermented
jams, damaged flour, rice, sago, and rotten pre-
serves, bought up at auction sales at Brisbane by
station agents at the lowest prices, and sold to
shearers at top prices. Frequently, as a result of
using this unwholesome food, the men become ill
and develop poisoning symptoms. The authorities
invariably jump to the conclusion that the wicked
unionists are to blame, and overlook the proba-
bility of the station itself being a wholesale
poisoner.—C.C. Clermont.
Victoria proposes to reduce the member-
ship of the Assembly from 95 to 80, and of the
Council from 48 to 40. Probably Vic. will be
very sorry for this move later on. It will make
the constituencies larger, therefore contests will
be more expensive, and the Fat Man will have a
better show. As a partial counterpoise, however,
when there are 40 M.’sL.C. instead of 48, there
will at least be a slightly better chance that they
may all fall down in a fit at once, or be struck by
lightning simultaneously, and thus give the
country a much-needed rest.
The S.A. Government’s Land for Closer
Settlement scheme is a fearfully and wonderfully
cumbrous affair. Instead of the Government
obtaining power for the compulsory purchase of
largo estates where land is wanted for settlement,
it is provided that, if an owner has more than
Jl5OOO worth of land, anyone wanting a farm may
select, in his mind’s eye, a piece of the surplus
soil. Then he may arrange with the owner as to
price. The whole land-settlement difficulty lies in
the fact that the big owner won’t sell at a reason-
able figure, or won’t sell at all, and, as the land-
lord can block the negotiation by asking -£l5OO an
acre, the present arrangement doesn’t seem to help
things much. If they agree, the selector can ask
the Government to resume the land at the price,
and the Government will send the matter to the
Land Board to report upon when it is sufficiently
rested. Then, if the report is favorable, Parlia-
mentary sanction will bo asked for, and, when it is
granted, the Government will buy the land for
cash and lease it to the applicant on moderate
terms—provided both parties haven’t died of old
age in the interim, and assuming that all this cir-
cumlocution doesn’t cost much more than twice
the value of the property. The lonesome agricul-
turist who wants to take up a few acres under this
system will need the patience of the immortals.
At one of Stout’s riotous meetings at Wel-
lington (M.L.) a lady elector interrupted the
speaker. Whereupon there rang through the hall
the cry of “Chuck ’er ou-u--u-u-ut! ’' She sub-
sided, therefore she wasn’t chucked. But if woman
is going to take to politics in real earnest she must
grow accustomed to be seized by the collar and
the waistband and scuffled down the front steps
with her hair down and her bonnet over one eye,
when she interrupts a hostile meeting. So far
only one woman has been chucked out in Maori-
land politics, and as an act of courtesy she was
ejected by some members of her own sex. Then,
instead of bombarding the building with road-
metal from outside, which is the orthodox thing
to do, or shoving her umbrella through the glass,
she went away crushed and weeping.
It is a poor, sad, thankless job to run a
strictly party newspaper, and have to prophesy
before each election that the return of your parti-
cular gang of thieves is absolutely certain, and
then to explain afterwards that the awful licking
which ensued is really a “ great moral victory. ”
The last two weeks before M.L. election were
filled with a unanimous whoop from the Tory
press that victory was assured, and there would
be a huge majority, and the two islands would
both be wiped up with Seddon, and all was over
except the drinks and congratulations. It had
said the same thing in ’9O and ’93, but this time
the event was really coming off. Then the disas-
trous thud of the Russell-Stout party was heard
all over Maoriland, and next day all the tired
editors started to explain that it was really a
“moral triumph,” and that they were perfectly
satisfied with the result, and the country was
really yearning for a Russell Ministry, but owing
to various causes, &c., &c., and the beastly cor-
ruption, &c., and the unblushing effrontery and
bribery, and the B.N.Z. swindle, and Ward’s
overdraft, &c., the voice of the country had been,
&c., &e. But in 1899 the outraged nation would
rise as one man against Seddon and dash his head
against, &c., &c., &e.
Mclb. Australasian wants, in the Tory
interest, a reduction in the membership of Vie.
Assembly. In the old days, according to the
Argus’s weekly edition, “ when the Assembly
consisted of 79 members it did its work ever so
much better than it does now. The debates were
brief, but real and effective.” In the Tory idea
everything old was good, and everything new is
all wrong. Consider the record of these brief and
effective old Parliaments : The first session of the
first Parliament represented a year and three days’
continuous jaw. Parliament No. 2; first session, 11
months and one week; second session, nine months.
Third Parliament had one 11 months’ session and
one of ten months. Fourth Parliament, only one
session of twelve months’ continuous shriek. Fifth
Parliament put up a record, inasmuch as it had five
sessions in one year and nine months, and did
nothing, and it had two complete sessions in 4|
months. Eighth and ninth Parliaments had an
eleven months’ session each ; tenth Parliament sat
continuously for months; eleventh
ment had also a session which went straight on for
over a year of incessant yell and execration.
That brings the record up to 16 years ago. And
the Australasian yearns for the brevity and
effectiveness of these good old politicians who
talked each other to death for twelve and fourteen
months at a stretch, and did nothing but throw out
Ministries at the rate of 20 in 26 years; and it
says that the men of to-day are too diffuse and
don’t stick to business.
Dear Bulletin, —There seems to be a
demand in Europe for Australian snakes’ heads,
which are now being exported pickled in gly-
cerine in considerable quantities. To what use
they are put I don’t know 1 Can you inform me 7
—Yours, T.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Jack Lyons made 72 in a club-match on
Adelaide Oval, on Nov. 28. This score came just in time
to make the big man a certainty against N.S.VV. on
Dec. 19.
* * • *
S.A. team against N.S.W. on Adelaide Oval,
Dec. 19, will consist of G. Giffen, Jones, Hill, Darling, A.
H. Jarvis, F. Jarvis, Lyons, Reedman, Travers, Evansand
Bailey. Jarvis had a tight squeeze to get in the team, as
M‘Kenzie is considered quite his equal behind the sticks.
Evans and Bailey are new players of promise, the latter
particularly being a fine batsman. The team is perhaps
the strongest S.A. ever put in the field, especially in
batting.
* * * *
The little crowd of 3000, who came to see the
corpse on Sydney Cricket Ground last Saturday, went
home disappointed; the lily-white amateurs couldn’t ride
fast enough to make an accident possible. The new track
could be much worse than it is, but its good time won't
arrive until a big cash-amateur field is going hotfoot for a
real fat purse. Then the crowd will be able to rub its
hands gleefully and sing, “ More work for the undertaker,
’nother little job for the tombstone-maker.” The track is
hard-and-fast, and the cashmen should break both necks
and records on it.
The sum of one shilling sterling was charged for
admission to the ground, and another for the grand-
stand, and the amount of sixpence for a programme—in
all, 2s. 6d. It was the worst half-crown’s worth that any-
body will get for the money, unless he pays the same to
go in again this Saturday. Some three of the amateur
cyclists could ride fast enough to keep warm, but as these
generally waited for the slow ones to make the pace,
racing was rather absent. The slowness was somewhat
relieved in the Novelty race, by making the men dis-
mount and run, wheeling their machines backwards.
Seeing two gophers run would have been quite exciting
compared with most of the performances.
The three Sydney men, Cameron, Hamilton,
and New, were the only ones who had a notion of lifting
her up, and these three, in the interprovincial three-men-
aside team race, came clean away from the Vic. and
Queensland riders in the last lap. Cameron won the
Jive-mile and Hamilton the One-mile championship.
Part of the programme was a so-called “ exhi-
bition ” of pole and high-jumping by an Irish athlete, M.
M. Roseingrave ; it mostly consisted of M. M. It. hitching
up his green pants and passing' remarks, in a somewhat
soprano voice, to his countryman, Malone, who kept
flourishing a stick, after the fashion of an excited Kil-
kenny cat brandishing its tail. To bill Roseingrave’s
efforts as “exhibition” is an obvious and miserable
dodge, because the young man from the green country
can't pole or high-jump worth a straw. The amateur
pole-jump record is lift. 9in., whereas the Roseingrave
“exhibition” scored only Oft. Klin. ; similarly, the ama-
teur high-jump is 6ft. s|in., while the Roseingrave
best is but sft. Oin. Roseingrave’s business is long
jumping, hurdle-racing, pigeon-shooting. But to get him
to do an}' of these things even as “ exhibition,” is about
as sensible as to get the hairy-legged young man to play
twos-and-threes by himself, or to play a game of cricket,
or leap-frog, or football. Any of these things would be
just as diverting as watching a man who can’t pole-jump
fall on the jumping bar and break it in two. Bomehow,
people who run sporting meetings always act on the basis
that the public is a great and universal ass which will
come to see anything. And the public is just now show-
ing by its judicious absence that it is beginning to under-
stand the shallow tricks of the sporting-people.
* * » *
The people who knew all about it for once fell
in simultaneously with the general public over the final
heat of the Austral. Not 10 people in Melb. considered it
possible that Kellow and Walne could make up that cruel
gap of 80yds. and catch the field, and certainly not one of
those 10 would have for a moment believed, prior to the
event, that the Austral of '96 would have been accom-
plished in 2min. 29secs. Kellow’s win was the result of a
combination of skill, pluck, and sound common sense on
the part of him and Walne, and of the utter drivelling
silliness of the men on the out marks. The latter, relying
upon Jackson to rush away as he did the previous Satur-
day, made no arrangements about pacing, and when the
Avoca miner failed to jump to the front they simply
pedalled along at an easy speed looking blankly at one
another, and allowed the enterprising couple behind to
catch them up.
The judging of the race was a fitting consumma-
tion to the other exhibitions of imbecility given by the
M.B.C. officials during the meeting. Whether Walne or
Clinton finished second no spectator has the temerity
to say, but no person in any position in the ground
could avoid seeing Elliott pass the post a full length and
a -half behind both. Yet the judges awarded Walne second
place and Elliott third, evidently never having seen
Clinton at all.
The real quality of such cyclists as Kellow and
Walne is only properly apparent when it is remembered
that last year Crisp won his Austral in 4ni. 42sec., starting
from 255yds. This time Kellow was on 15 and Walne on
20, and they finished first and second in 4m. 29sec. On
an unbanked grass track and with a big field to get round
this is not too bad.
* * * *
A Melb. daily, piqued at being unable to get
full particulars as to gate receipts at the Austral meeting,
has ventured to wonder what the M.B.C. intends to do with
its profits. Tins Bulletin broached this subject a vear ago.
Thus far the prosperous club has not even equipped itself
with a good, substantial-marking board. The amateurish
arrangement fixed up in the centre of the M.C.C ground
on the first day of the recent corroboree broke down
ignominiously on the second d«y, and the winning num-
bers in the Austral final, as proclaimed on the ordinary
cricket scoring-board, were not clear to people in the far-
away grand stand. A half-crown gate fee should cer-
tainly entitle one to a fair chance of knowing who’s who
when the judge gives his decision, especially when the
judge’s decision—as in the case of Elliott’s “ third ” for
the Austral—happens to contain a flagrant mistake.
♦ * * *
Kellow is, seemingly, the levellest-headed biker
in the Victorian crowd. The long man struggles for coin,
whilst smaller and more verdant contemporaries, of the
Parsons class are pumping themselves out in pursuit of
trumpery championships. His handicap performances
speak for themselves. Last New Year’s Day there was a
t'loo stake to be captured on the M.C.C. ground, and
Kellow took the trick judiciously. For the next 10
months he did nothing calculated to excite popular
admiration, but then came a plump little £5O prize at St.
Kilda. Kellow scored again. Victor}' on that occasion
meant a reduction in his start for the Austral—he was put
back from the 30 to the 15yds. mark—but a half-century
in hand was worth £3OO in the bush, anyway. The result
proved that he lost nothing by being put back. Kellow won
the Austral from 15yds. Being now the hero of the hour and
in top form, it was well to improve the shining- hour by
taking- the M. 8.0. Plate, so liellow duly gathered in
another £4O stake. From four races he has drawn £490.
Wise individual. The other fellows can toil for “ cham-
pionships,” with £2O attached, in scratch events. As soon
as a liiy sum of money is seen hanging to a crown of glory
long Kellow will have a good go for the diadem.
* • • *
The Bulletin has received from Barroni, of
Melb., a very fine photo, of O. B. Kellow, winner of this
year’s Austral.
* * » *
Kellow, the Austral winner, before going into
*the cycle business did eighteen months of dry-blowing and
pick-swinging in Westralia. Walne, the second man, is a
bridge carpenter. A constitution built up by hard work
stands to a man in a big contest ; Bilmartin’s great
physique is the production of years of heavy toil.
♦ * * *
The moral of this year’s Austral is that, in order
to win, a man must have a pacemaker. Kellow and Walne,
the back men, agreed to pace each other lap and lap
about; they tossed for choice of turn ; Kellow won ; Walne
began the severe work of pacemaking. Then, as the
field was caught in the three laps, Walne paced two laps
against Kellow’s one, and was necessarily the more ex-
hausted. Kellow subsequently nipped the £3OO first prize,
with Walne second ; but had the pacing turns been re-
versed, the finishing positions might have been reversed
also. Before the race Kellow wanted to “ whack it” with
Walne, but the latter would consent to only a £5O saver.
Supposing that Elliott, or Jackson, or Clinton had had a
pacemaker, where would the scratch men have been.
• * * *
Melb. Age, which has been an insane barracker
for Parsons, passed some ridiculous comments on the
riding of Walne and “ the champion ” in their Austral
heat—the one that Jackson ran away with. Parsons’
chance, according to the penny oracle, was ruined by the
awful selfishness of Walne, who waited behind “ the
champion ” instead of pacing him. Owing to the publi-
cation of this balderdash, perhaps, the man from Banana-
land paced Parsons for all he was worth when they came
together again in the third heat of the M.B.C. Plate, and
the result was as before. Walne, who lost many yards by
running wide, finished second ; Parsons couldn’t live with
him when the pinch came. Subsequently Walne (10yds.)
ran a respectable second in the Austral final, timed 13sec.
faster than last year's. In 1895 the overrated Parsons,
starting from scratch in the Austral final, never got near
to the winner, wherefore it may be reasonably assumed
from inter-annual time-test, as well as from latest “ form,”
that Walne can beat Parsons on their respective merits in
a two-mile handicap provided there be sufficient cash in-
ducement for him to do the deed.
* • * •
An all-important difference between horses and
cyclists has to be considered by racing tipsters. The horse
doesn’t know whether he is racing for £lO or for £3OO,
whereas the man does know, and, according to the value of
the stake, he is apt to lose his perve or otherwise.
Backers of Jackson for the Austral could see—if they had
intelligent eyes—that the red-hot favorite racing anxiously
in the final was not the same Jackson who had won his
preliminary heat without once looking over his shoulder.
The boy MTnnes was another palpable sufferer from
nerves.
* * * *
The forthcoming Martin-Parsons matches are,
on latest evidence, a good thing for Martin. The strong
plugger, after his two months enforced rest, is in the
rosiest of health, and his track-work is consistently
excellent. Parsons, on the contrary, is “ streaky ’’—bril-
liant to-day, dead-slow to-morrow. The distances are :
One mile (unpaced), five and ten miles (paced). Martin
will have A 1 pacing. The betting at present is 4tol on
plugger Bill. The pair, like Gaudaur and Stanbury before
their match, are “just like brothers.”
* * * •
R. Lewis, Megson, and Payne, the three N.S.W.
cyclists who went all the way to England to get well
beaten, when they could have got as well beaten by stay-
ing at home, have duly returned to Sydney. The doings
of the three make slow reading. Lewis started four times,
fell twice, lost four times ; Megson started six times, fell
thrice, lost five times ; Payne started seven times, fell
four times, lost seven times, and was suspended for
“ arranging a race.” The belated youths had a dull time
in England, for between the climate and their falls upon
the cement tracks they spent their days in rains and their
nights in plasters.
* * * *
Said that Parsons is suffering from inflamma-
tion of the intestines, first induced by iced drinks in
America. What is really the matter with Parsons, how-
ever, is the result of being frozen out in so many races
of late.
* * * *
Ken Lewis is still there with his splendid sprint,
winning the mile and five-mile international scratch
races at Adelaide last Saturday, and downing Relph, the
new Englishman, the Midland Counties champion. Lewis
sprinted away from Martin the last time the pair met.
He has perhaps the best-proportioned physique of any
cyclist in Australia : he weighs 9st. 121 b, stands sft. 9in.,
and measures 40in. round the chest. He comes of an
English father and Irish mother, and is an all-round man
—cyclist, boxer, swimmer, footballer, buckjump rider,
&c. He has put in plenty of solid work on farm and
station. He is 24 years old, and during the nine years he
has been cycling he has won altogether some £llOO.
* * * *
The great and only Bilmartin shortly intends to
start tandem, and will take to his loving heart the pretty
daughter of a Ballarat chemist, who is also the niece of
a well-advertised parson. The lady is one of the tip-top
musicians of the goldfield city, and, as Bill himself can
warble a little on occasions, she will probably piny his
accompaniments in future. The above statements will
be duly contradicted in next week’s Bulletin. The ap-
proaching marriage of a great man like Bilmartin is
always announced and contradicted a few times before it
really comes off.
* * * *
On the bike, as at every other kind of athletic
contest, a good big man is better than a good little ’un.
The big man is beginning to come freely into the fray.
Bilmartin grows beautifully less by comparison, and
Parsons—why, that pale young man will be nowhere
before he’s five-and-twenty.
* * * *
Tasmania has the bicycle-craze badly. The
latest victim is the Solicitor-General, who collided with
another bikist, and had to sacrifice two good Supreme
Court briefs because it was incompatible with his dignity
to appear in court with two black eyes, enlarged nose,
disarranged scalp, and considerable gravel-rash.
* ♦ * *
At ’Ome Lord Durham is on the warpath re the
vagaries of handicappers. In N S.W. some owners and
trainers have petitioned the A.J.O. committee with re-
gard to the patchy performances of their well-paid official.
A glance at the weights for Villiers Stakes and Summer
Cup will show any judge of weight-adjusting that there is
great room for improvement in the handiwork of the
A.J.C. official handicapper. Lord Durham’s suggestion of
two handicappers working conjointly deserves more than
a passing thought.
**. * *
Astronomer (Castor—Frailty), who cost 1575g5.
as a yearling, won his first race at recent Randwick
meeting. He is a horse likely to train on into a good ’un,
and, with but 7st. 61b. to carry, he should be near winning
the Carrington Stakes on New Year’s Day. Whv the
handicapper only raised him 61b. for his victory is un-
accountable.
« » * *
Fleet Admiral (Richmond—Footstep) has started
from Adelaide to Sydney, where he is to take part in the
valuable December Stakes. This fine-looking brother to
The Admiral was second in Maribyrnong Plate and third
in Nursery Handicap at recent Flemington meeting, so
that a great race should be seen between'him and Patriot
in which the latter may just get the better.

* * * *
The Y.R.C. seems not very anxious for an
enquiry re the alleged attempt to “ stiffen ” Thunder
Queen for the Oaks. A bookmaker’s name is mentioned
and two versions of his ill-fated misdemeanour have gone
the rounds. One yarn states that he administered a dose
personally or by proxy, to a horse in mistake for the mare,
whilst the other narrative declares that Thunder Queen
was properly hocussed, but too late in the dav for the
drug to achieve its mission. Behind these misty rumors
stands the large, unalterable fact that Ikey Mo in general
fielded against Thunder Queen for the Oaks as though he
knew she couldn’t win, whereas on paper the favorite
couldn’t very well lose. The story of the nobbling is sup-
ported by the eloquent testimony of figures in sundry
books. Nevertheless an inspired turf authority thinks
it “extremely doubtful if anything will come of the
matter.”
* * * *
The results of two principal handicaps at Rose-
hill last Saturday point to the undeniable chances pos-
sessed by Balance, Sabretache, Moorefield and Cartridge
of winning some substantial prizes at the approaching
Randwick meetings. The military double. Cartridge and
Sabretache, “on paper,” looks well for Villiers Stakes and
Summer Cup.
* * * *
The long-suffering flatite at Randwick pays his
shilling and gets very little for his money. Scratchings,
starters, &c., are to him an unknown quantity, as no
information on these very important racing points is
vouchsafed him, barring the few almost indistinguishable
hieroglyphics displayed at end of members’-stand in the
sacred lawn. Flatites don’t, as a rule, run to binoculars,
so a board, on which starters’ numbers and riders’ names
are legibly exhibited, should be provided by the rich
A. J.C. for multitudinous shilling patrons.
* * * *
I lattites at Rlemington don’t pay for admission,
yet grumble and are listened to. Their brethren at Rand-
wick do pay the humble shilling, don’t grumble much,
though they have legitimate cause to, and their interests
are not studied in the least. Surely before approaching
big summer meetings the A. J.C. authorities might have a
board erected with the horses’ numbers legibly displayed
for the information of the many shilling patrons who can-
not sport binoculars to decipher the numbers exhibited at
end of members’ stand in lawn.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —It is true, as “S.S.” writes,
that the V.R.C. derives a little money from the Plat at
Flemington. But the few flattites who contribute towards
the support of the show are not the grumblers. The cry
for a grand-stand gratis, and the suggestion that refresh-
ments ought to be thrown in, comes from the deadheads.
—F.P.

Extract from a manly British letter in
East Anglian Daily Times (Eng.)
We suddenly surprised five Kaffirs watching some
goats. . . . One short command was given, and the
five men were shot dead, and we rode on again as if
nothing had happened.
Of all the Merry Christmases that will in-
toxicate this continent shortly, probably the most
deliciously-joyful will occur where father carves
the duck with one of Baker’s Christy carvers, at
2s. 6d. post-free. These celebrated instruments
effectually prevent gravy on the ceiling, or stuffing
on the cloth, or gizzard in the eye of the youngest
olive-branch. And Baker, the well-known Hunter-
street (Sydney) cutler, has everything else of the
best.*

Public Notices.
In the Supreme Court of
New South Wales.
In Divorce.
No. 2342.
Between EMILY HAWKINS
Petitioner
and
WILLIAM HAWKINS
Respondent.
TO WILLIAM HAWKINS, LATE OF
SYDNEY.
Take notice that the said EMILY HAWKINS
has commenced a suit against you in this Honor-
able Court, and is applying for a dissolution of
Marriage on the ground that you have without
just cause or excuse wilfully deserted her, and
without any such cause or excuse left her con-
tinuously so deserted during three years and
upwards ; and take notice that you are required
to enter an appearance to the said suit at the
proper office of this Honorable Court by the 31st
day of DECEMBER next after the publication
hereof, and in the event of your not doing so
within the time limited, or obtaining further
time, the petitioner will proceed, and the suit be
heard and determined your absence notwith-
standing.
A copy of the petition filed herein may be had
on application to the undersigned free of charge.
Dated this 14th day of December, 1806.
JAMES WILLIAM ABIGAIL,
Solicitor for Petitioner,
97 Elizabeth Street, Sydney.
For the Registrar,
John Geo. Leary, Chief Clerk.

“ Tales of the
Convict System.”
CHAT WITH AN EX-DE-
TECTIVE.
NARROW ESCAPES OF A RETIRED
OFFICER.
Mr. Richard Wood was not at home when, says
an interviewer, I rang the bell at his residence,
Norfolk House, Cornwallis-street, Hastings, but
whilst I was inquiring he was pointed outcrossing
the road. His hale and hearty appearance, ereet-
ness of stature, and cheeriness of manner, seemed
almost to belie the fact that ex-Detective Sergeant
Wood was, as he subsequently told me, turned
sixty-five, and that many years of his life had been
spent in active service, exposed to all weathers,
and engaged in arduous work such as cannot fail
to tell on the physical frame. I asked a few
questions about his experience.
“ Yes,” he said, “ I was often exposed to risk.
Sciatica being my chief trouble. I had suffered
from it off and on for about fifteen years, and a
little while ago I was very bad with it. I think
no man has ever had sciatica worse than I have.
When I was in the police-force I have lain on the
floor all night in the police-station, and they have
had to bring me home in a cab.”
“A short time ago I hear you had an excep-
tionally bad attack! ”
“Yes, and my wife thought that I should not
get over it. My case seemed hopeless. You can
hardly name a medicine that I have not taken.
But these Pills that cured me as soon as I got to
know them—Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People, I mean —are nothing like other medicine.
I was induced to use them through reading of the
wonderful cure of a woman at Dungeness, and,
having done so, I consider they are the medicine
for any person ailing. I feel quite a new man.
The Pills have put new nerve into me. I used to
have a good nerve at one time when in the police-
force, and wanted it for some of the jobs that I
had to do. The continuation of this sciatica and
rheumatism —I also used to suffer from pains in
the head —tended to weaken my nerve. But now
I have got the nerve to go out and hit a lion. The
sciatica and rheumatic pains have entirely gone
from the hips and shoulders and other parts.”
Further interrogated, the ex-detective said he
regarded the Pills as “the best medicine that is
made.” He was now equal to walking almost any
distance—could walk to Eastbourne, 1(5 or 17 miles.
Perhaps Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People never had a more enthusiastic endorsement.
Mr. Wood was only too anxious to tell in his in-
teresting and intelligent manner of the tine remedy
! he had proved Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to be, as
they are in all the many disorders which arise from
an impoverished state of the blood and nerves,
such as anaemia, pale and sallow complexion,
general muscular weakness, loss of appetite, short-
ness of breath, pains in the back, nervous head-
aches, early decay, all forms of female weakness,
\ hysteria ? paralysis, locomotor ataxy, sciatica,
palpitations, low vitality, and wasting from any
cause.
Dr. Williams Pink Pills are obtainable from all
leading chemists, or from the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Coy., N.S.W., Sydney, who will forward
(post paid) on receipt of stamps or post order, 1
box for 2s 9d, or for 14s 9d.

Victorian butter will no longer be decently
charged by Danish butter-men with being the pro-
duct of unhealthy herds. Vic. Agricultural
Department proposes to appoint six qualified vets,
to examine every herd in the province, and con-
demn all diseased cattle.
When the M‘Gregor party ascended Mount
Scratchley (Papua) lately, they found growing on
the uplands English buttercups and daisies, and
heard and saw a bird very like the skylark. By
the way, many hundreds of ounces of gold have
been quietly shipped over lately to Queensland.
The Salvationists are going into the food
line in Sydney, and mean to feed the poorest of
the poor. “ When their food depot is opened,”
said Commandant Booth, the other day, “he
thought that they would be able to show a model
hotel, a model restaurant, where a man for a
penny, or possibly for a halfpenny, would obtain
the wherewithal to stay the cravings of hunger.”
Mr. Arthur Griffith wishes to notify intend-
ing patentees that he has commenced practice as a
Patents and Trade Marks Agent at 39 Castlereagh-
street, Sydney. He has entered into arrange-
ments with leading agents in Europe, America,
Australasia, and South Africa, so that inventors
inay rely upon having their interests protected.
Mr. Griffith’s charges will, in all cases, be so
hioderate as to place a patent within the reach of
the poorest inventor. For £2O, exclusive of the
cost of plans, Mr. Griffith will obtain Provisional
Protection for any invention in the seven Austra-
lasian colonies and Great Britain.*
The latest addition to Sydney journalism
is called The Light op the Nations of Truth
and the Christian Endeavor, and is compre-
hensively described on its front page as
A monthly journal on Coming Events, Signs in the
Heavens and the Earth, Spiritual Gifts, Healing by Laying
on of Hands and by Massage Treatment, Curing the Sick
with and without the assistance of medicine, Shorthand
Reports of Sermons, Lectures and Addresses, Healthy
Recreation and Amusement, Short Stories, Natural Foods
and their uses, Agricultural and Mineral Wealth, and
how to develop it, and other subjects bearing on the
Spiritual and Material Welfare of Nations and Individuals.
Besides other subscriber-inducing offers it promises
to give “ one share fully-paid-up in the Sunrise
Gold Mining Prospecting Co. to each one who
sends 20 new subscribers.”

“Papa’s Hair.”
A gentleman has sent us the following “ A few
weeks ago I bought a bottle of your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’
and it has acted with the most satisfactory results.
Previous to doing so, my hair had a mousey color
streaked with grey. The dear children would trv
to count papa’s grey hairs, but, because of the
number, could not succeed. Being only 44 years
of age, I determined to improve my hair, and tried
your ‘ Papa’s Hair,’ and am pleased to write that,
though at first there seemed little difference, gradu-
ally the hair regained its original color (there was
no dyeing whatever) and it is now strong and glossy.
I asked my little daughter this morning to count
my grey hairs, but when looking for them she cried
out— 1 Why, papa, there are no white hairs; mamma
must have pulled them all out.’ ”
This Preparation is not a Dye, but acts gradu-
ally on the Roots of the Hair, and restores Grey
Hair to its natural color and lustre.
No preparation will make hair grow better than
“Papa’s ILair.”
It effectually prevents the formation of Dandriff,
as it is called, the presence of which always denotes
diseased action in the cuticle of the scalp, causing
premature baldness, or a falling off of the hair. °
DIRECTIONS.—WeII shake the bottle, and with
a small piece of sponge rub into the roots of the
hair, night and morning, till the hair is restored to
its natural color ; afterwards apply twice a week.
3s. per Bottle, by post B<i. extra.

N.S.W. District Judge Coffey holds that
police evidence as to statements made by un-
defended prisoners should not be admitted unless
the policeman has warned prisoner beforehand
that the statement may be used. The distinction
here between a defended and undefended prisoner
is not clear to the eye of reason; but it is rather
anomalous that the law does not oblige a police-
man to warn, and that about half the N.S.W.
Judges say he should warn and half say the re-
verse.

A clergyman, on a holiday trip, visited a
blacks’ camp for the purpose of teaching the
Lord’s Prayer to the heathen ; having repeated it
many times to them, he wished them to say it
after him. Old gin (loq.): “ You bin yabber,‘gib
um daily bread,’ ‘ gib um daily bread.’ Why not
gib it more plour ? ”
Dan. O’Connor writes to The Bulletin
that a statement reprinted from Sydney Tele-
graph, to effect that, at Bathurst Convention,
“ Mr. D. O’Connor, M.L.C., filled in the ten
minutes that remained before the adjournment for
the garden-party in praising Bathurst and every-
body connected with it, as well as the speakers
who had preceded him,” is “ grossly false.” As a
matter of fact, Dan. says his speech was as con-
cise and pertinent a reference to the Federation
cause as time would allow, and “several promi-
nent gentlemen waited to congratulate ” him
upon it.

An excellent remedy against sleep-walk-
mg is a strip of sheet-metal—iron, zinc, copper—
laid alongside the somnambulist’s bed, so wide
and long that as soon as he puts his feet out of
bed they touch the cold metal. The shock
awakens him every time.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block m Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Colhns-street, Melb., a distance of C6oft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 0
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland •
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
mam walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long.*
The Christmas Bulletin for ’96 is the
biggest sixpenny publication yet attempted in
Australia, consists wholly of new matter in prose
and verse by the best Australian writers, and is
avishly illustrated throughout by the best artists
this side of the line. The circulation this year
has exceeded all previous records, and as orders
are still coming in, and the quantity available is
limited, only those who apply early can possibly
be supplied.
After the cross-examination of plaintiff by
Salomons, Q.C., in the case Ricketson v. Smith (a

carr\ large quantities of edible scrub), the former,
smarting from the severity of the Q.C.’s handling,
interviewed him and asked : “ Can you tell me,
Sir Julian, whether your great-grandfather,
.Nebuchadnezzar, ate scrub or grass ?But the
barrister declined to advise unless briefed.

N. S. W. Labor Minister Garrard declares that
the local unemployed are nearly absorbed, and in
proof explains how hard it is to get men to go
“scrubbing” to the Bogan. Does a kangaroo
willingly run to be shot ? Does a man earnestly
beg to be sent to starvation, destruction—Sheol ?
The fact that 100 fresh men can be got every fort-
night to heave mullock about at Botany Sewage
Farm for 6s. per day, and that Randwick Relief
Works and that hell on earth, Shea’s Creek, are
kept going all the time (to say nothing of the 900
that Sydney Benevolent Asylum is feeding), is
proof that the unemployed will for a long time yet
sit heavily on the chest of the jolly and fat
Cabinet.
The Eye-sight measure sent per post by
the inventors, Messrs. Greenfield and Barraclough,
the well-known opticians of George-st., Sydney,
is a wonderful little contrivance which will enable
eye-sufferers in the country to ascertain the data
necessary for obtaining proper spectacles. The
subtler forms of sight-defect cannot, of course, be
measured by the instrument, but it is thoroughly
effective for the more usual forms of eye affection
such as long sight, short sight, old sight. Full
particulars from Messrs. Greenfield and Barra-
clough. *
R. Temperley, a North Coast (N.S.W.)
journalist, lecturing in Sydney the other day,
reiterated that the whole of the Northern sugar
district was “slowly but surely drifting from a
flourishing white man’s territory into a district
of Hindoos. Leaving the Clarence district
out of the question, there were 500 colored
people on the Richmond and Tweed alone,
and even on the Clarence River black faces
were not uncommon. In one mill on the Tweed
River were now employed 200 kanakas.” This
colored labor was not employed in the Colonial
Sugar Co.’s works, added Mr. Temperley, who,
like every other Northern trader, has to be careful
what he says about the big monopoly. No ; but
the C.S.R. Co. crushes the cane grown by black
hands ; and it has imported thousands of coolies
for its black-labor mills and plantations in Fiji,
established in deliberate competition with the
North Coast farmers, for whose interests it pleads
so pathetically with the white side of its face.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
°f opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A certain recent butchery—which con-
sisted in the demolition of an aged couple in their
own house, for no visible purpose—has got strangely
mixed up. The assassin left nothing behind him
which looked like a clue, therefore the nearest
badly-dressed man was arrested, and a witness
was found to swear profusely against him. Never-
theless, the case broke down for want of anything
like reasonable evidence. Then the wife of said
witness learned, or thought she did, that he had a
previous partner still living, and that she wasn’t
his wife, and in her wrath she rose up and de-
nounced him as the real culprit. The local authori-
ties are consequently in a complicated situation.
The wife’s evidence is reckoned good against her
alleged husband if she is not his wife ; if she is
his wife it isn’t. Therefore, the first business is to
find the alleged first wife, and convict him of
bigamy, and then convict him of murder on the
evidence of the alleged second wife, unless she
repents before then and retracts everything she
has said. As no one has yet contrived to lay
hands on the first wife, if there is such a person,
things haven’t got fairly started. Until she is
found there is no visible evidence on which to
arrest the suspect on a charge of bigamy, and
until the bigamy is proved there is no good legal
evidence on which to arrest him on the other
charge. Therefore, he walks about at large, and
the local force is fast going off its head with worry
and suppressed excitement.

Yankee Packet .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Power o’ ploughs and clothes-pegs in her, pork
and beans for ev’ry sinner,
Pork and beans for captain’s dinner —
Pass her lines and pull away !
Hiram Brown, of New Orleans, men, lots of
graft ye know it means, men,
Lifting out those big machines, men—
Swing her in there, Hip Hooray !
Yankee packet’s down the water —
Blow, my bully boys, blow;
Third mate loved the skipper’s daughter—
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Wey-hey-ho. Wey-hey-Ho,
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Trim old tub, the Hiram Brown, sir; some dark
night she’ll dive right down, sir,
Every mother’s son will drown, sir;
She’s insured ‘ right up,’ you bet.
Built in ’5O, so they say, guess she’s almost had her
day.
Pass that shore-line on this way—
But she wobbles round it yet.
How’d you know ’twas a Yankee packet ?
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Know’d her by the awful racket—
Blow, my bully \ioys, blow.
Wey-hey-ho, the cook was jealous,
Spiled the soup and bust his bellus —
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Hand the boss them bills o’ lading—
Once she did a bit o’ trading
When the Yanks were South—blockading,
In the days of Stars and Bars.
Time they built the Alabama, Union steamer tried
to ram her,
Sent him down head-first, Goddamer 1
Shook her engines, saved her spars.
Once she did a bit o’ tradin’ —•
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Third mate killed the cook at Aden—■
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Wey-hey-ho. fill her up an’ let her go,
Down with coal to Callao—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Cotton cargo crammed to hatches,'
Out she runs with “ rob ” despatches—•
See them two big painted patches?
That’s the mark of shot and shell—
Out she runs beneath their noses,
Bangs away—oh, Holy Moses,
Ship’s afire ! Hey, man the hoses !
Go she must, or go to Hell.
Go she must, or go to blazes —
Blow, my bujly boys, blow ;
Shout the good old packet’s praises—
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Lively Loo from Boston sailing,
All the girls were left bewailing
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Left and right the guns went banging, whistles
tooting, bells a-clanging ;
Lots o’ gilt to that trip hanging,
Worth the risk and worth the fight.
Timbers ripped and sails all tattered,
Wheel-house smashed and starn-post shattered,
This same planking blood-bespattered,
Hiram Brown got through all right.
Lively Loo she took my' fancy—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
So I shipped with Captain Clancy
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
• Wind abaft—a lively breeze —
Sailed away to China seas—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
When the Yanks had finished shooting,
Hiram Brown she went recruiting—
That dark trade her skipper suiting
In the year of sixty-eight.
Changed her flag and got new papers,
Alter’d down to funnel scrapers,
But she starts her same old capers,
Seemed they couldn't run her straight.
Sad we left our loves behind us—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
To be true they iong enjoined us—•
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
But we found in foreign places
Welcome smiles on fairer faces—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Blackbird cargo, soon she’d gathered—blackbird
cargo, tied and tethered—
Rain and storm and wind safe weathered,
Sou’ by East away she slips;
Maybe cargo wasn’t willing, when with snowy
sails outfilling,
And the bo’sun’s whistle thrilling,
Squared away that pride of ships !
Oh ! our skipper was a daisy—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Drove the whole fo’castle crazy—
Blow, mv bully boys, blow.
So wo left him ruck and rumbo
In the harbor of Colombo —
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
On and off the coast o’ Chili, Hiram Brown she
kept until he
Made us think he’d gone quite silly,
But one night a schooner come
Right ’longside, and making fast, sir,
O’er our side in haste she passed, sir
(With a look-out on the mast, sir),
Six-and-eighty casks of rum !
“ Lively Loo” was heard no myre on— l
Blow, my bully boys, blow :
Went one night a dark lee shore on—
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Sharks, they ate the old man’s nose off,
Bit his ears and then his toes off'
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Schooner on her way is going, when our skipper,
cute and knowing,
Sets that rare old stingo flowing—
Taps a cask and treats his men ;
Steam’s got up, she makes right down there to
some small plantation town where
Niggers black and niggers brown there,
Served their Dons (for nothing)—then
Longshore lasses came to greet us—
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Bumboat men were glad to meet us—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Sold our togs to buy bad liquor,
Pawned my pants and popped the ticker—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Got our casks ashore at first, boys ; guess that job
would raise your thirst, boys ;
Maybe one or two was burst, boys,
Though for that we didn’t stop ;
Landed niggers next right slickly, ranged those
casks on wharf corrictly,
Then (obeying orders) quickly,
Perched a nigger each on top.
Got another ship to sail in—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Went to Arctic Ocean whalin’—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Struck a ’berg one night and sunk it,
Cold as ’ell, but couldn’t funk it—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Then our skipper, sleek and smiling, in a manner
most beguiling, *
Law and commerce reconciling,
Mounts upon a cotton-bale,
Joking with the senors lightly, speaking Spanish
there politely,
Calm and cool, collected,-sprightly,
Starts a lively auction sale.
Saw a “ right whale ” busy “ spoutin’ ”
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
“There she blows,” the look-out shoutin’—■
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
“ Lower away,” and off we goes, mate,
Sticks a harpoon in her nose, mate—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Each rich Don who wanted labor understood his
planter neighbor,
So their troops, with gun and sabre,
To arrest us did not come ;
Whilst our captain grow elated as the bids were
elevated,
And the same, you'll guess, related
More to nigger than to rum.
Off she goes and us behind her—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Got a calf, but never mind her—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Down she dives, our lances shunning—
Keeps the harpoon-reel a-running,
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
So each buyer quick would figger out the value of
the nigger,
And the auctioneer would snigger
When he threw the barrel in !
Guess that cargo paid her owners : likely they were
psalm-song groaners,
Scripture-text and proverb moaners,
But they winked at tricks o’ sin.
Up she comes, and right beside us—
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Goes about-now, woe betide us— ■
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Turns her tail gee-whoop and thrashed us,
Into fifty pieces smashed us—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Hiram Brown’s been o’er and under, cross the
seas in storm and thunder ;
Some rough night she’ll go asunder,
And Old Nick will have a lark.
Jack’s poor lass will be a griever—
Haul her in and hitch and heave her ;
Guess next trip the rats will leave her—•
She’s as old as Noah’s ark.
Missed the coxswain as we wallowed —
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Angry whale poor chap had swallowed —
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Second boat, she saw her spout then,
Killed the whale and cut him out, men—•
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
There’s the guy-rope rigged and ready !
Got your hatches broke already ?
Let her zip ! Hey, winchman, steady,
Case o’ hardware marked “ B.A.’’;
Longshore loafers down below there,
You’re too denied infernal slow there !
Hook away, and up ye go there !
Yankee packet’s in, hooray !
Vankee packet’s down the river—•
Blow, my bully boys, blow ;
Fifteen hundred bales to give her— l
Blow, my bully boys, blow.
Wey-hey-ho! Wey-hey-Ho!
Fill her up and let her go—
Blow, blow, my bully boys, blow.
Sydney, March, ’95.
EDWIN J. BRADY.
Ifc was shown the other day at a Sydney
suburban court that a corporation laborer charged
witli cruelty had struck his horse a severe blow on
the ribs with a shovel. Defendant asked to be
let off, saying it was usual for drivers to “touch
up” their horses with a shovel! A whip was no
use. Fined -£2 or 14 days’ hard.
The Journal op Cutaneous Diseases tells
of a Venezuelan lady, who had contracted leprosy,
being regularly visited by an American lady
friend. Before the Venezuelan lady died, her
American lady friend was a leper; before the
American lady died, a favorite nephew living in
her house had to be removed to a lazaret.
Another woman who had once been a lazaret
attendant developed the disease 14 years after
leaving the post. In a N.Y. case a woman had
acquired the horror through living for a year next
door to a man afterwards found to be a leper.
Leprosy (mat pake) was introduced into Hawaii
in 1838 by a Chinese cook in a native family. In
less than 10 years all the family and many friends
in their set had it; now Hawaii is one huge lazar*
house.

Chat with an Ex-Detective.
HARROW ESCAPES OP A RETIRED OFFICER.
Mr. Richard Wood was not at home when, says an in-
terviewer, I rang- the bell at his residence, Norfolk House,
Cornwallis-street, Hastings, but whilst I was inquiring he
was pointed out crossing the road. His hale and hearty
appearance, erectness of stature, and cheeriness of man-
ner, seemed almost to belie the fact that Ex-Detective
Sergeant Wood was, as he subsequently told me, turned
sixty-five, and that many years of his life had been spent
in active service, exposed to all weathers, and engaged
in arduous work such as cannot fail to tell on the physical
frame. I asked a few questions about his experience,
j “ Yes,” he said, “ I w’as often exposed to risk. Sciatica
being my chief trouble. I had suffered from it off and on
for about fifteen years, and a little while ago I was very
bad with it. I think no man has ever had sciatica worse
than I have. When I was in the police-force I have lain
on the floor all night in the police-station, and they have
had to bring me home in a cab.”
“ A short time ago I hear you had an exceptionally bad
ittack?”
“ Yes, and my wife thought that I should not get over
t. My case seemed hopeless. You can hardly name a
medicine that I have not taken. But these Pills that
pured.me as soon as I got to know them—Dr. Williams’
pink Pills for Pale People I mean—are nothing like other
medicine. I was induced to use them through reading of
he wonderful cure of a woman at Dungeness, and having
lone so I consider they are the medicine for any person
fling. I feel quite a new man. The Pills have put new
lerve into me. I used to have a good nerve at one time
i’hen in the police force, and wanted it for some of the
obs that I had to do. The continuation of this sciatica
nd rheumatism—l also used to suffer from pains in the
ead—tended to weaken my nerve. But now I have got
he nerve to go out and hit a lion. The sciatica and
heumatic pains have entirely gone from the hips and
houlders and other parts.”
Further interrogated, the ex-detective said he regarded
he Pills as “ the best medicine that is made.” He was
ow equal to walking almost any distance —could walk to
astbourne, 16 or 17 miles.

An Interrupted Ceremony .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Heard at a distance, the strains that filled the air
round the little iron shanty were musical enough,
mellowed with the softness of tongues that lisped
through an unknown language :
He is a rock in a weary land,
A wea-ry land,
A wea-ry land,
A shelter in the time of storm.
Parson Phillips, glancing in at the door of his
little mission house, could not forbear an amused
grin across at the “lady-teacher” as Kanaka
Georges loud wail of “wea-ry land” rose high
above all other voices in the glory of a newlv-dis-
covered tenor. Miss Ross did not smile. This
work among the Kanakas was an intensely earnest
business with her. Her eyes shone proudly
out from her thin sun-bleached face at the
two long rows of scholars on either side of
the table. I hey sat very close together, with their
elbows stretched over the rough pine boards, and
their black heads clasped in their hands. As the
hymn ceased she hurried forward with a pile of
paper books. 1

can read now,” she said. “Will you begin,

George was still airing his tenor, and his mourn-
a land ” echoed with the departing

the sun still lingered on the iron roof and walls.
1 he mosquitoes buzzed angrily round her hand as it
pushed the hair back from her forehead. The torn
thumb-marked books were scattered on the table •
there were dusty footprints on the floor. It was
all very sordid and mean, now that the glamour of
zeal ha,d passed away. She was so tired of it all
so tired but she dared not give in, for had she
not resolved that all her life now must be one
on f act °* reparation. She started to her feet
and began hurriedly to tidy the room, to push
back the forms against the wall, to collect the un-

tor the pressure of one man’s lips upon hers and to

would start up and work feverishly in her little
house, or, if it were night time, she would fall on
“er knees and force her mind to concentrate itself
on wild prayers, until her tired brain relaxed and
she woke hours later to creep, cramped and
shivering, into her bed.
She was glad, after such a night, to wake to the
knowledge that it was Sunday morning, and that
on that day at least there was small time for in-
truding thoughts. In the morning there was the
church-service, and then came exhausting Sundav-
school teaching in the heat of the afternoon.
Although it was July, the day was a still one, and
the crowded school-room was oppressively close.
She was struggling to make her story interesting
and to keep little heads from nodding when she
was called away.
Miss Ross,” said the Parson, “there’s a poor
chap got smashed up in a dynamite explosion at
the mines m Woody Island. Gould you go to him ?

* * » *
It was midwinter, but the names of the
seasons are of small account in the latitude of
Woody Island. It was midwinter, and the island
lay m the dazzling blue water, burned and parched
and panting for rain, with the heat mist dancing
above the red soil, giving a hazy indistinctness to
the brown, coarse grass and the stems of the screw
palm and stunted ti-tree.
The dust and sand rose in a cloud before the
woman s feet, the heat scorched through her shoe-
leather. On all sides among the scrub rose the
grey rock-like ant-hills, jagged and solid-looking,
like so many rough-hewn tomb-stones, making it
h i ■,/ olle to believe them the work of so tiny
a builder. It was a dreary, sun-scorched road,
with occasional minute patches of shade from the
yellow-blossoming wild cotton-tree, where the
leaves were glued together in ominous green
bunches, warning the wary of the swarming green
ants on every twig.
Ihe familiar old hymn tune was running in her
head and her steps kept time with its refrain—“a
wea-ry land, a wea-ry land ” —as she struggled on
footsore and half-exhausted. The Kanaka and
his wife sauntered easily some dozen yards behind
her. How and then they said a few words: and
once a laugh floated forward to her ears, and the
incongruity made her start. Her heart was throb-
bing until each breath was difficult. At last, at
last she was to meet him again—him who had
brought so many years of misery upon her, but
who had once loved her ; for whose sake she had
committed the unpardonable sin winch no years
of repentance and self-sacrifice could expiate, and
yet whom she forgave and loved still, as women
ha y e don e before} and will do till the end of time
He had left her to bear alone her time of agony*
and her shame and misery, and now he was
dying, and m his turn was lying uncared for and
alone. A wea-ry land, a wea-ry land.” She set
her teeth as she stumbled over the sharp stones or
sank m the hot loose sand.
* * * .

he lay there with closed eyes—dying. The woman

heavy, dazed eyes, and then with a look of recog-
nition. The end was near and he had outgrown
surprise. 6

Capsized recently in Collingwood Bay, New
Guinea, a whaleboat with six white diggers and
one r Tannnt S ’vf 1 of , whom were drowned except
one Taupota boy who was picked up swimming
fve days after the mishap. Sounds strong, but
there are well-authenticated instances of S.S.
CXagwCr B6lv “ aaoat two “ d
Writes- J . Bull, jun.,” to The Bulle-
tin:— The average out-back Australian is a
curious animal. He mostly lives in a two-roomed
hut with a hole in the roof to act as a chimney. A
small stool stands outside the back door with an
mu tln -dish and a piece of soap at the end of it.
lhe whole family washes out of this dish, and
when water is scarce the one dishful does until
the end of the week. There is usually a water-
cask alongside the house out of which the fowls,
dogs, calves, and the average Australians drink
whenever they feel dry. The boss of this hut is
usually called a selector ; this is because he always
selects a bullock from a neighboring run whenever
his beef-cask gets a bit low.” Which latter re-
mark is perilously near poor Walter Cooper’s his-
torical harness-cask and bullet-mould ” jibe.
Rh° : “Is or is not Frankenstein a possi-
W; , Tbe humerus (bone between elbow and
shoulder) of a boy’s arm was removed for decay,
the forearm and joint and the shoulder just being
left as before. Into the blank space thus made
Hr. MacLwen, of Glasgow, introduced the
chopped-up fragments of the shin-bone of an
amputated leg. The result was that these frag-
ments united into a solid mass, and welded on to
the shoulder and elbow bits, and so formed an
artificial humerus. The boy now uses the arm for
many purposes, such as dressing himself, taking
his food, and taking part in games. Dr. John S.
Miller, of New York, records a similar case-in
which he cut out most of a lady’s lower jaw,
and repaired her by filling the space between the
disconnected ends with mashed bone.”
